Its linguistic signature is the shift of attribution from the tool to the conditions of deployment. *The schools that implemented well saw strong effects; the average effect is smaller because some schools implemented poorly. Inconsistent fidelity across districts is what produced mixed results. Classroom culture varies; teachers need more professional development to get the full benefit.* The move reframes underperformance as an environmental problem rather than a product problem. The tool works; the schools don't.

This move has real empirical grounding, which is part of what makes it rhetorically durable. The Pane RAND 2014 evaluation of Cognitive Tutor found exactly the pattern the move invokes — no effect in year one, a statistically significant effect in year two attributable to school-level learning about how to deploy the system. Implementation does matter. Schools do get better at using tools with time. The move's legitimacy in some cases is what makes it deployable in others. When Sal Khan in April 2026 compared Khanmigo to a tutor sitting in the back of the room waiting to be asked for help — "Some will; most won't," in his phrasing — he was performing a version of implementation blame. The AI was ready; the classroom conditions did not bring students to it. This is not necessarily wrong. It may also not be the full explanation. The move's fingerprint is that it leaves the tool's claim intact while attributing any gap in evidence to what happened after the tool arrived.

The second move is **user blame**. The attribution shifts further, from the deployment conditions to the people using the tool. *Students aren't ready for the Socratic method. Teachers lack the digital fluency the tool requires. Users don't know how to prompt well. Self-regulated learners benefit; those who lack self-regulation don't.* The linguistic signature is the diagnostic framing of users — the description of what users can or cannot do — positioned as explanation for why the tool did not produce what was claimed.

DiCerbo's statement that "students aren't great at asking questions well" is a specific example. The observation is empirically accurate — students, in Khan Academy's dialogue data, often did not engage substantively with Khanmigo's prompts, and the "IDK IDK" pattern is the specific residue of that non-engagement. The question is what the observation is doing rhetorically. Used as diagnosis — *this is what we observe, this is what the apparatus needs to address* — the observation is analytically productive. Used as deflection — *the revolution didn't happen because students aren't prepared for it* — the observation protects the original claim by relocating its failure in the users rather than in the tool or the design. The difference between diagnosis and deflection is subtle in linguistic form. It is often detectable in what follows the observation: does the speaker then take responsibility for designing for the observed user behavior, or does the speaker attribute the tool's shortfall to the users and let the original claim stand?

The MOOC era provides a clean historical example of user blame at scale. Early MOOC claims — Sebastian Thrun predicting in 2012 that only ten universities would remain in fifty years — did not survive first-year completion data showing retention rates in the single digits. The rhetorical response across the MOOC research literature between 2013 and 2015 was that MOOCs worked for students with appropriate *self-regulation* and that low completion rates reflected insufficient self-regulatory capacity rather than the unsuitability of the massive one-to-many format. The move did real rhetorical work; it preserved the MOOC value proposition for a time by narrowing who the proposition was about. It also eventually failed to hold, as the self-regulation framing itself became a signal that the original universal-access-to-elite-instruction promise had been quietly abandoned.

The third move is **UX friction attribution**. The tool's underperformance is attributed to interface problems, onboarding experience, or product-design issues that the next iteration will address. *The next version has a better conversation flow. The onboarding has been redesigned based on user feedback. Help-seeking prompts are being refined. New engagement features are in development.* The linguistic signature is the invocation of a future product version whose improvements will close the gap between the original claim and current evidence.

Khan Academy's public communications across 2024 and 2025 contain specific examples. Product updates referenced "Help Me Focus" tools, "Lesson Hooks," refined conversational flows, and improved teacher-facing dashboards as mechanisms that would address observed engagement shortfalls. These product updates were not fraudulent; the organization was genuinely iterating on the tool. What makes the move rhetorical is that the *original claim* — the revolutionary transformation of education — was positioned as achievable once the UX friction was resolved. If the framing holds, no amount of evidence can falsify the original claim: any shortfall can always be attributed to friction the next version will address. The claim is insulated from evidence by an indefinite series of forthcoming improvements.

This move was particularly visible in the adaptive-learning era. Knewton's public communications across 2014 through 2018 routinely invoked the next version of its adaptive engine — the one that would finally deliver the psychometric-profile-of-every-student promise that earlier versions had not delivered. Jose Ferreira, Knewton's founder, had described the system as a "robot tutor in the sky" that could "semi-read your mind" to produce a custom learning experience for each student. Each iteration was framed as approaching this vision; none actually produced it. When the forthcoming version finally arrived without producing the promised effects, the move simply moved to the next forthcoming version, until Knewton's 2019 sale to Wiley for substantially less than its total venture funding ended the arc without ever arriving at the final version. The sale itself was rhetorically framed as a "strategic acquisition" rather than as a recognition that the original claim had not held.

The fourth move is **extending timelines**. The claim is not retracted; its time horizon is extended. *Year one was about adoption; year two will show impact. We are playing the long game. The full effects will emerge over multiple years of institutional learning. Students who start now will see compound benefits through middle school.* The linguistic signature is the reframing of the original claim from "what we're doing now" to "what we're working toward."

This move has legitimate versions and rhetorical versions, and the distinction matters. The Pane RAND 2014 Cognitive Tutor study, as already mentioned, documented a real second-year effect that was not visible in year one. This is an empirically supported version of timeline extension — the claim "effects may emerge in year two" can be true, based on specific evidence about how schools and teachers adapt to new instructional tools. In this legitimate form, the timeline extension is accompanied by a specific mechanism: what is the tool doing between year one and year two that produces the delayed effect? What are the schools learning? What is the evidence that this learning is occurring?

The rhetorical version strips the mechanism. It simply asserts that effects will emerge later, without specifying what needs to change to produce them or what evidence would falsify the prediction. Khan Academy's April 2026 pivot toward "investing in human systems alongside the tool" has legitimate features — human-system integration is a real variable, and classrooms where teachers actively scaffold AI-tool use do produce different outcomes than classrooms where students encounter AI tools alone. The move becomes rhetorical if the timeline extension is presented without the specific theory of how human-system investment produces the transformation that Khanmigo's three-year arc did not produce. Without such a theory, "investing in human systems" becomes an indefinite deferral — a next horizon that can always be pushed further when the current one fails to produce what was claimed.

The fifth move is **narrowing the claim post-hoc**. The original claim is revised to match current evidence, with the revision framed as if it had been the claim all along. *Khanmigo was always intended as a teaching assistant, not a replacement for teachers. The tool's primary value has always been in supporting teachers with administrative tasks. We never claimed every student would become a Rhodes scholar; we claimed to expand access to personalized support.* The linguistic signature is the recasting of what was said in the past to align with what present evidence supports.

The Khanmigo arc contains clean examples. The 2023 framing was about transformation of education on a civilizational scale — "an amazing personal tutor for every student on the planet," "the biggest positive transformation that education has ever seen." By 2025 and into 2026, the public framing had shifted substantially toward Khanmigo as a "teaching assistant" — a "sidekick for teachers" that helped with lesson planning, rubric generation, and administrative support, and that provided supplementary help to students who actively sought it. *Brave New Words*, published in May 2024, already contained the narrower framing, describing Khanmigo as a tool that would "unstick" students during independent practice — a meaningfully different claim from the 2-sigma tutoring revolution of April 2023. The narrowing can be framed honestly — *we have learned that the teaching-assistant use case is where the tool produces its most reliable value* — or it can be framed as if the narrower claim was always the claim, which relocates the original transformation framing as either a misunderstanding by the audience or an aspirational frame that was never meant literally.

The ITS tradition, which Essai 7 of this volume examined, provides the paradigmatic historical case of post-hoc claim narrowing in EdTech. ITS was launched with the expectation that it would approach Bloom's 2-sigma human-tutoring benchmark. When VanLehn's 2011 meta-analysis showed ITS producing effects in the 0.76 sigma range — less than half of Bloom's target — the field's rhetoric narrowed accordingly. ITS, it was now said, had always been intended to support homework and seatwork rather than to replace classroom instruction. It worked within its "limited area of expertise." The narrowing was gradual, deniable at each individual step, and ultimately decisive. The claim that ITS would approach human tutoring was retired, and the claim that ITS would supplement classroom instruction took its place, without any moment of explicit retraction.

The sixth move is **shifting the standard of comparison**. When the tool does not outperform the gold-standard baseline, the baseline is changed. *Khanmigo performs comparably to Google Search. Our tool produces effects similar to other widely-used EdTech interventions. Compared to no intervention at all, the tool produces meaningful gains.* The linguistic signature is the introduction of a new comparator that the tool can outperform, replacing the original comparator against which it could not.

Recent research on Khanmigo has begun invoking Google Search as a comparator — students using Khanmigo perform comparably to students using Google Search for homework help, with students reporting they "perceived it positively." This is a meaningful finding; Google Search is a widely-available tool, and a dedicated tutor producing comparable outcomes has a specific value proposition. It is also not the comparator the 2023 framing invoked. The 2023 claim was that Khanmigo would approach expert human tutoring — the 2-sigma benchmark Bloom established and that Khan himself invoked in the TED framing. The 2026 comparator is a free search engine. The two are not on the same axis. Moving between them is a rhetorical move, and the audience for the move often does not notice that the comparator has shifted.

This move also recurs in the ITS history. When ITS failed to reach Bloom's 2-sigma level of expert human tutoring, the field did not retract the comparison; it reframed it. Two moves were performed in parallel. First, the ITS effects were repositioned as "comparable to human tutoring" on the grounds that most human tutors were not actually expert tutors performing at 2 sigma. Second, the comparison itself was repositioned — ITS was now compared against no-tutoring control conditions rather than against expert human tutoring, and the smaller sigma against the weaker comparison was presented as the relevant finding. Both moves shifted the standard of comparison downward. Both preserved the rhetorical viability of ITS claims without requiring retraction of what had originally been claimed.

---

There is a seventh move, which the essai has been enacting by describing the first six, and which is the field's most honest response pattern, though it is the rarest. It is the move Khan Academy made in April 2026. The move is to describe the gap between claim and evidence in plain language, without performing the six deflections, and to credit that gap as real rather than as a problem to be rhetorically managed.

DiCerbo's statement — "So far I am not seeing the revolution in education" — is an example of this move. So is Sal Khan's description of Khanmigo as a "non-event" for most students. So is the *Chalkbeat* interview's title: *Why Sal Khan's AI revolution hasn't happened yet*. The statements are striking in the context of the six rhetorical moves because they do not perform any of them. They do not blame implementation, users, or UX. They do not extend the timeline or narrow the claim to match current evidence. They do not shift the comparator. They state what the evidence shows, in language the audience can understand, and let the statement stand.

The comparison case in EdTech history that most closely parallels this move is Sebastian Thrun's 2014 statement about Udacity. After MOOC completion rates proved what they proved, Thrun said publicly that Udacity had "a lousy product, one that did not provide a good service to [its] students." This is a specific kind of acknowledgment — the founder of a heavily-funded, much-publicized educational-technology company saying out loud that his own product had not worked. The 2014 Thrun moment was followed, within months, by a narrowing of Udacity's scope — pivot to corporate vocational training, abandonment of the degree-replacement framing — and the organization's subsequent trajectory did not hold to the honesty of the original acknowledgment. But the moment itself was real, and it had the same structure as DiCerbo's and Khan's moment in April 2026: the person most responsible for the claim describes the claim's failure to hold in the language of the failure rather than in the language of the rhetorical deflection.

This is the move the essai wants to credit specifically. Most organizations do not make it when their tools underperform. The institutional incentives run strongly the other direction: philanthropic funders, district partners, media coverage, Microsoft's cloud-computing credits, and organizational reputation all reward the performance of the six moves and penalize the honest description that Khan Academy provided. DiCerbo and Khan chose, publicly, to describe what they had seen, knowing the implications for Khan Academy's position in the AI-tutor conversation. This is a disciplinary move in the sense the whole volume is concerned with. It is the move the book's form is trying to enact — specific findings held openly, without the rhetorical machinery that protects claims from evidence.

The credit does not need to be unqualified. Khan Academy continues to operate Khanmigo, and the organization's April 2026 pivot toward "human systems integration" has legitimate features but could become an indefinite-deferral move if not accompanied by specific theory and evidence. What happens after April 2026 — whether Khan Academy continues to describe the evidence honestly as it accumulates, whether the organization commits to the kinds of measurement practices Essai 10 specified as necessary, whether Khanmigo continues or retires — will determine how the full arc ultimately reads. But the April 2026 moment was itself significant. An organization with every reason to deploy the six rhetorical moves chose, for a single interview, to describe what was happening without them. This is worth naming.

---

I want to name the other substantial act of honesty from April 2026, which came from inside the EdTech industry rather than from inside Khan Academy. Dan Meyer, the mathematics-education researcher who now serves as Chief Academic Officer at Amplify, published a piece in April 2026 titled *RIP Khanmigo & Edtech Industry Dreams of AI Tutors*. Meyer's piece is significant because it offers an industry-level diagnosis of the Khanmigo arc from a position that is inside the EdTech industry rather than outside it — Meyer is a product leader at a competitor, not an academic critic writing from a neutral stance.

Meyer's core observation is what he calls the "silver spoon" argument. Khanmigo, he notes, had every advantage the EdTech industry could offer: early OpenAI access before competitors could reach GPT-4 at scale, Microsoft backing on cloud computing, Sal Khan's existing brand recognition and public platform, the goodwill of decades of Khan Academy's genuinely good work on free educational content. If a product with every conceivable advantage could only achieve "non-event" status, Meyer argues, the problem lies with the underlying theory — the theory that an AI chatbot can serve as the personal tutor Bloom's 2-sigma evidence pointed toward — rather than with any specific organization's execution.

Meyer's second observation concerns what he calls the *Ouroboros of Tech* — the pattern of using technology solutions to address problems that were created by earlier technology solutions. Khanmigo was positioned as the answer to the problems of self-paced digital learning, problems that earlier waves of educational technology had themselves introduced. The pattern, Meyer argues, prevents the field from engaging with the underlying socioeconomic factors that actually shape learning outcomes — teacher support, student motivation, institutional coherence, neighborhood stability, family resources. A technology that addresses a problem another technology created is often not addressing the actual problem.

Meyer's third observation is specific and worth noting: he describes the "increasingly aggressive way Khanmigo inserted itself into the student experience" across the 2024-2026 deployment period, pushing prompts and nudges and reminders on students who had not initiated engagement. The aggressive insertion, Meyer argues, was itself a rhetorical attempt to force engagement where there was no student purpose — to produce the metric of use even when the underlying behavior that would generate learning was absent. The move is what happens when a tool has to appear to be working because its founding claim requires that it work, even when the actual interactional evidence suggests that it is not.

I quote Meyer because his piece is a specific kind of April 2026 document — not the institutional acknowledgment of Khan Academy itself, but the industry-level diagnosis of what the Khan Academy acknowledgment means for the broader EdTech industry. Together, the two April 2026 pieces — the *Chalkbeat* interview and Meyer's *RIP Khanmigo* — form a diagnostic moment in the field. The question is what happens in the subsequent months and years: whether the diagnostic moment becomes a genuine turning point in how the EdTech industry handles efficacy claims, or whether it is absorbed into the next hype cycle without substantive change.

---

I want to dwell on one further distinction before this essai closes, because it is crucial to how the essai should be read.

Each of the six moves has a legitimate version and a rhetorical version. Implementation does matter; some schools genuinely implement better than others, and effects depend on it. Users do vary; students with different levels of self-regulation engage with tools differently, and that variation is real. UX does affect outcomes; good interfaces produce different results than bad ones. Timelines do sometimes extend; some educational interventions produce delayed effects. Claims do sometimes refine appropriately; understanding a tool's specific value is what careful research does. Comparison standards do sometimes change appropriately; the right comparator for a given deployment may become clearer as the deployment evolves.

The distinction between legitimate and rhetorical uses of each move is what the essai is trying to teach the reader to draw. The distinction is not always clean in any single instance, but it has markers that become recognizable with practice.

The legitimate version of each move is accompanied by three features: a specific mechanism connecting the move to the outcome it explains; a specification of what evidence would falsify the move's claim; and an engagement with the implications of the move for the original claim, including potential retraction of the original claim if the move does not hold up. The rhetorical version typically lacks one or more of these features. It invokes the move without specifying how it works, what would disprove it, or whether the original claim would have to change if the move turns out to be wrong.

The Pane RAND 2014 Cognitive Tutor finding is a legitimate implementation-timeline move because Pane's team specified the mechanism (school-level institutional learning about deployment), specified what evidence would falsify the prediction (no effect in year two would have falsified the first-year-learning-curve claim), and engaged with the implications (if year two had also shown no effect, the first-year null finding would have constituted the complete finding, not a partial one). DiCerbo's "IDK IDK" observation is, similarly, a legitimate user-engagement observation because it described what was specifically observed, invited a diagnostic response rather than a defensive one, and was accompanied by DiCerbo's acknowledgment that the original claim had not held.

The rhetorical versions of these moves often share a diagnostic linguistic signature: they tend to be offered without the three features above. Implementation blame without mechanism — *what specifically* about the poor implementations produced the failure? Timeline extension without falsifier — *what evidence* in year three would finally show the move was wrong? Claim narrowing without engagement — *how does the narrower claim relate* to what was said at the beginning? When a move is offered without these features, it is probably performing rhetorical work rather than analytical work.

---

Before the closing, a brief observation about why these rhetorical moves are used so consistently across the EdTech industry. The answer is not primarily about individual character or organizational virtue. It is about institutional incentives.

EdTech organizations — whether non-profits like Khan Academy or venture-backed companies like Knewton — depend on sustained narratives of transformative impact to maintain their funding environments. Philanthropic funders, investors, district partners, and media coverage all reward confident claims about impact and penalize acknowledgments of underperformance. Microsoft's cloud-computing credits, partnerships with OpenAI, district purchasing decisions, teacher adoption rates — all are shaped by the narrative the organization can sustain. An organization that publicly acknowledges that its revolutionary product is a non-event is an organization whose next funding cycle, next district partnership, next media appearance will be harder to secure. The incentives do not reward honesty; they reward the maintenance of the narrative.

What Cass Sunstein has called "availability entrepreneurs" — actors whose institutional position depends on their ability to sustain attention to a particular issue or claim — face specific pressure to continue promoting the claim even as evidence moves against it. For Khan Academy, Khanmigo is not merely a product; it is the organization's claim on the AI-in-education conversation. Retreating from that claim has consequences that extend beyond the specific evaluation of the specific tool. The rhetorical moves are not failures of character; they are responses to a structure that rewards their deployment. Which is why honest acknowledgments like April 2026's are rare, and why they matter when they do occur.

Larry Cuban documented this pattern across the full twentieth-century history of educational technology in *Oversold and Underused* (2001). Each wave of technology — radio, instructional television, computer-assisted instruction, educational computing, online learning — followed the same cycle: inflated initial claims, deployment gap between promise and practice, rhetorical management of the gap through implementation blame or user blame or claim narrowing, and eventual replacement by the next wave without substantive resolution of whether the previous wave had worked. The pattern Khanmigo's 2023-2026 arc exemplifies is not new. It is, as the historical precedents this essai has documented demonstrate, the EdTech field's ordinary response to disappointing evidence. What is unusual is the specific April 2026 moment when Khan Academy resisted the pattern and named the gap honestly.

---

Here is what I would like you to carry out of this essai.

When you next encounter an EdTech organization responding to underperformance of its claims — in an annual report, in a press interview, in an academic publication, in a product-update announcement — you have a diagnostic apparatus for the response. Watch for the six moves. Notice which ones appear. Notice whether the move is accompanied by the three features that distinguish its legitimate form from its rhetorical form: specific mechanism, specified falsifier, engagement with implications for the original claim.

If a response performs the six moves without the three features, the response is doing rhetorical work. The organization is protecting its claim from evidence. This is not necessarily dishonest; it is structurally rewarded by the institutional incentives of the EdTech industry. But it is also not evidence that the original claim is being honestly revised in light of what the research has shown. A reader who recognizes the move can calibrate their belief in the organization's current framing accordingly.

If a response instead performs the seventh move — the honest description of the gap between claim and evidence, without deployment of the six deflections — the response is doing something different, and something rarer. It is the kind of response Khan Academy offered in April 2026. It is the response Thrun offered in 2014 before Udacity's subsequent trajectory moved in other directions. It is the response the book is trying to model in its own form, across every essai. It is not a move that is guaranteed to produce good outcomes for the organization that makes it; it may well be punished by funders and partners who prefer the maintained narrative. But it is the response that makes the organization's subsequent claims more rather than less credible. An organization that can describe its current failures openly is an organization whose next claim can be evaluated against its track record of honesty rather than against a track record of protected claims.

The next essai — the last of Part III — turns to what the claims have cost to produce, and what cost-blind comparison of efficacy figures across studies has concealed. The question of what claims cost, and who pays what when they are produced, is the question the fourth part of this volume takes up as its first subject. I hope you will continue with me.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai is the volume's most delicate balance: characterizing a rhetorical pattern that the Khan Academy arc exemplifies, while also crediting Khan Academy specifically for an honesty that distinguishes their April 2026 response from the typical pattern. I want to name where I am uncertain whether this balance holds.

I am not sure I have given Khan Academy enough credit. The April 2026 acknowledgment was genuinely unusual in the field. DiCerbo and Khan, as senior organizational figures with substantial institutional interests at stake, chose to describe what they had seen in language that did not perform the six rhetorical moves. This is a significant disciplinary act, and the essai names it as such. But the essai also uses the Khanmigo arc as the anchor for a broader critique of the rhetorical pattern, which means the arc is both the book's best example of the honest response and the book's vehicle for examining the dishonest ones. A reader sympathetic to Khan Academy might say the essai's dual use of the arc undercuts the credit, even when the credit is explicitly given. If this concern lands, tell me where.

I am not sure about the boundary between legitimate and rhetorical versions of each move. The three features I specified — mechanism, falsifier, engagement with implications — are a plausible set of markers, but they are not an algorithm. In any specific case, the reader has to judge whether the mechanism is genuinely explanatory or whether it is post-hoc; whether the falsifier is genuinely specified or whether it is an indefinite future; whether the engagement with implications is substantive or whether it is defensive. The essai provides the framework; the reader does the application. A reader who finds the framework insufficient — who thinks the legitimate-rhetorical distinction requires more than three markers, or different markers — has a legitimate methodological concern.

I am not sure about the historical precedents. The essai treats Knewton, MOOCs, ITS, and Cuban's documentation of earlier waves as comparable cases with the six moves appearing across them. This is compressed historical analysis; a longer treatment of any one of these cases would produce a fuller picture. The MOOC disillusionment period in particular is rich with specific examples of user blame and claim narrowing that the essai treats briefly. A reader who works specifically on one of these historical moments may find the treatment too thin. The alternative is to extend the essai substantially, which the volume's length constraints do not accommodate.

I am not sure about the Dan Meyer passage. Meyer's piece is specific and pointed, and the essai quotes his core observations directly. But Meyer is, as the essai notes, a product leader at a competitor to Khan Academy. A reader might reasonably ask whether his critique carries the same weight as a critique from a neutral academic observer, or whether the essai's use of Meyer risks importing his commercial positioning into the book's register. I have kept the Meyer passage because his industry-internal perspective offers something a neutral observer could not — a specific characterization of what the Khanmigo arc looks like from inside the EdTech industry itself. But the judgment is contestable.

Finally, I am not sure the essai avoids sounding cynical about the field. The risk is that a careful description of six rhetorical moves, across many organizations and many decades, lands as a claim that the entire EdTech field is operating in bad faith. This is not the essai's argument. The argument is that the field has developed a specific set of rhetorical responses to disappointing evidence, that these responses have both legitimate and rhetorical versions, that the rhetorical versions are often deployed even by well-meaning people because institutional incentives favor them, and that honest responses like Khan Academy's April 2026 acknowledgment are significant precisely because they resist the pressure to deploy the moves. A reader who finds the essai drifting toward cynicism — toward a blanket "EdTech claims are all false" framing — should tell me. The essai is trying to describe a pattern, not to condemn a field.

The next essai examines what the claims have cost to produce, and what cost-blind comparison conceals. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 11. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 26–28 pages. Word count: approximately 6,400.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**Block structure.** The essai uses a specific variant of the volume's five-block form: Block A opens on the 2023-2026 arc framing with specific figures (221,200 users, 2.0M users, YPL metric, 18+ hours dosage), Block B introduces the six moves with Khanmigo-specific examples embedded in each, Block C is the honest-response seventh-move section that credits Khan Academy (and adds the Thrun 2014 parallel from the Deep Research document), Block D is the Dan Meyer section added based on the research document's documentation of Meyer's specific arguments, Block E is the legitimate-versus-rhetorical distinction plus the institutional-incentives observation (drawing on Cass Sunstein's "availability entrepreneurs" framing and Larry Cuban's *Oversold and Underused*), and the closing is the diagnostic apparatus the reader carries forward. Block B is longer than typical because it does the work of introducing six moves with Khanmigo-specific examples for each; if the revision pass wants to tighten, this block is the natural place.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 6,400 words, projecting to 26-28 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — above the 22-25 range the spec suggests. The essai is dense with specific moves, specific Khanmigo figures and quotations, and specific historical precedents. Further compression risks losing the texture the essai's argument requires.

**The Deep Research document's specific contributions.** This draft incorporates material the prior draft (written from the prompt alone) could not have used:
- The specific 2023-2024 annual report figure of 221,200 users
- The specific 2024-2025 annual report figure of 2.0 million global users
- The Yearly Proficient Learner (YPL) metric of 60+ skills per year
- The 18+ hours dosage recommendation
- The Michigan Virtual pilot's "curious but complicated" characterization
- Jose Ferreira's specific "robot tutor in the sky" and "semi-read your mind" language
- Knewton's 2019 sale to Wiley for less than total venture funding
- Sebastian Thrun's 2014 "lousy product" statement as a specific parallel to DiCerbo's April 2026 acknowledgment
- Dan Meyer's "Silver Spoon" argument and "Ouroboros of Tech" framing
- The specific "perceived it positively" framing from Khanmigo-vs-Google-Search research
- Cass Sunstein's "availability entrepreneurs" framing
- Larry Cuban's *Oversold and Underused* (2001) as the documentary source for the twentieth-century pattern

**The Khan Academy honesty credit.** The essai credits DiCerbo's and Khan's April 2026 acknowledgment specifically as a disciplinary move, both in Block C and in the closing. The Thrun parallel is used carefully — credit for the 2014 moment without claiming that Udacity's subsequent trajectory vindicated the honesty. The credit to Khan Academy is similarly calibrated — credit for the specific April 2026 moment without claiming that Khan Academy's subsequent trajectory is guaranteed to hold.

**The six moves plus the seventh.** The essai's structural innovation is the seventh move — the honest response — as the move that the field rarely performs and that Khan Academy's April 2026 acknowledgment exemplifies. This structure came from the Deep Research document's framing and I have extended it as the essai's closing frame.

**The Dan Meyer passage.** New to this draft based on the research document. Meyer's industry-internal perspective offers something a neutral observer could not — specific characterizations of what the Khanmigo arc looks like from inside the EdTech industry. The passage uses Meyer's "Silver Spoon" argument, his "Ouroboros of Tech" framing, and his observation about the "aggressive way Khanmigo inserted itself into the student experience." I have been careful to note that Meyer is a product leader at Amplify (a Khan Academy competitor), so readers can calibrate accordingly.

**What the draft may have gotten wrong.**
- The essai's risk of sounding cynical is real. The combination of six rhetorical moves across multiple decades, plus Cuban's documentation of the twentieth-century pattern, plus Meyer's industry-internal critique, may still read as "the field is in bad faith" rather than as "the field has developed predictable responses to evidence-pressure." The closing uncertainty section flags this explicitly.
- The Meyer passage imports a specific tonal register that is more polemical than the book's typical voice. I have tried to quote Meyer's observations factually rather than adopting his tone, but a revision pass should audit whether this holds.
- The closing "what to carry forward" could slide toward prescription. The three features (mechanism, falsifier, engagement) are analytical apparatus, not prescription, but the line is delicate.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On Cost in the Denominator

*Essai 12*

---

I want to begin this essai with a retrospective.

Nine essais ago, the fourth essai of this volume examined Benjamin Bloom's 1984 paper and the 2-sigma finding that has structured educational-technology claims for four decades. The examination focused on what Bloom's two dissertations (Anania 1981 and Burke 1983) actually measured, under what conditions, with what duration, against what baseline. The examination did not center cost. I want to center it now.

Bloom's 1984 paper is usually remembered for the two-sigma claim — the finding that students receiving one-to-one tutoring combined with mastery learning scored approximately two standard deviations higher than students in conventional classroom instruction. The paper is sometimes remembered for Bloom's framing of this as "the 2-sigma problem" — the problem being that tutoring was effective but not scalable at reasonable cost. What is less often remembered is the specific phrase Bloom used to describe the cost barrier. He wrote that one-to-one tutoring was *"too costly for most societies to bear on a large scale"* — and he wrote this as the central motivation for the paper. The 2-sigma problem was not "how do we get tutoring's effect with different instructional design." The 2-sigma problem was "how do we get tutoring's effect at a cost that a society can bear."

The field has spent forty years citing Bloom's effect size while stripping the cost constraint out of the citation.

This essai is about what that stripping conceals, and about what happens when we put the cost denominator back into the comparison.

The specific argument is this. Cost is not a business concern about whether schools can afford an intervention. It is an *epistemic variable* — a constitutive feature of what any effect size is a finding about. An effect size produced at $5 per student per year and an effect size produced at $4,000 per student per year are not findings on the same axis. Comparing them without the cost denominator is a rhetorical move rather than an analytical one. What looks, in the bare sigma comparison, like one intervention being more effective than another may actually be two interventions deploying different quantities of resource, and the "effectiveness" comparison may be measuring resource intensity rather than instructional power.

I am going to walk through what this means for the anchor cases the book has examined in prior essais. Bloom at the 1984 tutoring conditions, priced in contemporary dollars. Kestin at the Harvard AI-tutor study, priced with the caveats the data permits. The Khan Academy arc at its marginal-cost pricing. Rori at the five-dollar deployment in Ghana. Cognitive Tutor at the RAND-documented $97 per student. i-Ready and DreamBox and ALEKS at their list prices. Saga Education tutoring at three-and-a-half-to-four-and-three-quarters thousand dollars per student per year. Each of these cases has been invoked in contemporary EdTech discourse; each has a cost structure; and the cross-study sigma comparisons that have structured the field's claims have typically stripped the cost structures out.

Before I walk through them, I want to be specific about what this essai is and is not arguing.

It is not arguing that expensive interventions are better. Saga tutoring's 0.25 sigma at $4,000 per student is not automatically superior to Khan Academy's 0.04 sigma at $10 per student just because the former is more expensive. The argument is the opposite: once you look at the cost denominator, the Khan Academy effect per dollar is substantially larger, even though its raw sigma is smaller.

It is also not arguing that cheap interventions are better. Rori's 0.36 sigma at $5 per student is not automatically superior to Saga's 0.25 sigma at $4,000 just because it is cheaper. The two interventions are serving different populations in different contexts with different baselines, and the cost denominator does not resolve those differences into a single ranking.

What the essai is arguing is that the interventions are not commensurable without the cost denominator. An effect size without cost is a number that does not support the comparative claims the field has been making with it. The comparison *is not happening on a single axis* until cost is in the frame. Adding cost does not produce a single ranking either; it produces a productivity frontier on which different interventions occupy different positions, each appropriate to different contexts and resource environments. The frontier is an analytical space. The single axis is a rhetorical one.

---

Let me start with Bloom, because the anchor findings of the book mostly invoke his paper.

Bloom's 2-sigma finding came from two doctoral dissertations — Anania's 1981 and Burke's 1983 — that the volume's fourth essai examined at length. The specific conditions were three-week instructional windows with undergraduate tutors trained for one week, using a 1-to-1 or 1-to-3 tutor-to-student ratio, with a mastery learning feedback-corrective infrastructure that allowed students to spend 20 to 33 percent more instructional time than the control group. These conditions produced the two-sigma effect.

What did these conditions cost?

In the specific dissertations, the cost was absorbed by the universities and the study infrastructure. The undergraduate tutors were graduate-program resources. The mastery learning materials were built by the research team. The three-week windows were short enough that the per-student cost of the experimental setup itself was modest. Bloom's paper does not attempt a formal cost accounting of the dissertation interventions, because the research was conducted at research-grade resource intensity without the translation-to-scale question in view.

What Bloom did attempt, and what makes his 1984 paper more honest than much of what followed, was to estimate what it would cost to deploy this intervention at scale. One-to-one tutoring with trained tutors, using mastery learning feedback across a full school year, at the intensity the dissertations documented, would require a substantial labor investment. In 1984 dollars, Bloom's implicit estimate suggested that this was prohibitively expensive for public education systems. Translating to contemporary dollars — using standard CPI adjustments that put 1984's dollar at approximately 2.7 to 3 times current value — places the cost at somewhere between $2,000 and $4,000 per student per year for the high-dosage tutoring conditions Bloom was pointing at.

We have a contemporary benchmark for this estimate. High-dosage tutoring programs that attempt to replicate Bloom's conditions in current public education settings — Saga Education, MATCH Corps, Blueprint Education, and comparable programs documented in the Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan 2020 meta-analysis of tutoring RCTs — report per-student-per-year costs in the range of $3,500 to $4,800. This is the price, in 2026 dollars, of producing the conditions Bloom's research was a finding about. Saga's reported effects, when evaluated in the contemporary randomized controlled trial literature, are in the range of 0.25 to 0.4 sigma — not the 2.0 sigma Bloom found, because the operational conditions in contemporary public schools differ from Bloom's three-week high-fidelity laboratory conditions. But the cost comparison is the comparison the field keeps stripping out. Bloom's 2-sigma effect, in its operational form, runs at roughly $4,000 per student per year.

This places Bloom's 2-sigma finding in a specific location on the cost-effectiveness plane. An effect size of 2.0 sigma at $4,000 per student produces a sigma-per-dollar of 0.0005. This is the fraction of a standard deviation you get for each dollar spent on the intervention. Keep this number in your mind as the rest of the essai walks through the other cases.

---

Take Kestin's 2024 Harvard physics AI-tutor study next.

The Kestin study's reported effect size, as the tenth essai examined, was in the 0.73 to 1.3 sigma range. The study does not report per-student deployment costs in primary form. What it does report is the composition of the intervention: a customized ChatGPT instance built on GPT-4, with system-prompt engineering performed by physics education researchers over some period of time, deployed to 194 Harvard undergraduates enrolled in Physical Sciences 2 for a research-session approximately 49 minutes in duration.

Without primary-source cost reporting, the essai can only reason about order-of-magnitude estimates. But the components of the cost are identifiable, and each can be approximated.

The compute cost — GPT-4 API calls at Socratic-dialogue token volumes — is not negligible but is also not enormous. For a 49-minute session of active dialogue, per-student compute costs were likely in the range of a few dollars to perhaps ten dollars, depending on message density. Scale this across 194 students and the research-phase compute bill is in the low thousands.

The labor cost — physics education researchers engineering system prompts, iterating on the tutor's design, monitoring its behavior, analyzing outputs — is substantially higher. Academic researcher time is expensive when properly accounted for, and the system-prompt engineering Kestin and colleagues describe was not a weekend's work. A conservative estimate places this cost in the tens of thousands of dollars for the research phase of the study.

The institutional baseline cost — the Harvard physics course infrastructure the AI tutor was deployed alongside, including lecturers, teaching assistants, course materials, and facilities — is already expensive, and the AI tutor was an addition to rather than a replacement for this baseline. Whatever the tutor's effect size, it was produced inside a course that Harvard was already running at research-university cost structures.

Projecting the Kestin deployment to scale is where the cost question becomes sharp. If the intervention required Harvard-grade system-prompt engineering, researcher oversight, and institutional baseline to produce the 0.73-to-1.3 sigma effect, deploying it at population scale would require either replicating these conditions (expensive) or accepting that the effect will not hold outside them (which changes what the effect size is a finding about). Khanmigo's experience — Khan Academy's own AI-tutor deployment at scale, with the marginal cost set at roughly $35 per student per year for district licenses — suggests that scaled AI-tutor deployment at affordable pricing produces substantially smaller effects than Kestin's research-grade deployment did. The DiCerbo "non-event" from the eleventh essai is, among other things, a finding about what scaled deployment at sustainable cost produces: something considerably smaller than 0.73 sigma.

We can place Kestin's research-grade deployment on the cost-effectiveness plane roughly. If we estimate the per-student all-in research cost at $100 to $200 (amortizing the research-team time across the 194 participants), the sigma-per-dollar is in the range of 0.004 to 0.013. This is substantially more efficient than Bloom's 0.0005, which is the specific honest thing the Kestin study suggests — at research-grade intensity in a high-resource context, AI-mediated instruction produces sigma per dollar that is meaningfully higher than high-dosage tutoring. What the Kestin study does not support is the claim that this efficiency persists when the research-grade conditions are removed. Khanmigo's operational deployment produces a sigma per dollar considerably smaller than Kestin's research-grade deployment, and this gap is the gap the apparatus needs to name rather than smooth over.

---

The Khan Academy arc, which the book has examined at several points, operates at a specific price tier that makes it the single most important cost anchor in current educational technology.

Khan Academy's district pricing for its core platform runs at approximately $10 per student per year — a marginal-cost figure that the organization has documented publicly in its pricing materials and in philanthropic-funding disclosures. This $10 does not include the amortized cost of Khan Academy's development work, which has been covered by over a hundred million dollars in philanthropic gifts across the organization's history. A for-profit organization trying to match Khan Academy's pricing would have to recover those development costs in its license fees, which would push the true cost-per-student considerably higher. The $10 is the marginal cost of delivering the platform to an additional district, not the fully-loaded cost of having built the platform in the first place.

With Khanmigo, Khan Academy's AI tutor, the marginal cost runs higher — roughly $35 per student per year for district pricing, or $44 annually for individual subscriptions. The 3.5-times increase over the base platform reflects the compute cost of generative AI dialogue and the incremental development and monitoring labor involved in maintaining an AI tutor at scale. Khan Academy's public framing has consistently emphasized that Khanmigo was priced to approximate its marginal cost rather than to generate profit, which is credible for a non-profit organization but leaves open the question of what a for-profit AI tutor at comparable quality would have to charge.

What effect sizes does the Khan Academy deployment produce? The second essai of this volume examined the 2022 Khan Academy evaluation by Thomas Grimaldi and colleagues, which reported effect sizes in the range of 0.04 to 0.09 sigma for the broad Khan Academy user population. The five-percent-problem framing from Laurence Holt, which the same essai invoked, surfaced that the aggregate effect was largely driven by the subset of students who used the platform at high dosage. For students who engaged with Khan Academy for the recommended 18 or more hours per year, effects were substantially larger — approaching the ranges reported for more resource-intensive interventions. For students who engaged below that dosage threshold, effects were much smaller. The average effect the evaluation reported was an average across this heterogeneous engagement distribution.

Placed on the cost-effectiveness plane, the Khan Academy deployment at roughly 0.06 sigma for the average user at $10 per student per year produces a sigma-per-dollar of 0.006 — meaningfully higher than Bloom's 0.0005. For high-dosage users, the sigma per dollar is higher still. This is not a claim that Khan Academy is "better" than high-dosage tutoring. Saga's 0.25 sigma is a real learning effect in a population that Khan Academy's intervention could not reach with the same magnitude; Saga and Khan Academy serve different kinds of work. What the cost-normalized comparison reveals is that the two are not on the same axis of "effectiveness." They occupy different positions on a productivity frontier, each appropriate to different contexts and resource environments. Treating them as competitors on a single sigma ranking — which the field's typical discussion does — is an analytical error that the cost denominator makes visible.

---

Rori, the WhatsApp-based AI math tutor deployed to Ghanaian secondary-school students, which the tenth essai examined, is the single most important anchor in the current AI-tutor literature precisely because its cost is rigorously reported.

Henkel and colleagues' 2024 study of Rori documented a marginal cost of approximately $5 per student per year. This figure includes the LLM API costs for the specialized pedagogical model Rori uses, the cost of data bandwidth for the 3G networks the tool is deployed over, and the operational costs of maintaining the WhatsApp-based delivery infrastructure. What it does not include is the amortized cost of Rori's development, which was funded by Rising Academies (Rori's developer) and philanthropic sources in ways analogous to Khan Academy's development history.

The effect size Rori produced was 0.36 sigma on curriculum-aligned math assessment, measured over an eight-month intervention, with substantial attrition that the study reports honestly. This is the highest effect-per-dollar finding in the contemporary AI-tutor literature by a large margin. At 0.36 sigma for $5 per student per year, the sigma-per-dollar is 0.072 — approximately 144 times more efficient than Bloom's two-sigma benchmark, approximately 12 times more efficient than the Khan Academy deployment at average-user engagement, approximately 5 to 18 times more efficient than Kestin's research-grade Harvard deployment depending on how the Kestin costs are estimated.

I want to be careful about what this comparison supports and what it does not.

The comparison does not support the claim that Rori is uniformly superior to other interventions. The Ghanaian baseline against which Rori's effect was measured is substantially weaker than the baselines against which Kestin or Saga or Khan Academy were evaluated — a fact the tenth essai examined at length. A $5 intervention that produces 0.36 sigma in a context where the counterfactual is under-resourced mathematical instruction is not the same finding as a $5 intervention producing 0.36 sigma in a well-resourced context. The denominator matters, but the baseline context matters too.

The comparison also does not support the claim that the Rori deployment can be replicated at $5 per student per year in other contexts. Different infrastructure, different labor costs, different data-bandwidth pricing, different content-development needs would all affect the marginal cost of a Rori-style deployment in, say, the United States. The $5 is a finding about Ghana specifically, not a portable price.

What the comparison *does* support is the observation that the current AI-tutor literature has been comparing findings across a range of sigma-per-dollar that spans roughly three orders of magnitude — from Bloom's 0.0005 at the low efficiency end to Rori's 0.072 at the high end — while treating all of them as findings on a single "effectiveness" axis. Rori is not the most effective intervention in the raw sigma sense; Bloom's 2-sigma tutoring is. But Rori is dramatically more effective per dollar. Once the denominator is visible, the comparison across these studies is a comparison between different points on a productivity frontier, not between points on a single ranking. And the productivity frontier is the analytical space the field needs to be working in, if the field wants to have honest conversations about where interventions should be deployed and under what conditions.

---

I want to walk more briefly through the other cases the book has examined, because the cost-denominator pattern is visible across all of them.

Cognitive Tutor, as documented in the Pane RAND 2014 report that the seventh essai examined, ran at $97 per student per year in implementation cost. The RAND report is unusually transparent about the cost composition: approximately $35 for materials and licensing, substantial additional costs for teacher professional development (three days of initial training plus ongoing coaching), technology infrastructure costs, and technical support. The comparison baseline in the study was traditional algebra textbook curriculum at $28 per student per year. Cognitive Tutor, therefore, was a 3.4-times resource-intensity increase over the baseline. The effect size Pane's team reported, the statistically significant second-year effect in high schools, was approximately 0.20 sigma. The sigma-per-dollar figure is 0.002 — more efficient than Bloom but substantially less efficient than Khan Academy's marginal-cost operation or Rori's low-cost deployment.

i-Ready and DreamBox, the adaptive-learning systems the eighth essai examined, run at approximately $22 to $35 per student per year per subject at list pricing, though districts typically negotiate lower prices under non-disclosure clauses. A single day of i-Ready professional learning support can cost $2,200. Large district deployments often spend over $100,000 on the "partner services" that make the software operate at claimed fidelity. The Harvard CEPR evaluations of DreamBox found that variation in effectiveness was driven substantially by teacher and school-level implementation practices — which means the true cost of a "working" adaptive-learning deployment includes the labor cost of the school-level management that makes it work.

ALEKS, which the seventh essai mentioned as a representative of the intelligent-tutoring-system tradition, runs at $179.95 per year for individual subscriptions, with institutional pricing that varies substantially across districts.

Duolingo, the ninth essai's anchor, operates at near-zero marginal cost per user because its revenue model is advertising and premium-tier subscription. The high-end Duolingo Max tier with GPT-4-powered features like video call tutoring runs around $30 per month to the user. The company's internal cost structure for delivering these features is proprietary, but the recent announcement that Video Call was being moved from Max to the broader Super Duolingo tier, sacrificing approximately $50 million in foregone bookings to drive user growth, reveals that the tradeoff between revenue and scale is actively managed in ways that standard cost-effectiveness accounting does not capture.

Saga Education high-dosage tutoring, the contemporary Bloom-lineage intervention I invoked earlier, runs at $3,500 to $4,800 per student per year. The effect size in Saga's randomized controlled trial literature is approximately 0.25 sigma. The sigma-per-dollar is around 0.00006 — the lowest of any intervention the book has examined, which makes Saga the least efficient intervention in the productivity-frontier sense, while also being the intervention that produces the most durable effects in the hardest-to-reach student populations. Both of these facts are simultaneously true, and the field needs to be able to hold them simultaneously rather than picking one to be "the finding."

---

I want to draw a pattern from what this walk-through reveals.

The current state of the AI-tutor literature discusses its findings as if they were points on a single axis of "effectiveness" that can be ranked against each other and against historical benchmarks. What the cost denominator reveals is that they are points on a productivity frontier — a two-dimensional space where the horizontal axis is cost-per-student and the vertical axis is sigma-produced, and where different interventions occupy different regions of the space.

Bloom's 2-sigma tutoring, at $4,000 per student, sits at high cost and high sigma. Saga's contemporary Bloom-lineage tutoring, at $3,500-$4,800 per student, sits at high cost and moderate sigma. Cognitive Tutor, at $97 per student, sits at medium-low cost and low-moderate sigma. i-Ready and DreamBox, at $22-$35 per student, sit at low cost and very low sigma, at least at the scales the evaluation literature has examined. Khan Academy, at $10 per student, sits at very low cost and very low sigma on average, with a high-dosage user subset at higher sigma. Rori, at $5 per student, sits at lowest cost and a sigma figure that is genuinely remarkable given the denominator. Kestin's Harvard research deployment sits at high per-student-cost (amortized) and high sigma, with an open question about whether the effect persists at scale.

These positions on the productivity frontier are different findings about different interventions in different contexts. The comparison across them is not a comparison of effectiveness in the way a bare sigma ranking implies. It is a comparison of where each intervention fits in a resource-effect space. A policymaker asking "what should we deploy in our district?" is asking a productivity-frontier question, because they have a budget and they have a student population and they need to know what produces the most sigma per dollar for their specific context. A sigma ranking without cost cannot answer the question.

This is where the existing methodological tradition becomes relevant, because economists of education have been building cost-effectiveness analysis tools for exactly this purpose for decades.

Henry Levin's ingredients method for cost-effectiveness analysis, developed across the 1970s and 1980s, specifies that a program's true cost is the value of all the resources required to implement it, regardless of who pays for them. A volunteer tutoring program is not actually free; its cost includes the opportunity cost of the volunteers' time and the administrative overhead to manage them. Clive Belfield and Levin's more recent work has elaborated this into a systematic methodology for ranking educational interventions by effect-per-dollar, using the ingredients method to establish true costs and then computing cost-effectiveness ratios.

Matthew Kraft's work on interpreting effect sizes, published in the mid-2020s, has argued that the field's reliance on Cohen's benchmarks for effect size interpretation — where 0.2 is "small," 0.5 is "medium," 0.8 is "large" — has led to the dismissal of interventions that are small-in-magnitude but large-in-policy-relevance. Kraft argues that effect size should be interpreted in light of three factors: the outcome measured, the study features, and the program cost. Under Kraft's schema, Khan Academy's 0.06 sigma at $10 per student may be more policy-important than a 0.5 sigma at $5,000 per student, because the former is deployable at scale across entire school systems while the latter can only reach a small subset of students. This is not a claim that the smaller effect is "better" in the psychological sense. It is a claim that policy decisions require cost-normalized comparison, and that raw sigma rankings cannot provide it.

The Abdul Latif Jameel Poverty Action Lab — J-PAL — founded by Abhijit Banerjee and Esther Duflo, whose work on cost-effective educational interventions in developing countries earned them the Nobel Prize in Economics in 2019, has been conducting cost-inclusive randomized controlled trials of educational interventions for nearly two decades. Their reporting convention uses "additional years of schooling gained per $100 spent" or "standard deviations gained per $100" as the reportable outcome. This is cost-inclusive efficacy reporting as standard practice. It has transformed the study of educational intervention in developing-country contexts. It has not transformed the study of educational technology in high-resource contexts, which is the observation the essai's closing will return to.

---

I want to name why cost has been systematically excluded from EdTech efficacy reporting, because the pattern is not an accident.

The exclusion has three specific features that the apparatus makes visible.

First, cost is typically reported in methods appendices rather than in findings sections, which means citations of the research typically strip the cost out. A paper that reports an effect size of 0.3 sigma and, in its methods appendix, notes that the intervention cost $50 per student per year, will be cited as reporting a 0.3 sigma effect. The $50 will not appear in the citation. The finding that circulates is the numerator, not the fraction.

Second, EdTech industry communications — investor presentations, philanthropic program documents, district-facing marketing — almost never report effect size normalized by cost. They report effect size and they report cost, but they do not compute or present sigma-per-dollar. The omission is rhetorical. An intervention at $50 per student producing 0.3 sigma looks more impressive when sigma is presented alone than when sigma-per-dollar is presented alongside. The communication formats optimize for the presentation of sigma, which means the cost information, when it appears, appears in ways that do not support direct comparison.

Third, the academic research conventions in educational psychology — the discipline that has dominated EdTech efficacy research — have historically not required cost reporting as a condition of publication. The Every Student Succeeds Act's evidence tiers include some consideration of implementation context, but cost-inclusive effect-size reporting is not a condition of meeting the tier standards. The What Works Clearinghouse has faced sustained criticism for its cost-blindness and has begun to explore cost-inclusive reporting, but the data remains sparse and relies heavily on publisher-reported pricing rather than independent audit.

These three features — appendix burial, rhetorical framing, discipline-level convention — together produce the pattern the book has been examining in various forms across the prior eleven essais. The cost-blind comparison is not a mistake by individual researchers. It is a structural feature of the measurement apparatus that the field has inherited and continues to operate under. Changing the pattern requires changing the reporting conventions, the publication standards, the citation conventions, and the discourse norms of the field simultaneously. This is a large ask, and it is the ask the essai's closing will articulate specifically.

---

Here is what I would like you to carry out of this essai.

When you next encounter an educational-technology efficacy claim — in a paper, a press release, an investor pitch, a philanthropic report — ask a specific sequence of questions.

The first: what is the effect size, and what conditions produced it?

The second: what did the intervention cost to produce that effect, measured honestly — not as list price, not as marginal cost to the school, but as the all-in cost of the resources the intervention deployed? Include the development costs amortized across the user base. Include the teacher training and support costs. Include the infrastructure the intervention assumes is already in place. Include the opportunity cost of the time the intervention takes from alternatives. The ingredients method, as Levin specified it, is the discipline for this accounting.

The third: what is the sigma-per-dollar? Compute the fraction explicitly. Do not accept the bare sigma as the reportable finding.

The fourth: where does this intervention sit on the productivity frontier relative to alternatives at matched cost? Would the same dollars spent on a different intervention produce more, less, or comparable sigma? This is the comparison that matters for policy and deployment decisions.

The fifth: what does the cost-normalized comparison reveal that the raw sigma comparison concealed? In my experience with the cases in this essai, the cost-normalized comparison almost always surfaces something the raw sigma did not: that an expensive intervention has purchased its effect rather than discovered it, or that a cheap intervention has achieved something that a fair comparison would credit more than the field typically does, or that two interventions the field treats as competitors are actually doing fundamentally different kinds of work at different points in the resource-effect space.

These questions will not resolve every comparison cleanly. The productivity frontier is a two-dimensional space, not a single ranking, and two interventions at different positions may both be defensible choices in different contexts. But the questions move the comparison from the sigma-ranking mode — where the field has been making claims it cannot defend — to the productivity-frontier mode, where the claims the field can actually defend are the ones worth making.

The thirteenth essai of this volume, which is the essai that follows this one, makes the deepest analytical claim the book has been building toward: that the measure-construct distinction, applied throughout the book's prior cases, is the single most important analytical tool for reading EdTech claims honestly. Cost, which I have treated in this essai as an epistemic variable, is one specific application of that deeper distinction. The sigma a measurement produces is one specific operationalization; the learning it purports to index is the construct. The cost a deployment consumes is the specific resource the measurement's operational conditions required; the educational productivity the deployment purports to embody is the construct. Reading carefully, at all the relevant layers, is what the thirteenth essai asks for explicitly. I hope you will continue with me.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai is the first of Part IV's "Deeper Concealments," and its relationship to the prior eleven essais is retrospective rather than novel. The essai re-examines cases the book has already examined, adding a dimension (cost) the earlier essais mentioned but did not center. I want to name where I am uncertain whether the retrospective move has worked.

I am not sure I have given each case its full complexity. Bloom's cost structure, Kestin's cost structure, Khan Academy's cost structure — each could be examined at greater length than the essai has allowed, and each contains uncertainties that the essai has compressed for the sake of the larger argument. The estimates I have offered for Kestin's all-in per-student cost are order-of-magnitude estimates drawn from what the study permits, not primary-source cost accounting. The Saga figure and the Rori figure are primary-source but the comparability between them is not fully specified. A reader who works specifically on one of these cases may find the treatment too compressed. The alternative is to extend the essai substantially, which would shift it away from the synthesis move it is trying to make.

I am not sure the sigma-per-dollar calculations are presented in a way that guards against misuse. The calculations are arithmetically straightforward, but their interpretation depends on the baselines and populations and timescales the underlying studies operate under — all of which the essai's prior chapters documented but this essai compresses. A reader who uses the sigma-per-dollar figures as rankings without the prior chapters' context for each case would misunderstand what the comparison supports. The essai is trying to model the right use of these figures, not the wrong one, but the distinction is fragile.

I am not sure the essai is fair to the field. The characterization of "cost-blindness as rhetorical move" places the field's conventions in a specific interpretive light — that the omission of cost is structurally produced by incentives that reward the omission. This framing is defensible but also critical, and a reader who believes the field's conventions are the natural result of different analytical priorities rather than structural bias may find the critical framing overstated. I have tried to note that the exclusion is structural rather than intentional, but the line between structural critique and moral critique can be delicate.

Finally, I am not sure the essai holds the Montaignian register well. The density of specific figures — dollar amounts, sigma values, sigma-per-dollar fractions — is higher than in most prior essais, and the essai's tone is more quantitative than the volume's characteristic voice. I have tried to keep the voice provisional throughout, acknowledging uncertainties, framing the analysis as what the apparatus reveals rather than as what must be concluded. But the essai approaches the form of a cost-effectiveness analysis more closely than any prior essai, and a reader for whom the Montaignian register is the reason to read the book may find this essai more technical than the register accommodates. If the balance between the analytical apparatus and the essai form slips in places, I would like to know where.

The next essai is the volume's synthesis moment — the deepest claim the book has been building toward about the measure-construct distinction, and the closest the volume comes to stating its central analytical tool plainly. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 12. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 26–28 pages. Word count: approximately 5,900.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**Block structure.** The essai follows a retrospective-synthesis pattern specific to Part IV's project: Block A opens on Bloom 1984's specific framing ("too costly for most societies to bear"), Block B defines cost as an epistemic variable and previews the case-by-case walk-through, Block C walks through the anchor cases individually (Bloom, Kestin, Khan Academy, Rori) with explicit sigma-per-dollar calculations, Block D briefly covers the remaining cases (Cognitive Tutor, i-Ready, ALEKS, Duolingo, Saga) to establish the pattern across the full field, Block E synthesizes into the productivity-frontier framework and the existing methodological tradition (Levin, Kraft, J-PAL/Banerjee-Duflo), and the closing specifies the sequence of questions the reader should carry forward. The structure has been extended beyond the typical five-block form to accommodate the retrospective walk-through.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 5,900 words, projecting to 26-28 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — above the 22-25 range the spec suggested. The retrospective walk-through of multiple cases required the extra length; without it, the productivity-frontier synthesis would not be grounded in specifics. A revision pass could compress the individual case treatments in Block C (particularly Cognitive Tutor, i-Ready, Duolingo in Block D) to bring the length down, but the Bloom, Kestin, Khan Academy, and Rori cases are load-bearing.

**The sigma-per-dollar calculations.** The essai computes specific sigma-per-dollar figures — Bloom at 0.0005, Cognitive Tutor at 0.002, Khan Academy at 0.006, Kestin at 0.004-0.013, Rori at 0.072, Saga at 0.00006. These are arithmetically derived from the effect sizes and costs the essai documents. They are useful for establishing the productivity-frontier framework but they depend on the underlying studies' cost and effect-size figures being roughly correct. The calculations are more reliable for cases where both figures are well-documented (Cognitive Tutor's RAND $97, Rori's Henkel $5, Saga's Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan range) and less reliable for cases where cost requires estimation (Kestin, Bloom retrospective). The essai's closing uncertainty section flags this.

**The Kestin cost question.** The Deep Research document was honest that Kestin's paper does not report primary-source deployment costs. I have used order-of-magnitude estimates (a few dollars to perhaps ten dollars in compute per student, tens of thousands in researcher time, institutional baseline cost constant) and combined them into a per-student all-in figure of $100-$200 amortized. This produces a sigma-per-dollar of 0.004-0.013. The estimate is defensible but not primary-source. A revision pass could either seek more specific cost documentation (if it exists) or revise the estimates to be more cautious.

**The productivity-frontier framework.** Levin's ingredients method, Kraft's effect-size-reframing work, and the J-PAL/Banerjee-Duflo Nobel-Prize-recognized cost-effectiveness tradition are each invoked briefly as established methodological precedents for cost-inclusive efficacy reporting. The essai does not extensively review these literatures — that would expand the essai considerably — but it establishes that the argument for cost-inclusive reporting is not novel and has substantial methodological backing outside the educational-psychology-dominant EdTech field. This gives the essai's closing recommendations a grounding rather than making them feel like personal prescriptions.

**The bridge to Essai 13.** The essai ends by framing cost as one specific application of the measure-construct distinction that Essai 13 will examine directly. This is the specific conceptual bridge between the two essais. The sigma a measurement produces is the operationalization; the learning it purports to index is the construct. The cost a deployment consumes is the specific resource the measurement's operational conditions required; the educational productivity the deployment purports to embody is the construct. The bridge places Essai 12 and Essai 13 in a specific relationship: Essai 12 is a specific application of the deeper principle that Essai 13 names explicitly.

**Specific claims I deliberately did not use.** The "ALEKS public pricing of $179.95" figure appeared in the Deep Research document and I used it cautiously, flagging that institutional pricing varies. The Duolingo "$50 million in foregone bookings" figure from the Video Call tier announcement I used because it is publicly sourced, but it is company-communicated and may be presented with rhetorical framing the essai does not fully examine. A revision pass could verify these more carefully.

**The Montaignian register question.** The density of specific figures in this essai is higher than in any prior essai in the volume. I have tried to keep the voice provisional and essai-ful throughout, but the quantitative work is substantial and the essai approaches the form of a cost-effectiveness analysis in several passages. This is flagged in the closing uncertainty section. A reader who finds the register tilted too far toward the technical may want passages softened. A reader who finds the quantitative work genuinely useful may want more of it. The balance is delicate and is the right thing to audit on revision.

**What the draft does particularly well.**
- Places Bloom 1984's cost framing ("too costly for most societies to bear") at the center of what his paper actually argued, rather than at the margin where it typically appears.
- Walks through the book's prior cases systematically with sigma-per-dollar calculations that make the productivity-frontier framework concrete.
- Grounds the closing recommendations in an established methodological tradition (Levin, Kraft, J-PAL) rather than presenting them as author prescriptions.
