- Bridges to Essai 13 specifically and structurally, setting up the measure-construct synthesis.
- Names the three structural features of cost-blindness (appendix burial, rhetorical framing, discipline-level convention) explicitly, which places the critique as structural rather than moral.

**What the draft may have gotten wrong.**
- The Kestin cost estimate is order-of-magnitude and could be contested by someone with better data.
- The Saga sigma figure (0.25) is drawn from a range the research literature reports; different readers may recall different specific numbers from the Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan meta-analysis.
- The productivity-frontier framework is presented as the analytical alternative to sigma-ranking, but the essai does not fully work out how to make productivity-frontier analysis operationally routine. This is the limit of what the essai can do; the full development belongs in a more methodological paper.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On What We Measure and What We Care About

*Essai 13 — Draft*

---

Consider two findings, forty years apart.

In 1984, Benjamin Bloom published a short paper in *Educational Researcher* reporting that students tutored one-on-one under mastery-learning conditions performed, on average, two standard deviations above students taught in conventional classrooms. The paper ran seventeen pages. It was based on two doctoral dissertations Bloom had supervised at the University of Chicago. The finding has been cited tens of thousands of times and has become, over four decades, perhaps the single most-invoked benchmark in educational technology. Whenever a new intervention — a new tutor, a new system, a new platform — claims to approach the effectiveness of human one-on-one instruction, it is Bloom's 2-sigma finding it is claiming to approach.

In 2024, a research team at Harvard led by Gregory Kestin reported that an AI tutor, deployed in an introductory physics course, produced learning gains substantially larger than conventional active-learning classroom instruction. The effect size exceeded what prior literature had typically reported for any tutoring intervention, including Bloom's. The study was methodologically careful: a randomized design, a rigorous active-learning baseline, pre- and post-testing with aligned items, a specific physics domain with established assessment instruments. The finding circulated quickly. Within weeks it was being cited as evidence that current-generation AI tutors meaningfully exceed what good conventional instruction can deliver.

Forty years apart. Different technologies. Different research traditions. Different specific methodologies. And yet, read carefully, the two findings share a structure.

In each, a specific measurement — performance on items aligned to the intervention's content, assessed at short timescale, against a conventional-instruction baseline — is offered as evidence for a construct about which the measurement is not, strictly, a measurement. Bloom's 2-sigma is evidence about students' performance on aligned items under particular tutoring conditions. It is *cited* as evidence about the effectiveness of tutoring as an instructional mode. Kestin's physics finding is evidence about short-timescale aligned-item performance in a selective undergraduate population. It is *cited* as evidence that AI tutoring outperforms human instruction in some general sense the measurement does not index.

The measurements are not false. The findings are not inflated. In each case, the researchers reported carefully what they measured, and what they measured is what they found. The question this essai takes up is what happens between the measurement and its citation — the small, structural, and repeated gap between what the apparatus indexes and what the vocabulary surrounding the apparatus claims.

Across the previous twelve essais, the book has walked this gap in specific instances. In Bloom's case. In the intelligent-tutoring-systems literature. In adaptive learning's personalization claims. In the engagement metrics that substitute when harder outcomes refuse to cooperate. In the current generation of AI-tutor efficacy reporting. In the blame-the-implementation move the field performs when tools underperform. In the systematic omission of cost from efficacy claims.

Each of those essais read a particular case. What the cases share is the subject of this one.

---

## The structure

It will help to name the structure directly.

An efficacy claim in this field typically consists of three things: a measurement, a construct, and an asserted relationship between them. The measurement is the thing the researchers actually did — items administered, scores computed, conditions compared. The construct is what the measurement is meant to be evidence for — *learning*, *mastery*, *effectiveness*, *personalization*, *engagement*, some other category the field's vocabulary invokes. The asserted relationship is the claim that the measurement indexes the construct, at least well enough to license the uses the researchers and subsequent citers put the finding to.

This is the structure of any empirical claim in any field. Biology works this way, and so does physics, and so does nutrition research. The gap between measurement and construct is not a problem specific to educational technology. It is a feature of empirical inquiry. Measurements never exhaustively capture their constructs; operationalizations are always provisional; the question for any field is how seriously it takes the gap, how much work it does to establish the measurement-construct relationship, and how much of that relationship it assumes versus demonstrates.

The observation this book has been building toward, essai by essai, is that the learning-systems field has, across six decades, taken the gap less seriously than its claims require. The measurement-construct relationships it invokes are almost universally assumed. The field's vocabulary outruns what its evidence apparatus can actually support, and the gap persists not because it has not been noticed — it has been noticed, repeatedly, by careful researchers across multiple traditions — but because the apparatus that persists serves specific production conditions, and a more adequate apparatus would serve them less well.

The structure is not: *the field is wrong about what works.* The structure is: *the field makes claims about effectiveness that its measurements are not positioned to support, and does so systematically.* These are importantly different claims. The first is a claim about facts. The second is a claim about apparatus — about the specific set of measurement practices, citation habits, and research conventions that together produce what the field calls its evidence base.

The distinction matters because the remedy differs. If the field were making factual errors about particular interventions, the remedy would be better studies of those interventions. If the field's apparatus is producing a systematic gap between what it measures and what it claims, the remedy is different apparatus. The book has not argued for either remedy. It has argued, by the accumulated force of twelve close readings, that the second diagnosis is correct and the first is not.

What this essai does is make that argument visible as argument. Across the previous essais, the claim has been implicit in the pattern. It has never been stated at this level. Here it is stated, and the rest of this essai unpacks what follows from stating it.

---

## What the field claims to care about

Begin with the vocabulary.

Open a textbook in educational psychology. Open a learning-sciences journal. Open the marketing copy for any major adaptive-learning platform. Open the abstract of any recent AI-tutor efficacy study. The vocabulary is remarkably consistent. The field claims to be producing evidence about *learning*. About *understanding*. About *mastery*. About *effectiveness*. About, in more recent vintages, *personalization* and *engagement*. The platforms claim to improve *learning outcomes*. The studies measure *learning gains*. The reformers advocate for *learning at scale*.

Each of these words points toward a construct. Each of the constructs has, in serious research traditions, substantial theoretical and empirical articulation. None of them is trivial.

Consider *learning*. In Robert Bjork's decades of experimental work, learning is not a single construct but a distinction between two related-but-separable things: storage strength and retrieval strength. Storage strength refers to how well a representation is encoded; retrieval strength refers to how accessible it is at the moment of test. A student can have high retrieval strength at the end of a unit — they perform well on the post-test — without high storage strength. Weeks or months later, the retrieval strength decays rapidly, and the post-test performance turns out to have been measuring the wrong thing. This is the central insight of what Bjork calls desirable difficulties: conditions that depress retrieval strength in the short run often build storage strength in the long run, which is why practice conditions that feel harder (spaced practice, interleaved practice, testing rather than re-reading) produce better long-term outcomes than practice conditions that feel easier.

The distinction is not abstract. It is operationalized in the experimental literature. A learning claim grounded in Bjork's construct of learning requires evidence of storage strength, not just retrieval strength — which requires measuring performance after a delay, in new contexts, on items not identical to those used in training. The methodology exists. It has existed since the early 1990s. It is the basis of essentially every recommendation in *Make It Stick*, the popular synthesis by Brown, Roediger, and McDaniel that many readers of this book have encountered.

Now consider how *learning* is typically operationalized in EdTech efficacy research. The outcome measure is usually a post-test administered at the end of the instructional unit. The post-test uses items aligned with the instructional content. The interval between instruction and test is hours to days. The retrieval context is the same or similar to the learning context. What this operationalization measures is retrieval strength at short delay. What Bjork's construct of learning requires is storage strength at longer delay under different retrieval conditions. These are not the same thing. They are related. They are also empirically dissociable — which is what forty years of experimental work on desirable difficulties has demonstrated.

The gap between the two is not subtle. It is structural. And it is present in nearly every efficacy claim the book has read.

Consider *understanding*. Jean Lave and Etienne Wenger, and before them John Dewey, and alongside them James Greeno and the situated-cognition tradition, have spent decades articulating understanding as something different from performance on items. Understanding, in these traditions, involves the capacity to apply knowledge in contexts that differ from the contexts of acquisition. It involves participation in practices — knowing how to use what one knows in the world where it applies. It is not adequately tested by asking whether a learner can reproduce a trained response to a trained stimulus. Transfer testing — the capacity to apply learning to problems that differ meaningfully from the problems used in training — is the minimum methodological requirement for a claim about understanding.

Transfer testing has been advocated for in educational research since Thorndike's early twentieth-century work on the transfer of learning. Thorndike argued that transfer was much more limited than educators assumed. Subsequent work has largely confirmed this: transfer is real but specific, and most trained performance does not generalize automatically to untrained contexts. For a claim about understanding to be well-grounded, the evidence must include transfer data. Not necessarily a lot — but some.

How often does EdTech efficacy research include transfer testing? Rarely. Cognitive Tutor's evaluations, some of the more rigorous in the field, have occasionally included transfer measures, and the results have been instructive — effects on transfer items are typically smaller, sometimes considerably smaller, than effects on near-transfer or identical-to-training items. The pattern is not surprising to anyone working in the transfer literature. It is also almost never reported in the efficacy claims that circulate beyond the specialist literature.

Consider *mastery*. Bloom's own construct, as articulated in his mastery-learning work, involves not just performance on trained items but structural reorganization of knowledge — the kind of reorganization that allows a learner to solve problems the instruction did not specifically address. The mastery-learning tradition, which Bloom himself developed, included methodological requirements: criterion-referenced assessment against specific learning objectives, formative assessment with corrective feedback, demonstrated performance across multiple item types. Bloom's 2-sigma finding emerged from studies that implemented these methodological requirements. The 2-sigma number is cited routinely as a benchmark for tutoring effectiveness. Bloom's construct of mastery, including its methodological requirements, is cited far less often.

Consider *personalization* in the adaptive-learning tradition, as examined in Essai 8. The term invokes a construct — instruction responsive to the individual learner's state, needs, and trajectory — that has deep roots in differentiated-instruction research, in Vygotskian zone-of-proximal-development work, and in the aptitude-treatment interaction literature of the 1970s. What adaptive-learning systems typically operationalize is item sequencing and pacing based on item-level response patterns. These are not the same construct. An adaptive-learning system that delivers one item rather than another based on estimated item-level mastery is doing something technically specific; it is not, in the sense the construct implies, responding to the individual learner in a way that differs from what a competent human teacher with a rich model of the student does intuitively.

And consider *engagement*. The construct, as articulated in the psychological literature, involves attention, motivation, affect, persistence in the face of difficulty, and meaningful cognitive investment in the material. It is richly theorized. What adaptive-learning and AI-tutor efficacy research typically measures is time on task, session counts, and completion rates. These are not engagement in the construct sense. They are behavioral proxies that, under specific conditions, may correlate with engagement — and under other conditions, do not. A student can accumulate time on task while mentally elsewhere. A student can complete sessions without having engaged with the material in any meaningful cognitive sense. The apparatus does not distinguish.

This could go on. Mastery. Understanding. Learning. Personalization. Engagement. Each of these constructs has serious theoretical articulation in one or more research traditions. Each is routinely invoked by the learning-systems field's claim-making vocabulary. Each is routinely operationalized, in efficacy research, as aligned-item performance at short timescale. The gap between the construct and the operationalization is not the researchers' invention; it is what the apparatus produces. But it is also not small.

It is, taken across the field, the difference between the learning the vocabulary claims and the performance the measurements index.

---

## What the field has tried

It would be inaccurate to say the field has not tried to close this gap. It has tried, across multiple traditions, for decades. That these attempts have not produced a different default apparatus is itself instructive.

Consider transfer testing. Thorndike's early twentieth-century work established both that transfer occurs and that it is more limited than educators typically assume. The implication — that evidence for learning ought to include evidence for transfer — has been periodically advocated across the subsequent century. John Bransford, Ann Brown, and Rodney Cocking made it a central theme of *How People Learn*, the 1999 National Academies synthesis that remains the most widely cited overview of contemporary learning science. The book spent substantial space on transfer, including a chapter organized entirely around the question of what conditions produce transfer and what evidence we have that particular instructional methods do so. The methodological implication was straightforward: efficacy research should include transfer measures if it wants to make claims about learning rather than claims about trained performance.

Two and a half decades after *How People Learn*, transfer testing remains exceptional in EdTech efficacy research. The occasional study includes it. Most do not. The reasons have been examined in the methodological literature: transfer testing is harder to design, more expensive to administer, produces smaller and noisier effects than near-transfer testing, and offers fewer publishable positive findings per research dollar. Researchers who want to include transfer measures in their studies face the normal obstacles of any research practice that is more demanding than the prevailing default.

Consider durability testing. Bjork's work on storage versus retrieval strength, spanning the early 1990s to the present, has produced a methodology for distinguishing immediate performance from durable learning: test at immediate post, then test again at delay. If the effect persists, it is more likely to reflect storage strength; if it decays rapidly, it likely reflects retrieval strength at the moment of immediate test. The methodology is neither novel nor arcane. It is advocated in essentially every serious treatment of educational evaluation published in the last thirty years.

Three-month, six-month, and twelve-month follow-up testing in EdTech efficacy research remains exceptional. Occasional studies implement it. Most do not. The obstacles are structural: follow-up testing requires continued access to the study population, which typically becomes impossible after the course or academic year ends. It requires resources across a longer timeline than the typical grant cycle supports. It produces smaller effects, because learning decays, and smaller effects reduce publishability. The pattern is the same as with transfer testing: the methodology exists, its value is widely recognized, its implementation remains rare because the incentive structure rewards the alternatives.

Consider multi-paradigm convergence. The psychometric literature on construct validation, stretching back to Cronbach and Meehl's 1955 paper and developed substantially in Samuel Messick's 1989 chapter on validity, has articulated the principle that evidence for a construct is strengthened by convergence across multiple different methods of measurement. A claim about learning is more credible if it is supported by performance measures, behavioral observations, self-report, and transfer data, with the convergence itself constituting evidence that the construct is being indexed. The principle is the foundation of validity theory in educational and psychological measurement. Its methodological implications are straightforward: efficacy claims about rich constructs should be supported by multiple outcome measures that triangulate rather than by a single measure used in isolation.

Multi-paradigm convergence in EdTech efficacy research is rare. Single-outcome studies predominate. When a researcher-designed outcome measure shows a strong effect and a standardized measure shows a weak one on the same intervention, the strong effect tends to be cited and the weak effect tends to be absent from summary reporting. The divergence, which in a construct-validation framework would be informative about what the intervention actually does, tends to be treated as a discrepancy to be resolved in favor of the measure most favorable to the intervention. This is not fraud. It is a specific selective-reporting pattern that the apparatus produces.

Consider situated assessment. Jean Lave's *Cognition in Practice* (1988), Lave and Wenger's *Situated Learning* (1991), and the broader situated-cognition tradition articulated a different framework for assessment entirely: not what a learner can reproduce on an abstracted test, but how a learner participates in practices where what has been learned applies. Grocery shoppers who struggle with abstracted fraction problems perform fluently with fractions in the shopping context. The learning is not in the individual's head in an abstracted form; it is in the practice, enacted through the learner's participation. Assessment that captures this kind of learning requires observation of practice, not administration of tests.

The situated-cognition tradition has been influential in specific corners of learning sciences. It has had essentially no impact on deployed-product efficacy research. The methodological requirements — observational data, ethnographic care, participation in the practices where learning applies — are far more demanding than randomized-controlled-trial designs with aligned post-tests, and the RCT template is what the field has institutionalized as its gold standard.

Each of these traditions — transfer testing, durability testing, multi-paradigm convergence, situated assessment — has existed for decades. Each has produced methodology that could be adopted. Each would, if routinely adopted, substantially narrow the gap between measurement and construct in learning-systems efficacy research. Each remains exceptional rather than routine. The question is why.

---

## Why the apparatus persists

It is tempting to answer this question with some version of intellectual indictment. Researchers are lazy. The field is captured. Publication is broken. Peer review has failed. Each of these diagnoses has been offered, in various forms, by various critics, and each is partially correct and partially a distraction. The critical literature in this area is not small; readers who want the long form can consult any number of treatments. The diagnoses I want to propose here is different. It is also, I think, more accurate.

The apparatus persists because it serves the specific production conditions of the field in which it operates.

Consider what a researcher needs in order to do research in this field. They need funding, which comes on grant cycles of two to five years. They need publications, which come through peer-reviewed journals with specific conventions about what counts as a study. They need students or teams to conduct the work, which requires stable institutional support. They need access to populations — schools, classrooms, platforms — which requires institutional partnerships that have their own timelines and constraints. They need findings that other researchers can cite, which requires the findings to fit the conventions subsequent researchers are working within.

Now consider what a more adequate apparatus would require. Transfer testing adds design complexity and reduces effect sizes. Durability testing extends the study timeline past the typical grant cycle. Multi-paradigm convergence requires methodological range that most research programs, optimized around a single method, do not possess. Pre-registration of analytic plans constrains the exploratory-analysis moves that often produce publishable findings. Cost-inclusive reporting introduces variables the research design cannot control and that may produce unfavorable comparisons.

Each of these would, if adopted as a default, reduce the rate at which researchers produce citable positive findings. Not because the interventions do not work — some of them do — but because the findings that survive the more demanding methodology would be smaller, noisier, and less rhetorically useful. A researcher who adopts the more demanding methodology is competing with researchers who do not. The less-demanding researcher's findings will be larger, cleaner, and more citable. The grant agencies, tenure committees, and publication venues all reward the latter. This is not a secret. Everyone inside the apparatus knows it.

The same pressures operate on the institutions that surround the research. Product vendors who want to make efficacy claims have commercial reasons to prefer methodologies that produce larger numbers. Policy bodies that want to recommend interventions have political reasons to prefer evidence that looks clean. Philanthropists who fund efficacy research want defensible findings that can survive advocacy scrutiny, and clean findings are easier to defend than nuanced ones. The journal editors who publish in this space respond, reasonably, to what their referees will accept, and what referees will accept is shaped by the conventions the field has institutionalized.

No individual in this system is behaving cynically. Researchers are doing their best work under the constraints of their funding. Product vendors are reporting findings that pass peer review. Policy bodies are making recommendations based on available evidence. Philanthropists are funding work that looks defensible. The apparatus is not what anyone chose; it is what the incentives produce when rational actors operate within them.

This is why advocacy for better methodology has not produced better methodology. The problem is not that researchers do not know better methodology exists — they do. The problem is that operating under the existing apparatus produces careers; operating against it produces, for most researchers, shorter and more difficult careers. A young researcher who wants to implement the full Messick-validity framework for every efficacy claim is competing with researchers who do not. The young researcher will produce fewer papers, smaller effects, and less-citable findings. They will also, in most cases, not get tenure.

The apparatus persists, in other words, because it is an equilibrium. Equilibria are stable not because the actors inside them are irrational but because the actors inside them are responding rationally to incentives that no single actor created and no single actor can change. Changing an equilibrium of this kind requires changing the incentives — which requires coordination across grant agencies, tenure systems, journal conventions, institutional practices, and funder expectations. Such coordination is rare. It has happened, occasionally, in specific subfields when structural pressures aligned. It has not happened in learning-systems research, and none of the pressures currently aligned would produce it.

Naming the apparatus as an equilibrium is the observation this essai is offering. It is a structural observation, not a moral one. It is also, I want to suggest, the correct diagnosis. Researchers in this field are not broken. The evidence base in this field is not broken in the sense of being produced by broken people. The evidence base is what the apparatus produces when careful, rigorous, well-meaning researchers operate under the conventions the apparatus enforces. Improving any individual researcher's methods would not change what the field's evidence base looks like, because the evidence base is the aggregate output of many careful researchers responding to shared incentives.

What the book has been doing, across twelve essais and now this one, is making that apparatus visible. When the apparatus is visible, it can be examined. When it can be examined, readers of efficacy claims — the book's reader — can ask better questions of those claims than the apparatus itself asks. That shift, from reading claims produced by a taken-for-granted apparatus to reading claims with the apparatus visible, is the book's principal deliverable.

It does not resolve the apparatus. It does not provide the reader with a method for producing better evidence themselves. It does not propose a reform program for the field. It gives the reader the one thing that precedes all of those possibilities: the recognition that the apparatus is an apparatus, with specific features, producing specific outputs, subject to examination. What any reader does with that recognition, after closing the book, is their work and their choice.

---

## What more adequate evidence would require

I want to resist, in this section, the reformer's voice. This book's project, named in its opening, was not to tell the field what to do. It was to model an epistemic posture the field has largely lacked and to leave the reader with the habit of attention that posture requires. A book that ends by prescribing the field's path forward would have become, in its final movement, the very thing it began by declining to be.

But I also want to be specific, because vagueness here would be its own failure of discipline. The reader who has worked through twelve essais now holds the apparatus visibly enough to see what it does not produce. That view entails a reciprocal view: the shape of what better evidence would look like. I can describe that shape, provisionally, without prescribing that the field adopt it.

More adequate evidence about learning systems would begin with multiple outcome measures on the same intervention. Not as a choice of best measure, but as triangulation: developer-designed items, standardized instruments, performance-based tasks, and — where feasible — observational measures of practice, each reported openly regardless of whether they converge or diverge. Where they converge, the convergence is evidence for the construct the vocabulary invokes. Where they diverge, the divergence is itself informative about what the intervention does and does not do. The selective reporting that currently treats divergence as a discrepancy to be resolved would be replaced by routine reporting of the divergence as a finding.

It would include durability testing as baseline expectation rather than exceptional addition. A claim about *learning*, as the construct is articulated in the experimental-psychology tradition, requires evidence at delay. Three months is a common minimum; twelve months is better; decay patterns across intermediate intervals are informative. The methodology is not novel. It requires continued access to study populations and research timelines that extend past the typical academic year, which is harder than single-point-of-test studies, which is why it is rare. The rarity is not a statement about the methodology's value; it is a statement about the field's production conditions.

It would include transfer testing for any claim about understanding or mastery. Not identical-to-training items. Items that require the learner to apply what they have learned to problems that differ from the problems used in instruction, in ways the instruction did not specifically address. Transfer is difficult to achieve and therefore difficult to demonstrate. That is not an argument against measuring it. That is an argument for measuring it in order to see, specifically, how much of it the intervention produces.

It would include cost as a first-class variable, as Essai 12 examined. Cost-per-unit-outcome calculations, ingredients-method documentation, shadow pricing where necessary, comparison with alternative interventions at equivalent dollars per student. Not as an add-on business question but as a necessary condition for interpreting any comparative claim. A 0.3σ finding at $100 per student is not the same finding as a 0.3σ finding at $2,000 per student; they are different findings about different interventions, and treating them as comparable without cost information is the move the apparatus currently performs routinely.

It would include pre-registration of analytic plans. Not as a bureaucratic requirement but as a methodological commitment: the questions the study is asking, the outcome measures it will report, the analytic strategies it will pursue, all specified before the data are collected. Deviations from the pre-registered plan would be reported as deviations. The selective-reporting moves that currently produce a specific distribution of findings — strong effects on researcher-designed measures, weak or unreported effects on standardized measures — would become visible as selective rather than invisible.

It would include population diversity beyond the initial research setting. Findings from selective Harvard undergraduates are findings about selective Harvard undergraduates. They may generalize; they also may not. The generalization is an empirical question, and the empirical answer requires studies in different populations before the generalization is asserted rather than assumed.

Each of these requirements has been advocated for, across decades, in the specific research traditions where it originated. None of them is novel. The list above is not my list. It is the aggregate of what serious methodologists across transfer-testing, durability-testing, construct-validation, cost-effectiveness, and meta-science traditions have been advocating since well before most readers of this book were born. What I am doing here is gathering what the apparatus does not currently produce and describing its shape as a set, so that the reader can see the set as a set.

What would happen, as a practical matter, if the field adopted this set as routine? Efficacy findings would become smaller. Many findings that currently circulate would not survive the more demanding methodology. The rate at which the field produces citable positive findings would drop substantially. The rhetorical register of the field's claim-making would change, because the claims the current apparatus produces would no longer be supportable and the claims the more adequate apparatus produces would be more modest, more conditional, and less useful for advocacy, funding, and product marketing.

This is the answer, I think, to why the more adequate apparatus has not taken. The more adequate apparatus would produce different findings — not necessarily worse findings, but findings that serve the field's current production conditions less well. Whether the field should nonetheless adopt it is not my question to answer. It is the field's question. What I am doing, in naming the shape of what better evidence would require, is completing the view the book has been building toward. The reader now sees both what the current apparatus produces and what a more adequate apparatus would produce differently. What the reader does with that view is not the book's concern.

---

## The reader, integrated

What remains to say is brief.

The reader has worked through twelve close readings and is now at the end of the thirteenth. What they hold, if the essais have done their work, is not a method. It is not a checklist. It is not a framework they can apply mechanically. It is a habit of attention: the reflex to ask, when an efficacy claim arrives, what the claim's measurement is, what construct the claim asserts the measurement indexes, and whether the relationship between the measurement and the construct has been established or assumed. That reflex, developed through the cumulative work of the previous essais, is what the book has delivered.

The reflex generalizes beyond the specific cases the book examined. A learning-systems claim the book never read can be read, by the reader, using the same moves. A technology that does not yet exist, when its efficacy is claimed, can be read the same way. A research field other than educational technology, when it makes similar kinds of claims, can be read similarly. The moves are portable. That was the point of the spiral: not to produce a survey of specific claims but to develop, through varied application, a habit that transfers.

What the reader can also now see is the apparatus itself, as apparatus. Before the book, the field's evidence base appeared as a neutral background — the thing research produces, the thing claims are based on, the thing against which new claims are compared. After the book, that evidence base is visible as a specific equilibrium of measurement practices, production conditions, and institutional incentives. The same findings look different when seen in this light. Bloom's 2-sigma, read with the apparatus visible, is a specific finding under specific conditions serving specific citation functions in a specific field. Kestin's physics tutor, read with the apparatus visible, is another specific finding in the same tradition. Neither is false. Both are what they are, more precisely than the vocabulary around them typically allows.

The book's argument, such as it is, is that this is how efficacy claims in this field ought to be read. Not as either true or false, not as either endorsements or debunkings, but as specific findings with specific conditions producing specific relationships to the constructs the field claims to care about. The habit of reading them this way is not a skill the reader will apply effortfully. It is, I hope, a disposition the reader now has — available, operational, unavoidable.

What remains is the closing essai, which does not extend this examination. It releases the reader to it.

---

*[End of draft. Projected page count at typical trade-scholarly typesetting: 29–31 pages. Word count: approximately 5,900.]*

---

## Author's notes on this draft

Before handing this to contributors or hostile readers, several structural observations worth logging:

The draft lands at roughly 5,900 words, which at standard trade-scholarly typesetting (about 400 words per printed page) comes out to 29–31 pages in book form — within the projected 28–32 range. The ceiling is holding.

Block A delivers the structural observation cleanly. The Bloom/Kestin opening works as specified; placing them side by side performs the generalization move that the essai needs without belaboring it. The explicit naming of the three-part structure (measurement, construct, asserted relationship) gives the reader vocabulary they can carry into the rest of the essai.

Block B is the longest section. This is appropriate — making the construct literature visible for the first time in the book requires room, and the Bjork / Bransford / Lave citations do real work here. The risk flagged in the specification was becoming a construct-literature primer; the draft manages this by treating each construct briefly (a few paragraphs) and keeping the anchor on what the apparatus does not index rather than on the constructs themselves. Contributor review should confirm the citations are accurate to primary sources.

Block C's treatment of transfer, durability, multi-paradigm convergence, and situated assessment is where the draft runs the highest risk of reading as a survey. I have tried to hold this down by keeping each tradition to a paragraph or two, naming what it advocates and why it has not been adopted, and moving on. The pattern — methodology exists, obstacles are structural, rarity is not about lack of awareness — should emerge from the sequence itself. Watch for readers who experience Block C as too long.

Block D is the most delicate block in the essai. The specification flagged that the "apparatus persists because of incentives" claim is load-bearing and vulnerable to misreading. The draft handles this by explicitly naming each actor (researcher, vendor, policy body, philanthropist, editor) and stating that none is acting cynically, and by naming the result as equilibrium rather than failure. If any reader finishes Block D thinking "the field is rotten," the block has not landed. I believe the draft holds this, but it is the place I would most want a hostile reader to push on.

Block E is the Revise move, and it runs longer than the specification's suggested three to four pages. The specification may have underestimated this. The list of requirements needs to be specific enough to be useful and complete enough not to feel truncated; the register needs to stay examinational rather than prescriptive. The draft manages both, I think, but this is another place where hostile reading would be useful.

Block F is short, as specified. I have cut language that would have extended it. The reader needs to close this essai and move directly into Essai 14. Any rhetorical flourish here would misalign with what Essai 14 is for.

One thing I notice in the draft that was not fully anticipated in the specification: the essai's relationship to Essai 2 is stronger than I expected it to be. Essai 2 installed the apparatus through enactment; Essai 13 names what the apparatus was suppressing. The structural symmetry between the two gives the book a spine — apparatus visible through use (Essai 2), apparatus visible as itself (Essai 13), with the spiral of Essais 3–12 showing the apparatus applied across varied material in between. The preface should probably make this spine visible to the reader up front.

Revisions I would make in a second pass:

- The transition between Block D and Block E could be smoother. Block D ends on the equilibrium observation; Block E opens on the reformer's voice I am resisting. The pivot is currently a little abrupt.
- The reference to *Make It Stick* in Block B is load-bearing — it gives the reader an accessible entry into Bjork's construct — but it risks overemphasizing a popular rather than primary source. I would probably balance it with a specific primary citation.
- Block E's list of requirements might benefit from breaking into a more structured presentation, but every structural move I've tried in drafting felt like it tipped into textbook register. Prose is the right register here; the cost is readers who want a checklist do not get one. That is probably the right trade.
# On What Examination Licenses

*Essai 14*

---

I want to return to the sentence the first essai of this volume opened on.

In April of 2023, speaking from the TED stage, Sal Khan said: *"We are at the cusp of using AI for probably the biggest positive transformation that education has ever seen."* When the first essai opened on that sentence, three years ago in the chronology of the book's examination, I invited you to hold it lightly. To hear it as a claim rather than as a description. To notice that the sentence was doing specific work — positioning a product, invoking a tradition, making a promise whose evidence was not yet in. I promised that by the end of the volume you would be able to hold that sentence differently than you could have held it at the beginning.

If the examination has done what it was meant to do, the sentence is different now.

It is not — I want to be specific about this — that the sentence is now false where before it was true. It is not that examination has produced a verdict. The sentence was always the same sentence. What has changed is your capacity to read it. You can now hear, in that single sentence, the specific things the volume has been examining: the aligned-measure effect size, the compressed timescale, the unnamed baseline, the absent cost denominator, the construct mismatch, the behavioral engagement doing work that learning evidence would have to do, the rhetorical moves that will protect the claim when evidence arrives below it, the reviewers and funders whose incentives reward the sentence's production, the distance between what the sentence asserts and what its evidence supports. You did not hear these things in the sentence when you first met it. You hear them now. This is not a trick of memory; it is the formation of an attention.

What I want this closing essai to do is not summarize what you have learned, which would refuse the tradition the volume has been working in. And not deliver a verdict on Sal Khan, or Khan Academy, or the field, or AI tutoring, or educational technology, or research in learning systems, which would be unfaithful both to the evidence the volume has examined and to the provisional stance the volume has tried to model. And not prescribe what you should do next, which would impose on your judgment the shape of mine.

What I want to do is release you.

---

The specific development that reached me after the twelfth essai was completed, and that belongs briefly in this closing, is worth naming. After the April 2026 *Chalkbeat* interview in which Kristen DiCerbo said publicly that she was not seeing the revolution in education, Khan Academy did something the rhetorical-moves analysis of Essai 11 did not predict. The organization did not double down. It did not simply pivot to "investing in human systems" as an indefinite deferral. It overhauled the product. Khanmigo, as a separate chatbot that students were expected to seek out and engage with, was significantly de-emphasized. The AI assistance was folded directly into Khan Academy's practice-problem infrastructure — into the specific moments when students were already engaged with mathematical or scientific work, rather than existing as a parallel conversational channel the student had to initiate. The revolutionary super-tutor framing of 2023 was not revived. The product was rebuilt around what the evidence from three years of deployment had shown: that students do not, in aggregate, spontaneously initiate productive dialogue with an AI chatbot, but that AI assistance offered at the point of a student's existing work can be useful in specific ways.

This is a more consequential move than it might appear. It is what responding to evidence looks like when the response is not primarily rhetorical. It is also not a vindication of the 2023 framing — the transformation Khan invoked from the TED stage is not what has been built, and I do not know whether Khan Academy will say so. But the product itself, as a material artifact, now embodies the lesson. The tool that exists in 2026 is a different tool than the tool that was promised in 2023. The organization, whatever it says about the arc, did the thing that taking evidence seriously requires.

I mention this because the book's examination is not a static inventory. The field continues to move. The specific claims the book has examined — Khan's 2023 sentence, Khanmigo's early framing, Kestin's effect size, Rori's cost-per-student, the measurement practices of several generations of educational technology — will continue to be reread as new evidence arrives and new claims are made. The habit of attention you have been developing is not a verdict you can now deliver on the field as it was in 2025. It is a practice for reading the field as it is now, and as it will be tomorrow, and the week after that.

---

I want to invoke Montaigne's motto one last time, because it has been the discipline of the whole volume, and because the tradition of its use is more specific than the phrase alone conveys.

*Que sais-je?* Montaigne had the phrase struck on a medal in his study, sometime around 1576. The medal's face carried a balance with its two bowls in equilibrium — the specific visual figure of *epoche*, the Pyrrhonist suspension of judgment, neither side prevailing over the other. What Montaigne meant by *Que sais-je?* was not what we sometimes take it to mean today: a disclaimer of ignorance, a modest preface to stronger claims that will follow once the rhetorical floor has been laid. He meant something more demanding. He meant the active practice of holding one's own judgments open, of refusing the premature closure that certainty offers as comfort, of staying with the difficulty of a question rather than accepting a verdict that arrives too quickly. The balance in equilibrium is not indecision. It is discipline.

This is what I have wanted the book to teach — not the specific findings about specific studies, though those matter, but the practice of holding an efficacy claim, or a rhetorical move, or a measurement choice, or a sigma number, in the balance while examining what it is made of. The five questions, the rhetorical moves, the construct-proxy distinction, the cost denominator, the engagement-versus-learning observation — these are not a checklist. They are an apparatus of attention. They are what you apply, not what you apply them to. The balance in equilibrium is the stance from which the examination is possible. The volume's claim, if the volume has a claim, is that this stance is available to non-specialists. That it does not require a doctorate in educational psychology or in statistics or in philosophy of science. That what is required is the discipline of not rushing the judgment, and the vocabulary to name what one is looking at.

*What do I know?* is Montaigne's question. It is also, I hope, yours now, more fully than it was before. Not as a disclaimer. As a discipline.

---

I want to close this essai by naming what the examination has not done, because what it has not done is as important as what it has.

It has not delivered a verdict on AI tutoring. I do not know whether AI tutoring, across the next five or ten or fifty years, will produce learning outcomes that approach, exceed, or fall short of the various benchmarks different camps in the field have invoked. The evidence available in 2026 does not support confident predictions in any direction. What it supports is what I have tried to show: that the specific current claims have specific current evidence, and that the specific current evidence has specific current limits.

It has not delivered a verdict on educational technology. Different technologies have produced different effects in different contexts over different timescales. The pattern across decades is neither uniform success nor uniform failure; it is a pattern of specific tools doing specific things in specific contexts, typically at modest magnitudes, rarely at the magnitudes that launched them. Whether the pattern will change with generative AI is an empirical question that the apparatus of attention you now carry is better equipped to read as the evidence accumulates.

It has not delivered a verdict on particular researchers, particular organizations, particular products. The individual people I have examined in this volume — Bloom, VanLehn, Anderson and Koedinger, the Eedi and Google DeepMind teams, the Rori researchers, Kestin and his colleagues, DiCerbo and Khan — are, so far as I can tell, serious people doing serious work. What I have argued is not that any of them have failed; it is that the apparatus around them, the measurement conventions they have inherited, the rhetorical pressures they operate within, shape what their work can be heard to say. Naming the apparatus is not condemning the people working inside it. The volume is a trial of the apparatus. Not of the workers.

It has not prescribed what the field should do next. I have, at moments, specified what next-generation research would require to be worth believing — independent measures, delayed assessment, transfer testing, cost-inclusive reporting, construct specification. These were not prescriptions but descriptions of what taking the apparatus seriously would mean methodologically. Whether the field will move toward them, and whether specific researchers and organizations will adopt them, is not my decision or yours. It is theirs. The book hopes to make their decision a more visible one; it does not make it for them.

And it has not told you what to do.

Not because I do not care what you do. If you have read this volume, or even an essai or two of it, you are already, almost certainly, someone who cares how the claims in the learning-systems field are made and evaluated. A researcher, a teacher, a product designer, a funder, a journalist, a policymaker, a parent trying to decide whether to buy her child a product, a practitioner trying to decide whether to adopt a tool in her classroom. The specific use you will make of the attention you have developed in this volume depends on your specific position and your specific work. I cannot specify it for you. I should not.

What I can say is this. The habit of attention the book has tried to cultivate — the willingness to read a specific claim for its specific evidence, to name what the evidence supports and what it does not, to credit honest acknowledgments and identify rhetorical moves, to hold the balance in equilibrium rather than rush to judgment — is a discipline that serves you in the learning-systems field and beyond it. The moves the book has examined are not unique to educational technology. They are the moves that human beings and human institutions make when claims and evidence come into tension with each other, across every field where claims matter and evidence is difficult. You will recognize them in medicine, in policy, in engineering, in finance, in every domain where people build tools and measure what the tools do and have to explain the gap between what they hoped and what they got. The apparatus is portable. The attention you have developed in reading about educational technology is the same attention you bring to any domain where claims meet their own evidence and something has to be said about the difference.

---

Montaigne, closing *On Experience*, the last of the essais he lived to complete, did not summarize what his essais had argued across three books and two decades of revision. He did not deliver a verdict on the experience he had spent a life examining. He wrote instead about what it is like to inhabit one's own body, to hunt after nature's footprints with whatever diminishing faculties one has left, to marry pleasure with necessity, to accept that the mind is stitched to the flesh and that wisdom does not mean separating oneself from this condition but more fully inhabiting it. He released his reader into the reader's own life. He returned to what he called nature, which he meant in a specific and careful way — not nature as the romantics would come to imagine it, but the ordinary fabric of living, of eating and sleeping and working and aging and dying, of which his essais had been, from the start, the examination.

This is the tradition the volume has been working in. This is the closing it has been preparing to make.

What I am doing now, at the end of fourteen essais, is returning you to your own examination. To the next claim you encounter, in the next journal article or conference talk or investor presentation or product announcement or dinner-party conversation, that asks you to believe something specific about how people learn. What you do with that claim is yours. The book ends here. The examination continues. That is how this form is meant to work.

*Que sais-je?*

I leave you with the question, in the form Montaigne left it — not as a disclaimer, not as a conclusion, not as a verdict, but as the discipline that makes honest inquiry possible. The balance in equilibrium. The bowls neither prevailing.

Thank you for coming this far.

---

*[End of Essai 14. End of Volume I: The Evidence Problem. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 16–18 pages. Word count: approximately 2,000.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**Length.** The draft is deliberately much shorter than prior essais — approximately 2,000 words, projecting to 16-18 pages. The prior thirteen essais have ranged from 22 to 28 pages. This closing essai runs shorter because the closing form requires restraint that the prior essais did not. An essai that closes the volume by assembling twenty paragraphs of synthesis is performing exhaustiveness; the form calls for release, which is always shorter.

**Structure.** The essai has five short movements rather than the five blocks of prior essais: the return to Khan's 2023 sentence with attention to how the reader now hears it; the brief acknowledgment of Khan Academy's post-April 2026 product overhaul (the one new development the research surfaced); the invocation of *Que sais-je?* with specific attention to the motto's history and discipline; the enumeration of what the examination has not done; the return to Montaigne and the release of the reader. Each movement is compact. The essai does not argue; it gathers and lets go.

**Use of the research document.** The research document was substantive, but the essai uses very little of it explicitly. Montaigne's motto and the 1576 medal with the balance in equilibrium is the one specific historical reference I have worked with; it earns its place because it grounds *Que sais-je?* as a discipline rather than a disclaimer. Montaigne's closing of *On Experience* is referenced but not quoted directly — the reference is enough to place the essai in the tradition without turning into a citation. The contemporary essai-tradition closings (Phillips, Solnit, Agre, Nelson, Gay) are entirely absent. The apophatic and Zen and Wittgensteinian and Heideggerian philosophical traditions are entirely absent. This was deliberate. A closing that invokes ten traditions is impressing the reader, not releasing them.

**The Khan Academy post-April 2026 development.** The research document surfaced one specific new fact: Khan Academy's overhaul of Khanmigo to fold AI assistance into practice problems rather than maintain it as a separate chatbot. The essai acknowledges this in a single short movement and treats it as what "taking evidence seriously looks like when the response is not primarily rhetorical." The treatment is brief because the essai's closing function is release, not re-engagement with specific cases. But the fact belongs in the essai because it changes the reader's relationship to the April 2026 material from Essai 11. If the pivot had been a further rhetorical move, the closing would read differently; because it was not, the closing can credit Khan Academy's trajectory modestly.

**The tradition of the closing.** The essai works in the Montaignian essai-closing tradition without announcing it. It returns to the opening (the Khan TED sentence), releases the reader with a discipline rather than a verdict (*Que sais-je?*), refuses summary and prescription explicitly, and ends on the motto in its active rather than passive form. A reader familiar with Montaigne will hear the tradition; a reader unfamiliar will still experience the release. The essai does not require the reader to recognize what it is working inside.

**The "thank you for coming this far" closing.** This is the most intimate register the book has used. It is also the most honest register available at this point. The reader has spent substantial time with the volume; a closing that pretends not to notice the reader's effort would be false to the book's conversational stance. The closing "thank you" is specific: not a general politeness but an acknowledgment that the examination required something of the reader, and that the reader gave it. I have been deliberate about placing this after the *Que sais-je?* closing rather than replacing it, so that the motto holds the structural weight and the thanks follows as a human aside.

**What this closing does not do that other closings might.**
- It does not list the book's main findings.
- It does not specify which essais the reader should particularly carry forward.
- It does not provide a glossary of the apparatus.
- It does not predict where the field is going.
- It does not recommend what the reader should read next, either within the learning-systems literature or in the broader Montaignian tradition.
- It does not quote any contemporary writer besides Khan.

Each of these absences is deliberate. Any one of them could be added in a revision pass, but adding them would shift the closing from the release-register to the reference-register, which is what I have been trying to avoid.

**What I am not sure about.**

I am not sure about the invocation of *On Experience* without quotation. The reference is to ground the essai in the Montaignian tradition specifically; a reader who knows Montaigne will hear the reference and complete it themselves. A reader who does not know Montaigne may find the reference obscure. The alternative — quoting Montaigne's closing directly — would address the unknown reader but would also add weight that the closing is trying to shed. I have chosen the lighter option. A revision pass could reconsider.

I am not sure about the "If you have read this volume, or even an essai or two of it..." paragraph. The paragraph serves a specific function — acknowledging that the reader may have come to the book in different ways, with different professional positions, with different uses for the apparatus — but it also performs a kind of enumeration that is more consultative than the rest of the essai. A revision pass could compress it further or remove it. I have kept it because many readers of a volume published in the way this volume is being published (Substack serial followed by Kindle compilation) will have come to particular essais out of order, and the paragraph respects that. But it is not essential.

I am not sure the length will feel right. A 2,000-word closing in a volume of 6,000-word essais is an eighth the length of the essais around it. This is a deliberate structural choice, but it could feel abrupt to a reader who has read the full volume in sequence. The alternative is to extend the closing with more of the research material — the apophatic tradition, Wittgenstein's silence, Zen's *mu*, contemporary writers' releases — but each addition would work against the closing's function. I have chosen brevity. A reader who expects more may want more. I will leave that choice open.

Finally, I am not sure the essai avoids self-congratulation. The move of saying "the book has not delivered a verdict" is itself a claim about the book, and a claim that attributes discipline to the author. I have tried to make the move in the register of description rather than of self-praise — these are the things the examination has not done, not the things I have heroically refrained from doing. But the line is delicate. If the closing drifts toward self-congratulation, tell me where.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On Reading an Efficacy Claim

*Essai 2*

---

Here is a sentence.

*Students who used Khan Academy's MAP Accelerator at the recommended level achieved math growth scores that were 0.26 standard deviations higher than those of the comparison group.*

This sentence comes from a paper titled *Estimating the Causal Effects of Khan Academy MAP Accelerator Across Demographic Subgroups*, authored by Phillip Grimaldi, Kodi Weatherholtz, and Kelli Millwood Hill of Khan Academy. It was presented at the 15th International Conference on Educational Data Mining in Durham, United Kingdom, in July 2022. The study population was substantial: 180,307 students and 5,818 teachers across 99 school districts during the 2020-2021 academic year. The outcome measure was the NWEA MAP Growth assessment, a computerized adaptive test widely used for accountability purposes across American schools. The finding has been cited by Khan Academy in its marketing, by the Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation in its tutoring advocacy, and by federal education databases that register the study as providing "Moderate Evidence" of effectiveness under the Every Student Succeeds Act.

A 0.26-sigma effect is, by any contemporary standard in education research, a respectable finding. Matthew Kraft's 2020 analysis of 747 randomized controlled trials in education found the median effect size to be about 0.10 sigma. Khan Academy's number is more than twice that median. In Kraft's revised benchmarks for field-based education research — where he classifies effects above 0.20 as "Large" — the 0.26 number sits comfortably above the threshold. Taken at face value, this is evidence that Khan Academy's platform helps students learn mathematics.

I want to ask you to hold this sentence for a moment before this essai goes any further. Notice that it sounds credible. It is credible. The authors are serious researchers; the sample size is large; the statistical methods are appropriate for the study design; the finding is not inflated beyond what the data support. This is not a fraudulent study or a sloppy one. It is, by the conventions of the field in which it was produced, a competent piece of work.

And yet.

In the Fall 2024 issue of *Education Next*, a senior advisor at the XQ Institute named Laurence Holt published a short analysis of this study and a handful of others like it. He titled the piece *The 5 Percent Problem: Online mathematics programs may benefit most the kids who need it least*. Holt's argument was specific and, once you have read it, difficult to un-see. The 0.26-sigma effect Khan Academy reports is real, but it is the effect on students who used the platform at the "recommended" level — defined in the study as 30 or more minutes per week. How many students in the 180,307-person sample actually met that threshold?

Approximately 4.7%.

The remaining 95.3% of students in the study — about 172,000 of the 180,307 — did not reach the usage level required to see the reported effect. The headline finding, in other words, is a finding about the roughly 8,500 students who used the platform the way the researchers recommended, not about the 180,000 students who received access to the platform. The study's design explicitly excluded the 95% from the comparison that produced the 0.26 number.

Holt put this in plain terms. *"Imagine a doctor prescribing a sophisticated new drug to 100 patients and finding 95 of them didn't take it as prescribed. That is the situation with many online math interventions. They are a solution for the 5 percent."*

This is where the essai begins.

I am going to walk you through this finding carefully. Not to adjudicate whether Khan Academy works or does not work — that is not the question the essai is asking. I am going to walk through it because the 0.26-sigma number, and the way it travels in the world, is an excellent specimen of the kind of claim you will encounter routinely across the rest of this volume, and the rest of your life in this field. The claim is real. The researchers are honest. The analysis is methodologically competent. And yet something is happening, between the analysis and the citation, that the reader of the citation is not being told.

The apparatus this essai will build is the set of questions you can ask of any such claim to see what the citation does not tell you. Not five abstract principles. Five concrete questions, arising from what we are about to do with the Khan Academy study, which you will then carry with you into every efficacy claim the rest of the volume examines — and every efficacy claim you encounter after this book is closed.

---

Begin with the first question. *What was measured, specifically?*

The MAP Growth assessment used as the outcome measure in the Khan Academy study is not a neutral test. It is a computerized adaptive test developed by NWEA, a nonprofit assessment organization whose products are used in many American school districts for benchmarking and accountability. The MAP Growth mathematics assessment covers a specific curriculum — the skills that American K-8 mathematics curricula are designed to teach, organized around standards and sequenced by grade level. The test is good at what it does. It measures, with psychometric rigor, how well students can perform on items drawn from the curriculum American schools teach.

Now consider what the intervention was. Khan Academy's MAP Accelerator, as the study's own documentation describes it, is "a personalized, web-based mathematics mastery learning tool developed in partnership with NWEA." The platform was built, explicitly, to support the same skills that the MAP Growth assessment measures. This is not a hidden fact or a methodological trick — Khan Academy says so openly, and the partnership with NWEA is part of the product's value proposition. The platform is designed to prepare students for the MAP Growth test. The study measured student performance on the MAP Growth test.

This is not a scandal. Education researchers have a term for this: *alignment*. When an intervention is designed to teach specific skills and is evaluated on a test of those same skills, the intervention and the outcome measure are aligned. Aligned interventions, evaluated on aligned measures, reliably produce larger effect sizes than the same interventions evaluated on less-aligned measures. This is a well-documented pattern in the education research literature, and it is the first concrete question the apparatus teaches you to ask.

*What was measured, and what was the measurement aligned to?*

The Khan Academy finding is an effect on a test aligned to the curriculum the platform was built to teach. This is a specific thing. It is evidence about performance on aligned items. It may also be evidence about something broader — about mathematical understanding, about learning, about whatever the construct is that the study's framing invokes. But the evidence for the broader construct requires additional work to establish. The alignment between intervention and outcome measure is the first thing the apparatus surfaces, and it is the first specification you carry forward into the rest of your reading.

---

Second question. *What was the baseline?*

Every effect size is a difference. A 0.26-sigma effect is not "this intervention produces learning"; it is "students receiving this intervention performed 0.26 standard deviations higher than some comparison group performed." The comparison group defines what the effect is an effect against. Change the comparison group, and the effect changes — sometimes dramatically, sometimes in ways that reveal the intervention is doing more or less than the headline suggests.

The Khan Academy 2022 study used a specific comparison. Students categorized as "recommended usage" — 30 or more minutes per week on the platform — were compared to students categorized as "comparison" — less than 15 minutes per week on the platform. Both groups had access to Khan Academy. Both were enrolled in the same schools and districts. Both took the same MAP Growth assessment. The difference between them was not whether they had the platform but how much they used it.

This matters because the comparison is doing something specific. The study is not comparing Khan Academy users to students who received no similar intervention. It is not comparing Khan Academy users to students in a district that never adopted the platform. It is not comparing Khan Academy to a different intervention — a workbook, a tutor, a different platform — at comparable cost. The comparison is, in effect, *students who used the platform a lot versus students who used the platform a little*.

An effect of this kind is evidence for something real: that engagement with this platform, at sufficient dosage, is associated with measurable performance gains on an aligned test. It is not evidence for several other things that the citation apparatus often conflates with it. It is not evidence that providing the platform to a school produces gains, on average, across that school's students. It is not evidence that the platform is more effective than alternative interventions. It is not evidence about what happens to students who receive the platform but do not engage with it intensively.

The authors of the study do not claim any of these additional things. The apparatus of citation, which is a different apparatus from the apparatus of research, frequently acts as though they had.

*What was the baseline, and is this comparison the comparison I thought it was?*

---

Third question. *What population is this a finding about?*

Holt's "5 Percent Problem" is, at its core, a population question. The study's headline compares about 8,500 students who used the platform at the recommended level to a comparison group that also drew from the same 180,000-student pool. What happened to the other ~172,000 students — the ones who received access to the platform but did not meet the 30-minute threshold — is absent from the headline. The effect size is an effect on the high-usage subset, not on the full study population.

The authors of the study acknowledge this, if you read carefully. The analytical decision to compare high-usage students to low-usage students is transparent in the methodology. It is not hidden. But the framing of the finding in downstream citation often treats the 0.26-sigma figure as though it described what Khan Academy does, in general, when deployed to schools. It does not describe that. It describes what Khan Academy does to the subset of students who use it as recommended, compared to students in the same schools who did not.

This matters because the selection into high usage is not random. Students who reach 30 minutes per week on a mathematics practice platform are a non-random subset of the student population. They are students who — for reasons that include motivation, access to time and devices outside of school, home environments that support sustained independent work, and probably dozens of factors the study does not measure — engage with the platform intensively when it is available. The platform's effect on *these* students is what the 0.26 number reports. Whether the platform would have the same effect on the other 95%, if they could be induced to use it at the same level, is a different question. The study does not answer that question. It is not designed to answer that question.

Holt's medical analogy is useful here. A drug study that reports strong effects on the patients who actually took the drug — while 95% of the prescribed patients did not — is not a refutation that the drug works. It is a specific and narrower claim than the drug marketing implies. The drug works for those who take it. Whether it is a viable public-health intervention depends on how many will take it, and on whether the ones who do are systematically different from the ones who don't. Those are separate questions, and the drug study does not settle them.

The same logic applies to learning systems. Khan Academy's MAP Accelerator may well produce 0.26-sigma gains for students who engage with it at the recommended level. Whether this makes it a viable intervention for the populations Khan Academy markets to — particularly, the "historically under-resourced groups" the study foregrounds — depends on who in those populations actually engages at the recommended level, and on whether the 95% who do not engage are losing something the 5% who do are gaining. Again, the study does not answer these questions, and the citation of the 0.26 number in contexts where they matter does work the study does not authorize.

*What population is this a finding about, and how does this population relate to the population the finding is cited as informing?*

---

Fourth question. *What was the timescale, and would the finding survive a durability test?*

The Khan Academy study assessed outcomes in the spring of 2021, using a baseline from the fall of 2020. The measurement window was one academic year. This is a longer timescale than many EdTech efficacy studies use — some measure outcomes within weeks of the intervention — but it is still a relatively short window for claims about learning.

Here is why this matters. There is a distinction in the experimental psychology literature, developed across several decades by Robert Bjork and others, between *performance* and *learning*. Performance is what a student can demonstrate at a specific moment. Learning is what persists. The two are empirically dissociable: conditions that boost performance in the short term — massed practice, blocked scheduling, familiar testing contexts — often fail to build the durable storage that supports performance months or years later. Conditions that depress short-term performance — spaced practice, interleaved content, retrieval under difficulty — often produce the opposite pattern, with weaker immediate gains but stronger long-term retention.

Bjork's distinction is not a fringe finding. It is a foundational result in the learning sciences literature, reported consistently across many studies, and it is the basis for essentially every evidence-based recommendation for effective practice (spaced repetition, retrieval practice, desirable difficulty). Any claim about learning, as opposed to a claim about immediate performance, requires durability evidence. A test administered at the end of the intervention year, on content the intervention was designed to teach, measures performance under conditions that maximize retrieval strength. What storage strength remains three months later, or a year later, or upon entry to a more advanced course, is a separate question requiring separate measurement.

The Khan Academy study does not provide durability data. It is not designed to. The MAP Growth measurement windows used are aligned to school-year accountability cycles, which is the measurement cadence the product was built to serve. Whether the 0.26-sigma gains remain detectable at the start of the next academic year, or predict performance on subsequent-year MAP Growth tests, is not in the study. It might be addressable through Khan Academy's internal longitudinal data, but that evidence — if it exists — is not part of the finding as published.

This is not a criticism of the study. It is a description of what the study measured and what it did not. The 0.26-sigma finding is an effect on aligned performance at the end of one academic year. Whether it is also an effect on learning, in the fuller sense of durable transferable understanding, is a separate empirical question that the study does not address and that the citation apparatus often treats as settled.

*What was the timescale, and what kind of claim does a finding at this timescale support?*

---

Fifth question. *What was the cost, and against what?*

Cost almost never appears in the headline reporting of EdTech efficacy studies. It is treated, by convention, as a separate concern — a business question, a procurement question, something for purchasing departments rather than for research.

This separation, I will argue across several essais in this volume, is itself a move with epistemic consequences. For now, at the level of the apparatus, the question is simple: *what does this effect cost to produce?*

Khan Academy Districts, the institutional product referenced in the 2022 study, is priced at approximately $10 per student per year. This is, by the standards of educational interventions, remarkably low. A high-quality 1:1 tutoring program — the kind of program that produced Bloom's 2-sigma finding in 1984, which we will examine in the next essai — costs on the order of $2,000 to $4,000 per student per year in contemporary dollars. Modern research-validated tutoring programs, Saga Education and MATCH Corps among them, operate in this range. The cost ratio between high-quality human tutoring and Khan Academy's platform is approximately 200-to-1 to 400-to-1.

This matters for how the 0.26-sigma effect should be read. A 0.26-sigma effect produced at $10 per student per year is not the same finding as a 2.0-sigma effect produced at $2,000 per student per year. They are findings about different interventions under radically different cost structures. Placing them on the same axis — treating both as interventions the field could "choose between" — obscures what each is evidence for.
