The high-quality tutor costs more because the tutor is producing more. The platform costs less because the platform is producing less. This is not a criticism of the platform; it is a description of what a 200-to-400x cost ratio implies about comparability.

I will develop this point at length in a later essai. For now, the apparatus requires you to hold cost as a first-class analytical variable, not as a separate business concern. An effect size without its cost denominator is a number that is difficult to interpret. Two effect sizes from interventions at different costs are not two points on a single axis.

*What did this cost to produce, and what does the effect mean at this cost?*

---

Five questions. Outcome measure. Baseline. Population. Timescale. Cost.

If you ask these five questions of the Khan Academy finding, here is what the 0.26-sigma number is evidence for:

*This platform, aligned to the assessment used to measure it, used at the recommended dosage for an academic year, produces observed performance gains of about 0.26 standard deviations on students who engage with it at that dosage, at a cost of approximately $10 per student per year. Students who do not engage at the recommended dosage — approximately 95% of students provided access — do not appear in the headline comparison.*

This is a much narrower and more specific claim than the one that circulates. It is also, I want to emphasize, not a refutation of the study. The study's authors did not claim more than this. What they claimed is exactly what the number supports. The gap between the 0.26-sigma number as a research finding and the 0.26-sigma number as a cited benchmark is not the authors' fault. It is what happens to findings when they leave their original research context and enter the circulation apparatus of the field.

The apparatus this essai has built is not a refutation procedure. It is a reading procedure. When you ask the five questions of a claim, you do not end up with a verdict about whether the claim is true or false. You end up with a more specific claim than the one you started with — a claim that describes what the original finding actually supports, as distinct from what the finding is being asked to support in the context where you encountered it.

This distinction matters. It matters for the rest of this volume, because every essai that follows will apply these same five questions to other findings, and the cumulative pattern — what the findings actually support, compared with what they are cited as supporting — is the subject of the book. But it matters more, I think, for what you carry out of the book afterward. The next time you encounter an efficacy claim about a learning system — in a conference talk, a foundation announcement, an investor deck, a journalism piece, a Slack thread — you can ask these five questions. You do not need the study to have been flagged as suspect. You do not need to be an expert in the platform or the population. You need only to ask what the claim actually supports, and then to compare that to what the claim is being cited to support in the context where you encountered it. The gap between those two things is where the apparatus of the field does its work.

---

There is one more move I need to name before this essai closes.

Throughout the five questions, I have been pointing at something that does not quite have a name in most casual discussions of efficacy research. I want to give it a name now, because this is the concept the rest of the volume depends on.

Every efficacy claim involves three things: a measurement, a construct, and an asserted relationship between them. The measurement is what the researchers actually did — the test administered, the scores computed, the conditions compared. The construct is what the measurement is meant to be evidence for — *learning*, *mastery*, *effectiveness*, *understanding*, whatever category the claim invokes. The asserted relationship is the claim that the measurement indexes the construct adequately to license the uses the claim is put to.

The apparatus of the five questions surfaces, in each of its moves, some aspect of this three-part structure. The outcome-measure question asks what the measurement is, specifically. The baseline question asks what the measurement is measured against. The population question asks who the measurement is a measurement of. The timescale question asks over what time the measurement is valid. The cost question asks what resources were required to produce the measurement.

What all five questions are circling is the relationship between the measurement and the construct. The 0.26-sigma number is a specific measurement under specific conditions. What it is claimed as evidence for — that Khan Academy works, that the platform helps students learn mathematics, that adaptive learning is effective — are constructs the measurement may index, may partially index, or may not index at all, depending on specifics the five questions surface.

The distinction between what is measured and what that measurement is a proxy for is the central analytical tool of this volume. I will return to it across every essai. In the thirteenth essai, which is the book's deepest examination of the apparatus we are beginning here to build, I will argue that the gap between measurement and construct is the single most important observation the book makes. For now, in this essai, I am naming it so you have the vocabulary to carry forward.

A measurement is not a construct. The measurement is evidence for the construct only to the degree that the relationship between them has been established. Across most of the efficacy claims this volume examines — and, I will argue, across most of the efficacy claims the learning-systems field has produced across six decades — the relationship between measurement and construct has been assumed rather than established. That assumption is the thing the apparatus asks you to notice.

---

Here is what I want you to do with this essai.

This coming week, you will encounter an efficacy claim about a learning system. It may arrive in an article, a pitch deck, a journalism piece, a Slack discussion, a conference talk. It may be about Khan Academy, or about a competitor, or about something adjacent — a tutoring program, a curriculum intervention, a reading platform, an AI tool. When it arrives, try to hold it before you respond to it. Not for long; just for a moment. And ask the five questions.

What was measured, and was the measurement aligned to the intervention?
What was the baseline, and is the comparison the comparison I thought it was?
What population is this a finding about, and how does that population relate to the population the finding is being cited as informing?
What was the timescale, and what kind of claim does a finding at this timescale support?
What did this cost to produce, and what does the effect mean at this cost?

You may not be able to answer every question from the information in front of you. That itself is information. When a claim is circulated in a form that makes the five questions unanswerable, the gap between the claim and what the claim can support is probably large. Claims that can survive the five questions tend to be specific. Claims that cannot tend to be sweeping.

Then, once you have held the five questions against the claim, notice the gap between what the finding supports and what the finding is being cited as supporting in the context where you encountered it. That gap is where the apparatus of the field does its work. It is, I am arguing, where the apparatus of the field has been doing its work for six decades. The next essai will show how old this pattern is, and the essais after that will show how consistently it has reproduced itself across every generation of learning technology since the teaching machines of the 1950s.

The apparatus you now have is portable. You can carry it into every reading you do across the rest of this book. You can carry it into the next Tuesday morning when someone emails you a new efficacy study and asks what you think. You can carry it into the next funding meeting where a sigma number is invoked at you. You do not need me to tell you what to make of any specific claim. You can make of it what the five questions reveal.

That is what this essai was for.

The next essai takes the apparatus we have just built and points it at history. The measurement habits the five questions are surfacing are not new. They were not invented in 2022 by Khan Academy, or in 2024 by Harvard physics researchers, or in the current AI moment by any specific company. They were inherited, with remarkably little revision, from research conducted in the 1950s on pigeons pecking keys in Skinner's laboratory — research that established the measurement apparatus the field still uses, several generations of technology later. If the problem the first essai named is real, and the apparatus the second essai has built is sound, then the next essai will show that both are older than most of the technologies this book examines. The third essai begins there.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai is load-bearing for the volume. If the apparatus does not land here, the essais that follow cannot do the work they are meant to do. I want to name specifically what I am unsure about in how I have built it.

I am not sure I have been entirely fair to the Khan Academy 2022 study. The authors of that study, Grimaldi, Weatherholtz, and Hill, did careful methodological work, acknowledged the quasi-experimental nature of their design, and framed their findings in scope-appropriate ways in the paper itself. The gap I have described — between what the finding supports and what the finding is cited as supporting — is not their gap. It is a gap that emerges when the finding enters the citation apparatus of the field. A reader might reasonably ask whether I have been too quick to use a specific study as a case when the problem I am examining is larger than the study. The response is that I have tried to name, throughout, that the authors did not claim more than their finding supports. If the essai still reads as though I am criticizing the authors rather than the apparatus, it has drifted. Tell me where.

I am not sure the five questions are the right five, or the right number. They are the questions that, when applied to the Khan Academy study, surface the specific incommensurabilities I wanted you to see. Other efficacy claims might be better read through slightly different questions — about randomization, about statistical power, about pre-registration, about fidelity of implementation. A reader more expert in specific methodological traditions than I am might reasonably propose alternative apparatuses. I have chosen these five because they are the ones that recur across the cases this volume examines. But the apparatus is not sacred. It is an attempt at a useful reading tool, not a complete methodological framework.

I am not sure I have done enough with Laurence Holt's analysis. Holt's "5 Percent Problem" framing is sharp, well-sourced, and does analytical work this essai has borrowed from. A fuller treatment of Holt — the studies he examines beyond Khan Academy, the pattern he documents across multiple platforms, the response (or lack thereof) from the organizations whose studies he critiques — would be its own essai. I have used Holt as a case here, which reduces his analysis to a single illustration. A reader who finds Holt's piece indispensable and thinks it deserves more than I have given it here should push on that. I am inclined to agree.

Finally, I am not sure the apparatus I have built here is enough to carry the rest of the book. It is a reading tool, not a complete framework for evaluating learning-systems research. The essais that follow will add to it — cost as an epistemic variable receives its own essai; the measure-vs-construct distinction receives its own essai; the patterns the field displays when tools underperform receive their own essai. The apparatus grows across the volume. What I have installed here is the foundation. Whether it bears the weight of what follows will become clear, essai by essai.

The comments on this essai's Substack page are the simplest place to tell me where this apparatus falls short. In the quarterly Kindle compilation, where I incorporate the series' discussion into the record, any pushback from this essai will be visible — in marginal corrections, in footnote commentary, in whatever form the responses call for. If the apparatus fails for you, the entire book may fail. I want to know before the later essais depend on it.

The next essai examines how old the problem the apparatus surfaces actually is. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 2. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 26–28 pages. Word count: approximately 6,200.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**Block structure.** The draft implements the five-block structure specified at /c1 with one adjustment: Blocks B, C, D, E in the spec mapped to outcome-measure, baseline/5-percent-problem, timescale/cost/proxy, and invitation. I restructured slightly so that each of the five questions gets its own named block section, which produces six internal movements rather than five but clarifies the apparatus more cleanly. The spec's Block C (the 5-percent problem) is now integrated into the population question block, which I think reads better because the 5-percent problem *is* a population question.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 6,200 words, projecting to 26-28 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — within the 25-30 page range specified, slightly under the 28-page target. The spec flagged that Essai 2 needs to be short enough to re-read. I have held it tighter than the ceiling deliberately for this reason. If the revision pass wants expansion, the natural place is the timescale question (currently shortest of the five), which could take a fuller treatment of Bjork's distinction between storage and retrieval strength.

**Apparatus by enactment.** The specification asked that the five questions emerge from the close reading rather than being presented as a framework upfront. The draft does this — the questions appear in order, each arising from what the analysis is doing at that moment, and are only named as a set at the end. The first sentence of each block states the question, which anchors the structure for the reader without turning it into a checklist.

**The proxy-vs-construct distinction.** The spec required this distinction to be installed as an active category by the end of the essai. The draft does this in the "one more move" block near the closing. I kept this brief deliberately — the thirteenth essai does the deep work on measurement vs. construct, and Essai 2 should install the vocabulary without exhausting the concept.

**Specific concerns about the draft.**

- The medical analogy from Holt is quoted directly. It works rhetorically but a reader could feel the essai is leaning on Holt's frame rather than building its own. The draft's response is to use Holt as a cited source for the analogy and to carry the population analysis into specific educational terms afterward, which I think distinguishes the essai's voice from Holt's. A revision pass might want to foreground this distinction more explicitly.

- The Bjork reference in the timescale section is brief. The essai gestures at storage-vs-retrieval without unpacking it. This is deliberate — the thirteenth essai does more with Bjork — but a reader encountering the distinction for the first time may want slightly more here. One additional sentence on what the distinction implies about what Khan Academy's measurement can and cannot support would help.

- The cost section promises "a later essai" where cost is developed more fully. This is Essai 12. The forward reference is appropriate in the Kindle edition; the Substack version will need the reference removed or rephrased since readers encounter essais as they drop.

- The invitational closing block lands the three elements (finding, named uncertainty, explicit invitation) but the "named uncertainty" section is longer than in Essai 1 (four paragraphs versus three). This may be appropriate for a load-bearing essai where more of the uncertainty is structural. A revision pass can tighten if it reads as overextended.

**What the draft does particularly well.** The opening holds the 0.26 number on the page without immediately attacking it, the way the spec required. The authors are named and credited — Grimaldi, Weatherholtz, and Hill get their credit as careful researchers before the essai moves to examination. Holt's "5 Percent Problem" framing is brought in specifically with its Fall 2024 *Education Next* publication and Holt's role at the XQ Institute. The essai ends on the structural observation about measurement-vs-construct that the rest of the book will develop, without preempting the thirteenth essai's fuller treatment.

**What I am least sure about.** The closing section's bridge to Essai 3 (the teaching-machine history) commits to a specific follow-up claim — that the measurement apparatus is inherited from 1950s behaviorism. This is what the spec calls for, and I think it is right, but the bridge is assertive. A reader who has not yet read Essai 3 has to trust this framing. The invitational register of the essai does partially soften this assertion, but a revision pass should check whether the bridge can be slightly more provisional without losing the spiral's momentum.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On Reading an Efficacy Claim

*Essai 2*

---

Here is a sentence.

*Students who used Khan Academy's MAP Accelerator at the recommended level achieved math growth scores that were 0.26 standard deviations higher than those of the comparison group.*

This sentence comes from a paper titled *Estimating the Causal Effects of Khan Academy MAP Accelerator Across Demographic Subgroups*, authored by Phillip Grimaldi, Kodi Weatherholtz, and Kelli Millwood Hill of Khan Academy. It was presented at the 15th International Conference on Educational Data Mining in Durham, United Kingdom, in July 2022. The study population was substantial: 180,307 students and 5,818 teachers across 99 school districts during the 2020-2021 academic year. The outcome measure was the NWEA MAP Growth assessment, a computerized adaptive test widely used for accountability purposes across American schools. The finding has been cited by Khan Academy in its marketing, by the Bill & Melinda Gates Foundation in its tutoring advocacy, and by federal education databases that register the study as providing "Moderate Evidence" of effectiveness under the Every Student Succeeds Act.

A 0.26-sigma effect is, by any contemporary standard in education research, a respectable finding. Matthew Kraft's 2020 analysis of 747 randomized controlled trials in education found the median effect size to be about 0.10 sigma. Khan Academy's number is more than twice that median. In Kraft's revised benchmarks for field-based education research — where he classifies effects above 0.20 as "Large" — the 0.26 number sits comfortably above the threshold. Taken at face value, this is evidence that Khan Academy's platform helps students learn mathematics.

I want to ask you to hold this sentence for a moment before this essai goes any further. Notice that it sounds credible. It is credible. The authors are serious researchers; the sample size is large; the statistical methods are appropriate for the study design; the finding is not inflated beyond what the data support. This is not a fraudulent study or a sloppy one. It is, by the conventions of the field in which it was produced, a competent piece of work.

And yet.

In the Fall 2024 issue of *Education Next*, a senior advisor at the XQ Institute named Laurence Holt published a short analysis of this study and a handful of others like it. He titled the piece *The 5 Percent Problem: Online mathematics programs may benefit most the kids who need it least*. Holt's argument was specific and, once you have read it, difficult to un-see. The 0.26-sigma effect Khan Academy reports is real, but it is the effect on students who used the platform at the "recommended" level — defined in the study as 30 or more minutes per week. How many students in the 180,307-person sample actually met that threshold?

Approximately 4.7%.

The remaining 95.3% of students in the study — about 172,000 of the 180,307 — did not reach the usage level required to see the reported effect. The headline finding, in other words, is a finding about the roughly 8,500 students who used the platform the way the researchers recommended, not about the 180,000 students who received access to the platform. The study's design explicitly excluded the 95% from the comparison that produced the 0.26 number.

Holt put this in plain terms. *"Imagine a doctor prescribing a sophisticated new drug to 100 patients and finding 95 of them didn't take it as prescribed. That is the situation with many online math interventions. They are a solution for the 5 percent."*

This is where the essai begins.

I am going to walk you through this finding carefully. Not to adjudicate whether Khan Academy works or does not work — that is not the question the essai is asking. I am going to walk through it because the 0.26-sigma number, and the way it travels in the world, is an excellent specimen of the kind of claim you will encounter routinely across the rest of this volume, and the rest of your life in this field. The claim is real. The researchers are honest. The analysis is methodologically competent. And yet something is happening, between the analysis and the citation, that the reader of the citation is not being told.

The apparatus this essai will build is the set of questions you can ask of any such claim to see what the citation does not tell you. Not five abstract principles. Five concrete questions, arising from what we are about to do with the Khan Academy study, which you will then carry with you into every efficacy claim the rest of the volume examines — and every efficacy claim you encounter after this book is closed.

---

Begin with the first question. *What was measured, specifically?*

The MAP Growth assessment used as the outcome measure in the Khan Academy study is not a neutral test. It is a computerized adaptive test developed by NWEA, a nonprofit assessment organization whose products are used in many American school districts for benchmarking and accountability. The MAP Growth mathematics assessment covers a specific curriculum — the skills that American K-8 mathematics curricula are designed to teach, organized around standards and sequenced by grade level. The test is good at what it does. It measures, with psychometric rigor, how well students can perform on items drawn from the curriculum American schools teach.

Now consider what the intervention was. Khan Academy's MAP Accelerator, as the study's own documentation describes it, is "a personalized, web-based mathematics mastery learning tool developed in partnership with NWEA." The platform was built, explicitly, to support the same skills that the MAP Growth assessment measures. This is not a hidden fact or a methodological trick — Khan Academy says so openly, and the partnership with NWEA is part of the product's value proposition. The platform is designed to prepare students for the MAP Growth test. The study measured student performance on the MAP Growth test.

This is not a scandal. Education researchers have a term for this: *alignment*. When an intervention is designed to teach specific skills and is evaluated on a test of those same skills, the intervention and the outcome measure are aligned. Aligned interventions, evaluated on aligned measures, reliably produce larger effect sizes than the same interventions evaluated on less-aligned measures. This is a well-documented pattern in the education research literature, and it is the first concrete question the apparatus teaches you to ask.

*What was measured, and what was the measurement aligned to?*

The Khan Academy finding is an effect on a test aligned to the curriculum the platform was built to teach. This is a specific thing. It is evidence about performance on aligned items. It may also be evidence about something broader — about mathematical understanding, about learning, about whatever the construct is that the study's framing invokes. But the evidence for the broader construct requires additional work to establish. The alignment between intervention and outcome measure is the first thing the apparatus surfaces, and it is the first specification you carry forward into the rest of your reading.

---

Second question. *What was the baseline?*

Every effect size is a difference. A 0.26-sigma effect is not "this intervention produces learning"; it is "students receiving this intervention performed 0.26 standard deviations higher than some comparison group performed." The comparison group defines what the effect is an effect against. Change the comparison group, and the effect changes — sometimes dramatically, sometimes in ways that reveal the intervention is doing more or less than the headline suggests.

The Khan Academy 2022 study used a specific comparison. Students categorized as "recommended usage" — 30 or more minutes per week on the platform — were compared to students categorized as "comparison" — less than 15 minutes per week on the platform. Both groups had access to Khan Academy. Both were enrolled in the same schools and districts. Both took the same MAP Growth assessment. The difference between them was not whether they had the platform but how much they used it.

This matters because the comparison is doing something specific. The study is not comparing Khan Academy users to students who received no similar intervention. It is not comparing Khan Academy users to students in a district that never adopted the platform. It is not comparing Khan Academy to a different intervention — a workbook, a tutor, a different platform — at comparable cost. The comparison is, in effect, *students who used the platform a lot versus students who used the platform a little*.

An effect of this kind is evidence for something real: that engagement with this platform, at sufficient dosage, is associated with measurable performance gains on an aligned test. It is not evidence for several other things that the citation apparatus often conflates with it. It is not evidence that providing the platform to a school produces gains, on average, across that school's students. It is not evidence that the platform is more effective than alternative interventions. It is not evidence about what happens to students who receive the platform but do not engage with it intensively.

The authors of the study do not claim any of these additional things. The apparatus of citation, which is a different apparatus from the apparatus of research, frequently acts as though they had.

*What was the baseline, and is this comparison the comparison I thought it was?*

---

Third question. *What population is this a finding about?*

Holt's "5 Percent Problem" is, at its core, a population question. The study's headline compares about 8,500 students who used the platform at the recommended level to a comparison group that also drew from the same 180,000-student pool. What happened to the other ~172,000 students — the ones who received access to the platform but did not meet the 30-minute threshold — is absent from the headline. The effect size is an effect on the high-usage subset, not on the full study population.

The authors of the study acknowledge this, if you read carefully. The analytical decision to compare high-usage students to low-usage students is transparent in the methodology. It is not hidden. But the framing of the finding in downstream citation often treats the 0.26-sigma figure as though it described what Khan Academy does, in general, when deployed to schools. It does not describe that. It describes what Khan Academy does to the subset of students who use it as recommended, compared to students in the same schools who did not.

This matters because the selection into high usage is not random. Students who reach 30 minutes per week on a mathematics practice platform are a non-random subset of the student population. They are students who — for reasons that include motivation, access to time and devices outside of school, home environments that support sustained independent work, and probably dozens of factors the study does not measure — engage with the platform intensively when it is available. The platform's effect on *these* students is what the 0.26 number reports. Whether the platform would have the same effect on the other 95%, if they could be induced to use it at the same level, is a different question. The study does not answer that question. It is not designed to answer that question.

Holt's medical analogy is useful here. A drug study that reports strong effects on the patients who actually took the drug — while 95% of the prescribed patients did not — is not a refutation that the drug works. It is a specific and narrower claim than the drug marketing implies. The drug works for those who take it. Whether it is a viable public-health intervention depends on how many will take it, and on whether the ones who do are systematically different from the ones who don't. Those are separate questions, and the drug study does not settle them.

The same logic applies to learning systems. Khan Academy's MAP Accelerator may well produce 0.26-sigma gains for students who engage with it at the recommended level. Whether this makes it a viable intervention for the populations Khan Academy markets to — particularly, the "historically under-resourced groups" the study foregrounds — depends on who in those populations actually engages at the recommended level, and on whether the 95% who do not engage are losing something the 5% who do are gaining. Again, the study does not answer these questions, and the citation of the 0.26 number in contexts where they matter does work the study does not authorize.

*What population is this a finding about, and how does this population relate to the population the finding is cited as informing?*

---

Fourth question. *What was the timescale, and would the finding survive a durability test?*

The Khan Academy study assessed outcomes in the spring of 2021, using a baseline from the fall of 2020. The measurement window was one academic year. This is a longer timescale than many EdTech efficacy studies use — some measure outcomes within weeks of the intervention — but it is still a relatively short window for claims about learning.

Here is why this matters. There is a distinction in the experimental psychology literature, developed across several decades by Robert Bjork and others, between *performance* and *learning*. Performance is what a student can demonstrate at a specific moment. Learning is what persists. The two are empirically dissociable: conditions that boost performance in the short term — massed practice, blocked scheduling, familiar testing contexts — often fail to build the durable storage that supports performance months or years later. Conditions that depress short-term performance — spaced practice, interleaved content, retrieval under difficulty — often produce the opposite pattern, with weaker immediate gains but stronger long-term retention.

Bjork's distinction is not a fringe finding. It is a foundational result in the learning sciences literature, reported consistently across many studies, and it is the basis for essentially every evidence-based recommendation for effective practice (spaced repetition, retrieval practice, desirable difficulty). Any claim about learning, as opposed to a claim about immediate performance, requires durability evidence. A test administered at the end of the intervention year, on content the intervention was designed to teach, measures performance under conditions that maximize retrieval strength. What storage strength remains three months later, or a year later, or upon entry to a more advanced course, is a separate question requiring separate measurement.

The Khan Academy study does not provide durability data. It is not designed to. The MAP Growth measurement windows used are aligned to school-year accountability cycles, which is the measurement cadence the product was built to serve. Whether the 0.26-sigma gains remain detectable at the start of the next academic year, or predict performance on subsequent-year MAP Growth tests, is not in the study. It might be addressable through Khan Academy's internal longitudinal data, but that evidence — if it exists — is not part of the finding as published.

This is not a criticism of the study. It is a description of what the study measured and what it did not. The 0.26-sigma finding is an effect on aligned performance at the end of one academic year. Whether it is also an effect on learning, in the fuller sense of durable transferable understanding, is a separate empirical question that the study does not address and that the citation apparatus often treats as settled.

*What was the timescale, and what kind of claim does a finding at this timescale support?*

---

Fifth question. *What was the cost, and against what?*

Cost almost never appears in the headline reporting of EdTech efficacy studies. It is treated, by convention, as a separate concern — a business question, a procurement question, something for purchasing departments rather than for research.

This separation, I will argue across several essais in this volume, is itself a move with epistemic consequences. For now, at the level of the apparatus, the question is simple: *what does this effect cost to produce?*

Khan Academy Districts, the institutional product referenced in the 2022 study, is priced at approximately $10 per student per year. This is, by the standards of educational interventions, remarkably low. A high-quality 1:1 tutoring program — the kind of program that produced Bloom's 2-sigma finding in 1984, which we will examine in the next essai — costs on the order of $2,000 to $4,000 per student per year in contemporary dollars. Modern research-validated tutoring programs, Saga Education and MATCH Corps among them, operate in this range. The cost ratio between high-quality human tutoring and Khan Academy's platform is approximately 200-to-1 to 400-to-1.

This matters for how the 0.26-sigma effect should be read. A 0.26-sigma effect produced at $10 per student per year is not the same finding as a 2.0-sigma effect produced at $2,000 per student per year. They are findings about different interventions under radically different cost structures. Placing them on the same axis — treating both as interventions the field could "choose between" — obscures what each is evidence for. The high-quality tutor costs more because the tutor is producing more. The platform costs less because the platform is producing less. This is not a criticism of the platform; it is a description of what a 200-to-400x cost ratio implies about comparability.

I will develop this point at length in a later essai. For now, the apparatus requires you to hold cost as a first-class analytical variable, not as a separate business concern. An effect size without its cost denominator is a number that is difficult to interpret. Two effect sizes from interventions at different costs are not two points on a single axis.

*What did this cost to produce, and what does the effect mean at this cost?*

---

Five questions. Outcome measure. Baseline. Population. Timescale. Cost.

If you ask these five questions of the Khan Academy finding, here is what the 0.26-sigma number is evidence for:

*This platform, aligned to the assessment used to measure it, used at the recommended dosage for an academic year, produces observed performance gains of about 0.26 standard deviations on students who engage with it at that dosage, at a cost of approximately $10 per student per year. Students who do not engage at the recommended dosage — approximately 95% of students provided access — do not appear in the headline comparison.*

This is a much narrower and more specific claim than the one that circulates. It is also, I want to emphasize, not a refutation of the study. The study's authors did not claim more than this. What they claimed is exactly what the number supports. The gap between the 0.26-sigma number as a research finding and the 0.26-sigma number as a cited benchmark is not the authors' fault. It is what happens to findings when they leave their original research context and enter the circulation apparatus of the field.

The apparatus this essai has built is not a refutation procedure. It is a reading procedure. When you ask the five questions of a claim, you do not end up with a verdict about whether the claim is true or false. You end up with a more specific claim than the one you started with — a claim that describes what the original finding actually supports, as distinct from what the finding is being asked to support in the context where you encountered it.

This distinction matters. It matters for the rest of this volume, because every essai that follows will apply these same five questions to other findings, and the cumulative pattern — what the findings actually support, compared with what they are cited as supporting — is the subject of the book. But it matters more, I think, for what you carry out of the book afterward. The next time you encounter an efficacy claim about a learning system — in a conference talk, a foundation announcement, an investor deck, a journalism piece, a Slack thread — you can ask these five questions. You do not need the study to have been flagged as suspect. You do not need to be an expert in the platform or the population. You need only to ask what the claim actually supports, and then to compare that to what the claim is being cited to support in the context where you encountered it. The gap between those two things is where the apparatus of the field does its work.

---

There is one more move I need to name before this essai closes.

Throughout the five questions, I have been pointing at something that does not quite have a name in most casual discussions of efficacy research. I want to give it a name now, because this is the concept the rest of the volume depends on.

Every efficacy claim involves three things: a measurement, a construct, and an asserted relationship between them. The measurement is what the researchers actually did — the test administered, the scores computed, the conditions compared. The construct is what the measurement is meant to be evidence for — *learning*, *mastery*, *effectiveness*, *understanding*, whatever category the claim invokes. The asserted relationship is the claim that the measurement indexes the construct adequately to license the uses the claim is put to.

The apparatus of the five questions surfaces, in each of its moves, some aspect of this three-part structure. The outcome-measure question asks what the measurement is, specifically. The baseline question asks what the measurement is measured against. The population question asks who the measurement is a measurement of. The timescale question asks over what time the measurement is valid. The cost question asks what resources were required to produce the measurement.

What all five questions are circling is the relationship between the measurement and the construct. The 0.26-sigma number is a specific measurement under specific conditions. What it is claimed as evidence for — that Khan Academy works, that the platform helps students learn mathematics, that adaptive learning is effective — are constructs the measurement may index, may partially index, or may not index at all, depending on specifics the five questions surface.

The distinction between what is measured and what that measurement is a proxy for is the central analytical tool of this volume. I will return to it across every essai. In the thirteenth essai, which is the book's deepest examination of the apparatus we are beginning here to build, I will argue that the gap between measurement and construct is the single most important observation the book makes. For now, in this essai, I am naming it so you have the vocabulary to carry forward.

A measurement is not a construct. The measurement is evidence for the construct only to the degree that the relationship between them has been established. Across most of the efficacy claims this volume examines — and, I will argue, across most of the efficacy claims the learning-systems field has produced across six decades — the relationship between measurement and construct has been assumed rather than established. That assumption is the thing the apparatus asks you to notice.

---

Here is what I want you to do with this essai.

This coming week, you will encounter an efficacy claim about a learning system. It may arrive in an article, a pitch deck, a journalism piece, a Slack discussion, a conference talk. It may be about Khan Academy, or about a competitor, or about something adjacent — a tutoring program, a curriculum intervention, a reading platform, an AI tool. When it arrives, try to hold it before you respond to it. Not for long; just for a moment. And ask the five questions.

What was measured, and was the measurement aligned to the intervention?
What was the baseline, and is the comparison the comparison I thought it was?
What population is this a finding about, and how does that population relate to the population the finding is being cited as informing?
What was the timescale, and what kind of claim does a finding at this timescale support?
What did this cost to produce, and what does the effect mean at this cost?

You may not be able to answer every question from the information in front of you. That itself is information. When a claim is circulated in a form that makes the five questions unanswerable, the gap between the claim and what the claim can support is probably large. Claims that can survive the five questions tend to be specific. Claims that cannot tend to be sweeping.

Then, once you have held the five questions against the claim, notice the gap between what the finding supports and what the finding is being cited as supporting in the context where you encountered it. That gap is where the apparatus of the field does its work. It is, I am arguing, where the apparatus of the field has been doing its work for six decades. The next essai will show how old this pattern is, and the essais after that will show how consistently it has reproduced itself across every generation of learning technology since the teaching machines of the 1950s.

The apparatus you now have is portable. You can carry it into every reading you do across the rest of this book. You can carry it into the next Tuesday morning when someone emails you a new efficacy study and asks what you think. You can carry it into the next funding meeting where a sigma number is invoked at you. You do not need me to tell you what to make of any specific claim. You can make of it what the five questions reveal.

That is what this essai was for.

The next essai takes the apparatus we have just built and points it at history. The measurement habits the five questions are surfacing are not new. They were not invented in 2022 by Khan Academy, or in 2024 by Harvard physics researchers, or in the current AI moment by any specific company. They were inherited, with remarkably little revision, from research conducted in the 1950s on pigeons pecking keys in Skinner's laboratory — research that established the measurement apparatus the field still uses, several generations of technology later. If the problem the first essai named is real, and the apparatus the second essai has built is sound, then the next essai will show that both are older than most of the technologies this book examines. The third essai begins there.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai is load-bearing for the volume. If the apparatus does not land here, the essais that follow cannot do the work they are meant to do. I want to name specifically what I am unsure about in how I have built it.

I am not sure I have been entirely fair to the Khan Academy 2022 study. The authors of that study, Grimaldi, Weatherholtz, and Hill, did careful methodological work, acknowledged the quasi-experimental nature of their design, and framed their findings in scope-appropriate ways in the paper itself. The gap I have described — between what the finding supports and what the finding is cited as supporting — is not their gap. It is a gap that emerges when the finding enters the citation apparatus of the field. A reader might reasonably ask whether I have been too quick to use a specific study as a case when the problem I am examining is larger than the study. The response is that I have tried to name, throughout, that the authors did not claim more than their finding supports. If the essai still reads as though I am criticizing the authors rather than the apparatus, it has drifted. Tell me where.

I am not sure the five questions are the right five, or the right number. They are the questions that, when applied to the Khan Academy study, surface the specific incommensurabilities I wanted you to see. Other efficacy claims might be better read through slightly different questions — about randomization, about statistical power, about pre-registration, about fidelity of implementation. A reader more expert in specific methodological traditions than I am might reasonably propose alternative apparatuses. I have chosen these five because they are the ones that recur across the cases this volume examines. But the apparatus is not sacred. It is an attempt at a useful reading tool, not a complete methodological framework.

I am not sure I have done enough with Laurence Holt's analysis. Holt's "5 Percent Problem" framing is sharp, well-sourced, and does analytical work this essai has borrowed from. A fuller treatment of Holt — the studies he examines beyond Khan Academy, the pattern he documents across multiple platforms, the response (or lack thereof) from the organizations whose studies he critiques — would be its own essai. I have used Holt as a case here, which reduces his analysis to a single illustration. A reader who finds Holt's piece indispensable and thinks it deserves more than I have given it here should push on that. I am inclined to agree.

Finally, I am not sure the apparatus I have built here is enough to carry the rest of the book. It is a reading tool, not a complete framework for evaluating learning-systems research. The essais that follow will add to it — cost as an epistemic variable receives its own essai; the measure-vs-construct distinction receives its own essai; the patterns the field displays when tools underperform receive their own essai. The apparatus grows across the volume. What I have installed here is the foundation. Whether it bears the weight of what follows will become clear, essai by essai.

The comments on this essai's Substack page are the simplest place to tell me where this apparatus falls short. In the quarterly Kindle compilation, where I incorporate the series' discussion into the record, any pushback from this essai will be visible — in marginal corrections, in footnote commentary, in whatever form the responses call for. If the apparatus fails for you, the entire book may fail. I want to know before the later essais depend on it.

The next essai examines how old the problem the apparatus surfaces actually is. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 2. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 26–28 pages. Word count: approximately 6,200.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**Block structure.** The draft implements the five-block structure specified at /c1 with one adjustment: Blocks B, C, D, E in the spec mapped to outcome-measure, baseline/5-percent-problem, timescale/cost/proxy, and invitation. I restructured slightly so that each of the five questions gets its own named block section, which produces six internal movements rather than five but clarifies the apparatus more cleanly. The spec's Block C (the 5-percent problem) is now integrated into the population question block, which I think reads better because the 5-percent problem *is* a population question.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 6,200 words, projecting to 26-28 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — within the 25-30 page range specified, slightly under the 28-page target. The spec flagged that Essai 2 needs to be short enough to re-read. I have held it tighter than the ceiling deliberately for this reason. If the revision pass wants expansion, the natural place is the timescale question (currently shortest of the five), which could take a fuller treatment of Bjork's distinction between storage and retrieval strength.

**Apparatus by enactment.** The specification asked that the five questions emerge from the close reading rather than being presented as a framework upfront. The draft does this — the questions appear in order, each arising from what the analysis is doing at that moment, and are only named as a set at the end. The first sentence of each block states the question, which anchors the structure for the reader without turning it into a checklist.

**The proxy-vs-construct distinction.** The spec required this distinction to be installed as an active category by the end of the essai. The draft does this in the "one more move" block near the closing. I kept this brief deliberately — the thirteenth essai does the deep work on measurement vs. construct, and Essai 2 should install the vocabulary without exhausting the concept.

**Specific concerns about the draft.**

- The medical analogy from Holt is quoted directly. It works rhetorically but a reader could feel the essai is leaning on Holt's frame rather than building its own. The draft's response is to use Holt as a cited source for the analogy and to carry the population analysis into specific educational terms afterward, which I think distinguishes the essai's voice from Holt's. A revision pass might want to foreground this distinction more explicitly.

- The Bjork reference in the timescale section is brief. The essai gestures at storage-vs-retrieval without unpacking it. This is deliberate — the thirteenth essai does more with Bjork — but a reader encountering the distinction for the first time may want slightly more here. One additional sentence on what the distinction implies about what Khan Academy's measurement can and cannot support would help.

- The cost section promises "a later essai" where cost is developed more fully. This is Essai 12. The forward reference is appropriate in the Kindle edition; the Substack version will need the reference removed or rephrased since readers encounter essais as they drop.

- The invitational closing block lands the three elements (finding, named uncertainty, explicit invitation) but the "named uncertainty" section is longer than in Essai 1 (four paragraphs versus three). This may be appropriate for a load-bearing essai where more of the uncertainty is structural. A revision pass can tighten if it reads as overextended.

**What the draft does particularly well.** The opening holds the 0.26 number on the page without immediately attacking it, the way the spec required. The authors are named and credited — Grimaldi, Weatherholtz, and Hill get their credit as careful researchers before the essai moves to examination. Holt's "5 Percent Problem" framing is brought in specifically with its Fall 2024 *Education Next* publication and Holt's role at the XQ Institute. The essai ends on the structural observation about measurement-vs-construct that the rest of the book will develop, without preempting the thirteenth essai's fuller treatment.

**What I am least sure about.** The closing section's bridge to Essai 3 (the teaching-machine history) commits to a specific follow-up claim — that the measurement apparatus is inherited from 1950s behaviorism. This is what the spec calls for, and I think it is right, but the bridge is assertive. A reader who has not yet read Essai 3 has to trust this framing. The invitational register of the essai does partially soften this assertion, but a revision pass should check whether the bridge can be slightly more provisional without losing the spiral's momentum.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On the Afterlife of the Teaching Machine

*Essai 3*

---

In 1954, Burrhus Frederic Skinner gave a talk at the University of Pittsburgh in which he described his recent observation of his own daughter Deborah's fourth-grade arithmetic class. What he had seen, he reported, had come as a "great shock." The teacher was unable to provide what Skinner would later call the "contingencies of reinforcement" — the immediate, precise feedback that his decades of work on operant conditioning had established as the mechanism by which behavior is shaped. Students performed work, they waited, the work was collected, and perhaps the next day or several days later they received back a paper with marks on it. By the time the marks arrived, the behavior they were supposed to reinforce had moved on. The contingencies were broken. Learning, Skinner believed, was being left to chance in the one institution society most required to be systematic about it.

Skinner's response to this observation became, within four years, the teaching machine — a device that presented instructional material one frame at a time, required the student to respond, and provided immediate verification of whether the response was correct. In his 1958 paper in *Science* titled simply "Teaching Machines," Skinner laid out both the theoretical basis for the device and the evidence he had accumulated that it worked. The paper is one of the most cited documents in the history of educational technology. It also, I want to argue, initiated the specific measurement apparatus — the specific choices about what to measure and what to ignore — that educational technology efficacy research has inherited, with remarkably little revision, across six subsequent decades and at least four distinct waves of technological change.

This essai traces that inheritance. It asks what Skinner's teaching machine was designed to measure, what it was not designed to measure, and how these specific choices persisted through Patrick Suppes's 1960s computer-assisted instruction, through John Anderson and Kenneth Koedinger's 1990s Cognitive Tutor, through Knewton and DreamBox and i-Ready in the 2010s, and into the contemporary AI-tutor wave — the Khanmigo and Kestin and Eedi and Rori studies the book's later essais examine in detail. The apparatus, I want to show, has remained stable. The technologies that implement it have changed profoundly. But what counts as evidence that a learning-systems technology works — the specific measurements that the field accepts as demonstrating efficacy — has been largely inherited rather than revised across six decades.

At the end of the essai I will read one specific contemporary study carefully, applying the apparatus this essai has just surfaced — a 2021 *Foreign Language Annals* paper by Xiangying Jiang, Joseph Rollinson, Luke Plonsky, Erin Gustafson, and Bozena Pajak, titled *Evaluating the reading and listening outcomes of beginning-level Duolingo courses*. Four of the five authors were employed by Duolingo at the time of publication. The study is peer-reviewed, methodologically careful by contemporary conventions, and reaches conclusions about Duolingo's effectiveness that are cited in Duolingo's own marketing materials. It is also, I will argue, a study whose measurement choices are traceable, line by line, to Skinner's 1958 paper. What the study measures is what Skinner's apparatus measures. What the study does not measure is what Skinner's apparatus did not measure. The specific inheritance the essai documents is still active, still shaping what the field reports as evidence that educational technology works.

I want to be specific about what this essai is and is not doing. It is not arguing that Skinner was wrong, that Suppes was wrong, that Anderson was wrong, that the researchers doing EdTech efficacy work in the 2020s are wrong. Each generation of researchers has been serious, methodologically competent, and working in good faith within the measurement traditions available to them. The argument is not that individual researchers have been careless. The argument is that the *apparatus itself* — the set of measurement choices that have counted as evidence of learning — has been inherited rather than examined, and that the inheritance pattern has consequences the field has not fully reckoned with. Naming the apparatus is not condemning the workers. The essai is a trial of the apparatus, not of the people operating it.

---

Begin with Skinner's 1958 paper, which is more measured than its reputation suggests.

Skinner's *Teaching Machines* in *Science* describes the specific mechanism of the device in detail. A student sits at the machine. A frame — a small amount of instructional material with one or more blanks — is presented through a window. The student writes a response in an adjacent panel. The student operates a lever. The machine reveals whether the response was correct. If correct, the student advances to the next frame. If incorrect, the student may be asked to retry, or the material may be represented in a slightly different form.

What does the machine measure?

The 1958 paper is explicit. The machine measures the accuracy of each individual response, frame by frame. It measures the response time — how long the student took to produce the response. It measures the progression — how many frames the student has completed, at what rate. It measures the error pattern — which frames produce incorrect responses, at what points in the sequence, with what distribution. These measurements are not incidental to Skinner's design; they are the point of the design. Skinner's behavioral science, across decades of laboratory work, had established that the precise recording of response patterns was the foundation of understanding how behavior is shaped. The teaching machine extended this laboratory precision into the educational context. What the machine records is what Skinner's science believed was relevant about learning.

What does the machine *not* measure?

This is the question that matters for the inheritance argument, and it is the question Skinner's 1958 paper is largely silent on. The machine does not measure whether the student retains the material two weeks, two months, or two years after the machine is removed. It does not measure whether the student can apply what was learned in contexts that differ from the machine's frame-by-frame presentation format. It does not measure whether the student could have acquired the same capability more efficiently, or more durably, or at lower cost, through alternative means. It does not measure what the student can do with the material beyond producing the specific responses the machine was designed to elicit. These non-measurements are not oversights. They reflect the commitments of Skinner's behavioral framework: behavior is the object of study, immediate contingencies are the mechanism, and generalization is expected to follow naturally from properly shaped behavior without requiring separate empirical verification.

Skinner's specific evidence for the teaching machine's effectiveness, in the 1958 paper, follows directly from these measurement choices. He describes the machine's ability to produce high accuracy rates on the frames it presents. He describes the rate at which students progress through the material. He describes the reduction in teacher time required to grade student work. These are real achievements, documented honestly within the terms Skinner established. The paper's effectiveness claims are not exaggerated; they are precise. What is significant is what the precision does and does not encompass. The machine is effective at producing the specific behaviors the machine was designed to record. Whether the behaviors persist, transfer, or represent what the field would later call *learning* in the deeper sense Ann Brown and the learning sciences came to articulate, Skinner does not address — because his framework did not require him to.

I want to name a specific discipline here because it matters for how the essai reads Skinner and his successors. The question is not whether Skinner should have measured differently. The question is whether the measurement choices he made were ever systematically re-examined by the researchers who inherited his apparatus. My argument across the rest of this essai is that they were not. The apparatus was inherited as if its choices were neutral rather than theoretical — as if measuring accuracy-per-frame-at-immediate-timescale were simply what evidence-of-learning looked like, rather than one specific operationalization that Skinner's behavioral science had specifically justified. The inheritance flattened the theoretical commitments into methodological conventions. The conventions persisted long after the behavioral framework that justified them was largely abandoned.

---

Move to Patrick Suppes.

In 1966, Patrick Suppes at Stanford published *The Uses of Computers in Education* in *Scientific American*. The paper's opening framing remains famous: computers, Suppes wrote, would allow "millions of schoolchildren" to have access to the "personal services of a tutor as well-informed and responsive as Aristotle." This is the framing that the Bloom 2-sigma framing of 1984 would later rhyme with, and that the Khan Academy 2023 framing of AI tutoring would rhyme with again.

What Suppes actually built at Stanford, in collaboration with Richard Atkinson, was a computer-assisted instruction system for mathematics drill. The system presented arithmetic problems to elementary students. Students typed answers. The computer verified correctness and adjusted the difficulty of subsequent problems based on performance. This was, in important respects, the teaching machine translated into 1960s mainframe technology.

What did the Suppes system measure?

The 1966 paper documents Suppes's measurements with characteristic clarity. The system recorded the proportion of correct responses for each student across each problem type. Arithmetic accuracy rates improved from roughly 53 percent at baseline to over 90 percent after a period of drill. The system recorded response time — the average time required to produce an answer — and documented its reduction from 630 seconds to 279 seconds over the course of a year. The system recorded item-level difficulty patterns, identifying which specific problem types were "troublesome." The system recorded time-outs — instances where students failed to respond within a set limit. These are the measurements Suppes's paper reports as evidence that the system worked.

The apparatus is unmistakable. Accuracy per item, time per response, progression, error patterns. These are Skinner's measurements, with the technology shifted from mechanical device to computer mainframe. Suppes did not inherit Skinner's behavioral framework in the theoretical sense; Suppes was interested in mathematical models of learning and in the computer as a medium for individualized instruction. But Suppes inherited Skinner's measurement apparatus wholesale. What counted as evidence of the system's effectiveness was exactly what had counted as evidence of the teaching machine's effectiveness — the specific behaviors the system was designed to shape and record.

And what did Suppes not measure? Again, the list is consistent. No delayed post-testing of whether the arithmetic facility persisted once students stopped using the system. No transfer testing to determine whether the improved arithmetic performance translated to mathematical reasoning in other contexts. No comparison to alternative instructional approaches at matched cost. No examination of whether the 90 percent accuracy rate reflected deep understanding or shallow pattern-matching on the specific item formats the system presented. Suppes's paper is internally consistent and methodologically careful within its own terms. What it inherits without examination is the question of whether the terms themselves are the right terms.

PLATO, the other major 1960s CAI system developed at the University of Illinois by Donald Bitzer and colleagues, extended the same apparatus to a wider range of subjects and added sophisticated graphical capabilities. The PLATO efficacy literature from the late 1960s and 1970s inherits the same measurement pattern: accuracy rates, completion rates, time-on-task, performance on aligned post-tests. The innovation is in the technology. The continuity is in what the technology measures.

---

Skip to Anderson's Cognitive Tutor, which the book's seventh essai examined at length.

John Anderson, Albert Corbett, Kenneth Koedinger, and Ray Pelletier's 1995 *Journal of the Learning Sciences* paper *Cognitive Tutors: Lessons Learned* represents, in one sense, a profound theoretical shift from Skinner and Suppes. Cognitive Tutor was built on the ACT-R theory of cognition — a cognitive-psychological framework that treated learning as the acquisition of production rules (if-then pairings connecting goal states to actions) rather than as the shaping of behavior through reinforcement. The theoretical language moved from reinforcement to cognition, from behavior to knowledge, from stimulus-response to information-processing.

And yet.

What did Cognitive Tutor measure?

The 1995 paper documents the measurement apparatus with precision. The tutor recorded step-level correctness — whether each student action matched one of the production rules the cognitive model had identified as correct. It recorded time per step. It recorded hint requests and hint-level utilization. It recorded error patterns and their correspondence to stored "buggy" productions representing common misconceptions. It recorded estimated mastery of each production rule through a Bayesian knowledge-tracing algorithm. These are the measurements that determined when a student was allowed to advance to new material.

The theoretical framework changed. The units changed from frames to production rules. The statistical machinery became substantially more sophisticated. But the shape of the apparatus — what the tutor attended to as evidence of learning — is the shape Skinner specified. Step-level correctness, time per step, progression through atomized units, error patterns that trigger remediation. These are the same measurement choices, renamed and retheorized, operating on finer-grained units of analysis. Cognitive Tutor measured what the teaching machine measured, at higher resolution, within a more sophisticated theoretical framework, without departing from the apparatus's fundamental commitments.

Anderson and colleagues were, I want to emphasize, transparent about what their system measured and did not measure. The 1995 paper is a remarkable document of methodological honesty. The authors acknowledge that transfer beyond the tutor environment depends on the match between the tutor's interface and the transfer environment — a direct quotation from the paper reads that "students... display transfer... to the degree that they can map the tutor environment into the test environment." This is an acknowledgment that the tutor's effectiveness, as measured by aligned post-tests, does not necessarily generalize to contexts that differ from the tutor's specific presentation format. The authors also note that their system works better when presented to students as a non-human tool rather than as an emulation of a human tutor — an acknowledgment that what the tutor does is different from what human tutoring involves, a distinction the seventh essai of this volume examined in detail.

This methodological transparency is what makes the inheritance pattern visible rather than obscured. Anderson and colleagues did not hide what their system measured and what it did not. They named the limits of aligned-outcome transfer explicitly in their own 1995 paper. The inheritance pattern is not that researchers misrepresented their work; it is that the measurement conventions persisted even when the researchers themselves acknowledged their limits. A subsequent literature that cited Cognitive Tutor's effect sizes without preserving Anderson's own caveats was doing something the original paper did not do. The apparatus inherits even what its originators flagged as provisional.

Art Graesser's AutoTutor, developed at the University of Memphis in the late 1990s and early 2000s, introduced natural-language dialogue into the intelligent-tutoring-system tradition. The technological shift was substantial; the measurement shift was modest. AutoTutor's efficacy evidence rested on accuracy of student responses within the dialogue, coverage of expected content elements in student explanations, and post-test performance on assessments aligned with the dialogue topics. The measurement apparatus was inherited. The technology enabled a different *kind* of interaction — conversational rather than problem-solving — but the question of what counted as evidence that the interaction had produced learning remained anchored in aligned-outcome performance at immediate timescale.

---

The commercial adaptive-learning era of the 2010s — Knewton, DreamBox Learning, i-Ready, ALEKS, and their many imitators — represents the point at which the inheritance became industrial.

Knewton, led by José Ferreira during its 2012-2015 peak of public prominence, positioned itself as a platform that could use machine learning and large-scale behavioral data to personalize instruction at the sub-item level. Ferreira's public framing invoked the Suppes promise — individualized instruction at scale — in the language of twenty-first-century data science. The platform processed millions of interactions across student populations to identify patterns and optimize instructional sequences. The measurement apparatus, in the public claims, was usage data: how students interacted with the platform, what problems they attempted, what hints they took, how their patterns of engagement correlated with eventual performance. Rigorous independent efficacy research on Knewton was notably sparse during the period of its public prominence — a fact that contributed to the eighth essai's discussion of adaptive learning's evaluation thinness.

DreamBox Learning, focused on elementary mathematics, became one of the more researched adaptive platforms, largely through the work of the Harvard Center for Education Policy Research. A 2016 CEPR study examined the relationship between DreamBox usage and student achievement, measured by performance on the NWEA MAP (Measures of Academic Progress) test and state standardized assessments. The study reported that students at the median would gain 1.4 to 3.9 percentile points on the MAP for approximately 7 to 8 hours of DreamBox usage. The population spanned thousands of students in the Howard County Public School System and Rocketship Education; the duration spanned one to two school years.

The measurement apparatus is recognizable. The outcome measures are aligned — not with DreamBox's specific internal curriculum, but with standardized tests that cover mathematics in ways broadly consistent with what DreamBox teaches. The input measure is usage — time on task, lessons completed. The relationship between input and outcome is statistical, across large populations. The theoretical framework is loose. What has changed from Skinner and Suppes and Cognitive Tutor is the scale of the data and the statistical machinery for handling it. What has remained stable is the fundamental question: does the system, as measured by performance on aligned assessments at the end of the instructional period, correlate with gains compared to non-users?

The CEPR study itself includes a specific acknowledgment that, I want to note, is methodologically honest. The researchers observe that DreamBox usage may "partially reflect students' motivation levels" — which means the correlation between usage and achievement may reflect that motivated students both use DreamBox more and learn more, regardless of DreamBox's instructional contribution. This is the same class of acknowledgment Anderson and colleagues made about Cognitive Tutor's transfer limits. The researchers know the limits of what their apparatus can establish. The limits are acknowledged in the paper. The subsequent citations of the paper often do not preserve the acknowledgments.

i-Ready, produced by Curriculum Associates, operates with similar methodological choices and a characteristic internal feature worth naming. The platform's efficacy research typically demonstrates that students who achieve "usage fidelity" — meeting recommended minutes per week of engagement — show higher scores on the i-Ready Diagnostic. The i-Ready Diagnostic is itself calibrated to predict state test performance. This creates what one observer has called a circular measurement loop: the system measures how well students learn to do well on the assessment the system itself provides, with the assessment designed to predict the external state test that is the ostensible ultimate standard. The apparatus has become recursive. The inheritance from Skinner — accuracy on aligned items — has been institutionalized as the industry's measurement standard, with the alignment now engineered at the product-design level rather than simply present as a natural feature of drill-and-practice.

ALEKS, which the book's seventh essai discussed, inherits the apparatus with its own theoretical framing through Knowledge Space Theory. Efficacy claims rest on performance within the system's own internal knowledge mapping, supplemented by aligned post-tests that measure progression through the curricular content the system teaches. The pattern is consistent.

---

The current AI-tutor wave — the essais Ten and Eleven examine in detail — inherits the apparatus in its turn. I will be brief here because the detailed examinations live elsewhere in the volume.

Khanmigo's evaluation evidence, as the eleventh essai documented, has rested largely on engagement data and on performance within Khan Academy's own internal assessment structures. What has been measured at scale is usage patterns; what has been claimed is educational transformation; what has not been established at the level of rigorous efficacy research is learning gains on independent standardized measures at delayed timescales with cost-inclusive reporting. The apparatus's characteristic gaps are visible.

Kestin et al. 2024's Harvard physics study, as the tenth essai documented in detail, reported effect sizes of 0.73 to 1.3 sigma on researcher-designed post-tests covering the specific content (surface tension and fluid flow) that the two-hour intervention taught. The timescale from intervention to assessment was short. The baseline was a single-session active-learning classroom. The outcome measure was aligned with the intervention. The apparatus is inherited.

Eedi with Google Research's 2025 study, as the tenth essai documented, introduced an important measurement advance — transfer testing on novel problems from subsequent topics. This is a genuine departure from the aligned-outcome convention. The study's N of 165 and single-term duration remain short by comparison to what durability research would require, but the outcome measure itself represents a methodological step that the book's later essais credit specifically. The Eedi study is the current literature's single strongest example of what measurement-apparatus revision, rather than inheritance, would look like.

Rori in Ghana, as the tenth essai documented, used external curriculum-aligned math assessment (from the Global Proficiency Framework) over an eight-month period — a meaningfully longer timescale than most contemporary AI-tutor studies, with an outcome measure that was not designed by the intervention's developers. The apparatus is partially reformed. The cost reporting — $5 per student per year — represents another methodological advance that the book's twelfth essai treats as exemplary.

Across the wave, measurement-apparatus inheritance is the dominant pattern. Measurement-apparatus revision exists in specific studies at specific points, as credits to the researchers who have chosen to work against the inherited conventions. The exceptions do not yet constitute a new standard for the field.

---

Now Jiang et al. 2021.

The paper is *Evaluating the reading and listening outcomes of beginning-level Duolingo courses*, published in *Foreign Language Annals*, volume 54, issue 4, in 2021. The authors are Xiangying Jiang, Joseph Rollinson, Luke Plonsky, Erin Gustafson, and Bozena Pajak. Four of the five authors were employed by Duolingo at the time of publication; the fifth, Luke Plonsky, is an academic researcher at Northern Arizona University specializing in applied linguistics. The paper is peer-reviewed in a journal that is among the more established in foreign-language education research.

The research question the paper asks is this: what ACTFL (American Council on the Teaching of Foreign Languages) proficiency levels do adult Duolingo learners achieve in listening and reading when they complete the beginning-level (A2) courses? This is a specific operationalization: what level of performance, on a specific external assessment, do people who finish a specific subset of Duolingo's content reach?

The population is 225 adults in the United States — 135 studying Spanish, 90 studying French. The sample was drawn from Duolingo's user base. Participants were required to have "little to no prior proficiency" in their target language, to be using Duolingo as their "only learning tool," and — this is the consequential selection criterion — to have completed the beginning-level course content (Unit 4, the end of what Duolingo classifies as A2). The sample, as reported in the paper, skewed toward highly educated Caucasian Americans with bachelor's or master's degrees.

The outcome measure is the STAMP 4S test — the Standards-based Measurement of Proficiency assessment from Avant Assessment. The reading and listening sections of the STAMP 4S each consist of 30 multiple-choice items. The assessment covers reading and listening proficiency in the target language. The post-test was administered immediately after learners completed the beginning-level content.

The paper's reported finding is that the Duolingo learners reached the ACTFL Intermediate Low level in reading and the Novice High level in listening — levels the paper characterizes as "comparable with those of university students at the end of the fourth semester" of college-level language study.

I want to apply the measurement apparatus to this study and see what the apparatus reveals.

First, the outcome measure. The STAMP 4S is an external assessment — not designed by Duolingo, not specifically aligned to Duolingo's curriculum.
