This is a methodological improvement over the purely internal-assessment pattern common in EdTech efficacy research. The assessment does cover reading and listening comprehension broadly rather than Duolingo's specific content. However, reading and listening are also the specific modalities that Duolingo's interface is designed around — multiple-choice comprehension items, translation tasks, listening exercises with visual or multiple-choice responses. Speaking and writing, which are modalities Duolingo's app-based format supports weakly, are explicitly excluded from the study. The measurement is therefore aligned with the specific subset of language proficiency that Duolingo's interface is engineered to build. The STAMP 4S is external; the choice to measure only reading and listening makes the assessment narrower in a way that favors Duolingo's specific strengths.

Second, the timescale. The post-test is administered immediately after completion of the beginning-level content. There is no delayed assessment at three months, six months, or one year after course completion. The Bjork distinction between retrieval strength (immediate accessibility) and storage strength (durable retention) is directly relevant here: the STAMP 4S scores reflect what Duolingo users can do at the moment they finish the course, not what they can do later. Whether the Intermediate Low reading proficiency persists six months after the user stops using Duolingo is not tested. This is the same timescale pattern the apparatus has inherited across six decades.

Third, the selection criterion. The study assessed only learners who *completed* the beginning-level content. Duolingo's attrition rates, like those of most app-based learning platforms, are substantial — most users who download the app never reach the end of the beginning-level content. The population measured is therefore the "survivors" of Duolingo's reinforcement structure. What 100 people who completed the course achieved is different from what 100 people who started it achieved. The paper is transparent about this selection criterion, but the framing of the findings — *Duolingo users reach Intermediate Low in reading* — is routinely re-expressed in Duolingo's marketing materials as *Duolingo is as effective as four semesters of college Spanish*, without preserving the completion-threshold restriction. The effective claim elides the selection.

Fourth, the baseline. The comparison is historical — "comparable with those of university students at the end of the fourth semester." There is no contemporaneous control group of comparable students using a different learning approach. The comparison is between Duolingo completers' immediate post-test scores and historical averages from a different population under different conditions. This is a defensible approach given the study's observational design, but it is not an experimental comparison. The *comparable to four semesters of college* framing treats the two groups as if their outcomes had been measured under equivalent conditions, which they have not.

Fifth, the cost. The study does not report per-student cost or time-cost. Duolingo is free to users at its base tier, which has specific rhetorical value — the framing is that free app is comparable to paid college course — but the comparison elides the substantial time investment Duolingo users put in (typical completion times run hundreds of hours), the forgone opportunity cost of that time, and the question of what the same time investment in a structurally different approach (human-tutored instruction, immersion experience, structured self-study with textbook and audio materials) would produce. The cost denominator, as the twelfth essai of this volume argued, is constitutive of what a comparative claim supports. Its absence here is characteristic of the apparatus.

Sixth, the author affiliations. Four of the five authors were Duolingo employees at the time of publication. This is not a disqualifying fact — industry-funded research can be rigorous, and the paper does note the affiliations transparently. It is a contextualizing fact. When a study of a product is authored primarily by the product's developers, the specific measurement choices they make — what to measure, what to exclude, what comparisons to draw — warrant attention. Which outcome modalities were measured (reading and listening — Duolingo's strengths) and which were not (speaking and writing — Duolingo's weaknesses) reflects choices that a non-affiliated research team might have made differently.

What emerges from applying the apparatus to Jiang et al. 2021 is not that the study is fraudulent. The study is methodologically careful within its own terms. What emerges is that the specific choices the study makes about what to measure, how, over what timescale, with what comparison, at what cost — these choices are exactly the choices Skinner's teaching machine made in 1958, refreshed across six intervening decades of technological change. Aligned outcome measure. Short timescale. Selection of successful users as the study population. Comparison to a baseline that elides context. No cost denominator. The apparatus has persisted. The technology has changed. What counts as evidence that the technology works has been inherited rather than examined.

---

There have been alternatives across the same six decades. The apparatus persisted in spite of them, not in the absence of them.

Edward Thorndike's work on transfer of learning, published in papers in 1906 and 1924, established that improvement in one mental function rarely produces general improvement in others unless the two share "identical elements." Thorndike's methodological implication was that learning gains claimed by any specific instructional intervention must be tested outside the conditions of the intervention — in contexts structurally different from the training environment — to establish what the training has actually produced. Transfer testing, as Thorndike conceived it over a century ago, has existed as an available methodology for the entire history of educational technology efficacy research. It has been used occasionally, praised routinely, and treated as impractical or aspirational rather than as baseline standard. The Eedi 2025 study's inclusion of transfer testing is a credit to its researchers in part because Thorndike's tradition has remained largely unadopted in the broader EdTech efficacy literature.

Robert and Elizabeth Bjork's *New Theory of Disuse*, developed through the 1980s and codified in the 1992 Bjork and Bjork chapter, established the empirical distinction between retrieval strength (current performance) and storage strength (durable retention). The Bjorks' research program demonstrated, repeatedly, that conditions maximizing immediate performance (massed practice, aligned testing, minimal difficulty) could actively impair long-term storage, while conditions introducing what they called "desirable difficulties" (spaced practice, interleaving, retrieval practice) could depress immediate performance while building substantially more durable learning. The methodological implication is unmistakable: immediate post-test performance is at best an incomplete, at worst a misleading, measure of what a student has learned. Delayed post-testing is required to distinguish performance from learning. The Bjork distinction has been canonical in the learning sciences for over three decades. Its adoption in educational technology efficacy research has been sporadic.

John Bransford, Ann Brown, and Rodney Cocking's *How People Learn*, the 1999 National Academies consensus volume, argued explicitly that assessment should "tap understanding rather than merely the ability to repeat facts" and advocated for measurement approaches that capture deep conceptual learning rather than surface-level performance. The volume synthesized two decades of learning-sciences research into a clear methodological challenge to the aligned-outcome convention. The challenge was widely read, widely cited, and only narrowly operationalized in efficacy research practice.

Samuel Messick's *Validity* chapter in the 1989 edition of *Educational Measurement* — which the thirteenth essai of this volume examines in detail — provided the most sophisticated framework available for thinking about what a test score actually supports as an inference about learning. Messick's unified theory of validity specified that a test score's interpretation requires examination of construct-relevant versus construct-irrelevant variance, construct under-representation, and the consequences of the test's use. Messick's framework, applied rigorously, would require EdTech efficacy research to examine what its outcome measures actually index rather than assuming that performance-on-aligned-items equals evidence-of-learning. The framework has been standard in measurement theory for over three decades. Its rigorous application in educational technology efficacy has been partial at best.

These alternatives have not been hidden. They are taught in graduate programs in educational psychology, measurement, and the learning sciences. They are cited in methods sections of EdTech efficacy papers. What has not happened, across six decades of technology change, is their adoption as the standard apparatus of the field. The inherited apparatus — aligned outcomes at immediate timescale, with selection of engaged users, against historical or minimal baselines, without cost denomination — has remained dominant. The alternatives have remained alternative.

---

I want to step back and name what this pattern means.

The measurement apparatus of the learning-systems field is not a neutral product of scientific progress. It is a specific theoretical commitment — Skinner's, initially — that has been inherited across six decades of technological change, carrying specific assumptions about what counts as evidence of learning into contexts whose theoretical frameworks have long since moved beyond Skinner's behaviorism. What this means is that when contemporary AI-tutor research reports that an intervention produced a 0.73 sigma effect on a researcher-designed post-test aligned with the intervention content after a short intervention window, with cost unreported, the finding does specific work that is traceable in structure and kind to Skinner's 1958 claims about the teaching machine. The technology has become incomprehensibly more powerful. The apparatus of evidence has changed far less.

The question the essai invites you to carry forward is simple. When you encounter an educational technology efficacy claim — an advertisement from a company, a press release from a university, a peer-reviewed paper in a journal — apply the apparatus's characteristic questions. What did the outcome measure? Was it aligned with what the intervention taught? Over what timescale from intervention to assessment? Against what baseline? With what population selection? At what cost? If the answers match the Skinnerian pattern — aligned outcome, immediate timescale, engaged user selection, historical or weak baseline, no cost denominator — the finding you are being asked to treat as novel evidence of educational technology's promise is, in structure, a contemporary instance of the apparatus Skinner initiated. It may be right or it may be wrong. What it cannot be, in the absence of the alternatives Thorndike and Bjork and Bransford and Messick have long specified, is the definitive evidence of transformative learning its rhetoric typically invokes.

This is the pattern the volume's next several essais examine as it manifests in specific traditions: the Bloom 2-sigma framing in Essai Four, the comparative sigma claims that treat non-commensurable studies as commensurable in Essai Five, the meta-analytic aggregation that smooths the apparatus's characteristic features in Essai Six. Each essai after this one tells a specific story about how the apparatus shapes particular claims in particular ways. This essai has traced the apparatus itself — its origins, its inheritance, its active operation in a contemporary study you can read carefully yourself. Whether the essais that follow show you a pattern you recognize from your own reading, or whether they show you something you have not encountered before, the apparatus will be there. Its inheritance is the volume's subject.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai walks six decades in the space the book typically gives to a single study, and the compression has costs. I want to name where I am uncertain whether the compression has worked.

I am not sure I have been fair to Skinner. The 1958 paper is more methodologically careful than its reputation suggests, and my essai tries to credit this by noting that Skinner was working honestly within a theoretical framework that specified what his measurements should capture. But the essai's argument requires treating Skinner as the originator of an apparatus that has had specific consequences, and that framing places Skinner in a lineage whose subsequent uses he did not author and cannot be held responsible for. A reader sympathetic to Skinner as a historical figure might find the treatment compressed to the point of caricature. I have tried to avoid this specifically, but the compression is real.

I am not sure the Suppes and Anderson passages give those traditions their due. Suppes and Anderson were, like Skinner, serious researchers whose work transformed the fields they operated in. The essai treats them primarily as inheritors of Skinner's apparatus, which is one true thing but not the only true thing about their work. A reader who works specifically in the CAI or ITS traditions may find the framing too compressed. The alternative would be to extend the essai considerably, which would shift the balance away from the inheritance argument.

I am not sure about the treatment of Jiang et al. 2021. The study is methodologically careful within its terms, and my application of the apparatus surfaces specific features of the study's design that the paper itself is transparent about — the reading-and-listening restriction, the completion-threshold selection, the lack of delayed post-testing, the author affiliations. But a reader who finds the critique unbalanced could fairly ask whether I would apply the same apparatus as rigorously to a study whose findings I happened to find more palatable. I have tried to ask the same questions I would ask of any study in the tradition. Whether I have succeeded is partly for the reader to judge.

I am not sure about the overlap with Essai Nine. The book's ninth essai examines Duolingo specifically through the engagement-versus-learning distinction — what the platform's headline metrics (daily active users, streaks, time-on-app) do and do not index about language acquisition. This essai engages Duolingo through a different question: whether the measurement apparatus of the Jiang et al. 2021 study continues the inheritance from Skinner's teaching machine. The two essais cover different terrain, but they share a subject, and a reader coming to Duolingo fresh in this essai after having read Essai Nine may find the treatment partially redundant. I have tried to approach Jiang from the specific angle this essai requires — the measurement-apparatus inheritance — rather than from the angle the ninth essai handles. But the two essais engage the same platform from complementary directions, and the reader will need to hold both in mind.

Finally, I am not sure the essai's inheritance claim is sufficiently tentative. The argument that a measurement apparatus persists across six decades is a strong empirical claim, and I have made it while compressing six decades into a single essai. A specialist in any of the eras I discussed could identify developments and variations the essai has not captured. The apparatus has not been *completely* static; there have been local variations, partial adoptions of alternatives, contested territory within specific programs of research. What I am claiming is that the dominant pattern has been inheritance rather than revision, and that the pattern is visible in specific contemporary instances. A reader who finds this framing insufficiently nuanced for the complexity of the historical record has a fair objection.

The next essai — the fourth of this volume — examines what the Bloom 1984 2-sigma claim actually supports, under the apparatus this essai has just named. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 3. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 28-30 pages. Word count: approximately 6,800.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**Block structure.** The essai follows a historical-sweep-plus-close-reading structure specific to its function: Block A opens on Skinner's 1953 classroom observation and the 1958 *Science* paper, Block B extends to Suppes and PLATO, Block C examines Cognitive Tutor and the ITS tradition with its own honest acknowledgment of limits, Block D traces the adaptive-learning era (Knewton, DreamBox, i-Ready), Block E briefly covers the current AI-tutor wave, Block F is the extended close reading of Jiang et al. 2021, and Block G names the alternatives (Thorndike, Bjork, Bransford, Messick) that existed and were not adopted. The structure has been extended beyond the typical five-block form to accommodate the six-decade sweep.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 6,800 words, projecting to 28-30 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — above the 22-25 range the spec suggested. The six-decade inheritance argument required the extra length; without it, the argument becomes assertion rather than demonstration. A revision pass could compress particularly Block D (adaptive learning) and Block E (AI-tutor wave) since those are covered at length in other essais. But the Skinner/Suppes/Anderson/Jiang sections are load-bearing and compressing them would weaken the spine.

**Respect for Skinner.** The essai treats Skinner's 1958 paper as the more careful document it actually is, not as the caricature it is often reduced to. The measurement choices Skinner made are named as theoretical commitments rather than as errors. The apparatus's subsequent inheritance is what the essai critiques, not Skinner's own work. This respect-within-critique framing is discussed specifically in the closing uncertainties section.

**Respect for Anderson and Koedinger.** The Cognitive Tutor passage specifically credits Anderson et al.'s own 1995 acknowledgment of their system's transfer limits — that students display transfer to the degree they can map the tutor environment to the test environment. This is a direct quotation from the primary paper, and the essai uses it to show that the methodological transparency the 1995 paper displays is what makes the inheritance pattern visible. The subsequent citations of Cognitive Tutor's effect sizes that strip Anderson's caveats are what the essai critiques, not the 1995 paper itself.

**The Jiang et al. 2021 close reading.** This is Block F and is load-bearing for the essai's closing argument. The reading applies specific apparatus questions — outcome measure (STAMP 4S reading and listening, alignment with Duolingo's interface modalities), timescale (immediate post-test), selection (completion threshold, survivor bias), baseline (historical rather than contemporaneous), cost (absent), author affiliations (four of five Duolingo-employed). The questions are applied as the apparatus the earlier blocks surfaced — they are not a separate critique imported for Jiang specifically. This disciplinary continuity between the historical sweep and the contemporary case is what the essai's structure requires, and the Jiang section tries to model it explicitly.

**The Essai 9 overlap question.** I have been specific about this in the uncertainties section. Essai 9 examines Duolingo through the engagement-versus-learning distinction (headline metrics, "IDK IDK" observation structure, behavioral engagement proxying for cognitive engagement). Essai 3 examines Duolingo through the measurement-apparatus inheritance (what Jiang measures, what it does not, how the choices continue Skinner's pattern). The two essais share a platform but engage different questions. A reader who reads both essais will encounter Duolingo twice from two different angles, which is how the volume is designed to work — spiral returns that add analytical layers.

**The alternatives that did not take.** The seventh block names Thorndike 1906/1924 on transfer, Bjork 1992 on storage-versus-retrieval, Bransford et al. 1999 on deep understanding, and Messick 1989 on construct validity as methodological alternatives that have existed across the same six decades and have not been adopted as the field's standard apparatus. This block prepares the ground for Essai 13's synthesis on the measure-construct distinction and keeps the essai from reading as a pure indictment. The alternatives *existed*; the field *chose* the inherited apparatus rather than adopting them; the choice has consequences that are worth naming.

**What the draft does particularly well.**
- Anchors in a specific primary source (Skinner 1958) with direct attention to what that paper actually claimed and measured, rather than to its caricature.
- Traces the inheritance through specific studies in specific eras, not through general characterizations.
- Credits Anderson et al. 1995 for its own methodological transparency about transfer limits, and shows how the inheritance problem is that subsequent citations did not preserve what the original paper acknowledged.
- Applies the apparatus to Jiang et al. 2021 as a live instance of the pattern, producing specific observations that the paper itself supports (the reading-and-listening restriction, the completion-threshold selection, the author affiliations).
- Names the alternatives (Thorndike, Bjork, Bransford, Messick) as available and unadopted rather than as absent, which prepares the volume's methodological reform arguments in later essais.

**What the draft may have gotten wrong.**
- The six-decade sweep is compressed. Specialists in any specific era may find the treatment too thin for the complexity of the historical record.
- The Jiang critique is sharp, and a reader sympathetic to the study may find it unbalanced. I have tried to apply the same questions I would apply to any EdTech efficacy study, but the appearance of applying critical attention specifically to this Duolingo study without applying comparable attention to other specific studies in the essai is unavoidable within the essai's length.
- The alternatives-that-did-not-take section (Block G) is brief, with one paragraph per tradition. A more extensive treatment of any one of Thorndike, Bjork, Bransford, or Messick would strengthen the specific methodological argument. The essai defers to Essai 13 for the Messick development, which is structurally right for the volume but leaves Block G more gestural than ideal in this essai specifically.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On Bloom's Two Sigma

*Essai 4*

---

In 1984, the educational psychologist Benjamin Bloom published a seventeen-page paper in *Educational Researcher* under the title *The 2 Sigma Problem: The Search for Methods of Group Instruction as Effective as One-to-One Tutoring*. The paper reported findings from two doctoral dissertations Bloom had supervised at the University of Chicago — Joanne Anania's in 1981 and Joseph Arthur Burke's in 1983 — and it advanced a specific claim. Students who received one-to-one tutoring combined with mastery learning, Bloom wrote, performed approximately two standard deviations above students in conventional classroom instruction on the same assessments. In the language that has since become dominant, the effect size was about 2.0 sigma. The average tutored student outperformed approximately 98 percent of the conventionally instructed students in the comparison group.

This finding has become, across the four decades since its publication, the single most-cited number in the learning-systems field. It is invoked in nearly every justification of tutoring as an educational intervention, in nearly every funding pitch for adaptive learning systems, in nearly every marketing claim that an AI-powered tool can "approach the effectiveness of one-on-one instruction." Sal Khan referenced it from the TED stage in 2023 as justification for building Khanmigo; he developed the framing further in his 2024 book *Brave New Words*. The Gates Foundation invokes it in its documentation of personalized-learning investments. The Chan Zuckerberg Initiative invokes it. McKinsey invokes it in consulting work for school districts. When someone working in this field says "we want to close the two-sigma gap," they are invoking this paper, whether they have read it or not.

I want to ask you to hold this number for a moment before this essai goes any further.

*Two standard deviations.* Nothing else in the published literature on educational interventions approaches it. The typical effect size reported in large-scale field-based randomized controlled trials of educational interventions is approximately 0.10 sigma — one-twentieth of the Bloom figure. The median effect reported across Kurt VanLehn's 2011 meta-analysis of intelligent tutoring systems was 0.76 sigma. The pooled effect from Nickow, Oreopoulos, and Quan's 2020 systematic review of ninety-six randomized tutoring evaluations was 0.37 sigma. No finding in the subsequent four decades of education research has reproduced Bloom's 2.0 sigma result at comparable magnitude. The 2-sigma number, as a measured finding, sits alone.

And yet it circulates as though it were a benchmark — a target, a standard that successful educational interventions might approach. An ordinary empirical observation, when repeated often enough without its conditions, becomes a law of nature in the reader's ear. The gap between what Bloom actually showed and how the 2-sigma figure now functions in the field's rhetorical economy is the subject of this essai.

I want to be clear about what this essai is not doing. It is not arguing that Bloom was wrong. It is not arguing that the finding was fabricated, inflated, or methodologically unsound. The 1984 paper is careful, internally consistent, and explicit about its conditions. Bloom himself was not making the kinds of claims that are made on his behalf today. The paper's title names a *problem* — the gap between what intensive tutoring produces and what conventional classroom instruction produces, and the cost barrier that prevented tutoring from being scaled as a universal intervention. Bloom was not claiming tutoring could be universally deployed; he was calling for research into group-instruction methods that might approach tutoring's effectiveness at scalable cost. What the 2-sigma figure has been asked to do in the forty years since would have surprised him.

The essai reads Bloom's paper carefully, walks through what the finding actually supports, examines what the replication record has shown across four decades, and then examines how the number has been deployed as a benchmark in contemporary AI-tutor advocacy. The reader's question at the end is not "was Bloom right?" — he was right about what he measured. The reader's question is "what kind of evidence is a 2-sigma figure from 1984, and what can that evidence support?"

---

Begin with the studies themselves.

The Anania and Burke dissertations that provided Bloom's data were specific experiments in specific conditions. The subjects were fourth, fifth, and eighth graders in American schools, studying probability in mathematics and cartography in social studies. These subject areas were chosen deliberately. Bloom and his students wanted to minimize the effect of prior learning on outcomes — topics that most students would have encountered before would have compressed the range of possible gains. Probability and cartography were relatively unfamiliar to the student populations tested, which allowed the experiments to measure the impact of instruction without prior-knowledge confounds.

Three instructional conditions were compared. Conventional instruction: roughly thirty students per teacher, traditional lecture-and-assessment format, summative assessment at the end of the unit. Mastery learning: also in groups of roughly thirty, but with formative assessment built in throughout the instructional sequence, and with "correctives" administered to students who did not initially demonstrate mastery before advancing to the next material. And one-to-one tutoring: students receiving individualized instruction from a tutor, at ratios of one-to-one, one-to-two, or one-to-three, with mastery-learning principles built into the tutoring interaction.

Each condition ran for approximately three weeks. Assessment was via researcher-designed tests aligned to the instructional content. The results Bloom reported in 1984 were as follows: mastery learning produced effects of roughly 1.0 sigma compared to conventional instruction; tutoring produced effects of roughly 2.0 sigma. The 2.0 sigma figure — the one that has become canonical — came from the combined effect of tutoring plus mastery learning, compared to conventional classroom instruction without either.

I want to pause on several features of this design that are rarely named in contemporary invocations.

First, the tutors in these experiments were not professional educators. They were undergraduates who received approximately one week of training before tutoring began. When current AI-tutor advocates invoke Bloom's "super tutor" as what they are building toward, they are invoking, as the original benchmark, undergraduate students with a week of training, working with small groups of children on unfamiliar material over a three-week window. This is not a criticism of the original design; Anania and Burke used the tutors they had. It is an observation that the "expert human tutor" who populates contemporary AI-tutor rhetoric is a construct the original experiments did not include.

Second, the tutoring condition was confounded with mastery learning by design. Students in the tutoring condition did not simply get one-to-one instruction; they got one-to-one instruction combined with the formative assessment, corrective feedback, and criterion-referenced advancement that characterize mastery learning as an instructional approach. The 2.0 sigma figure is the combined effect of both interventions. VanLehn and others have since argued that mastery learning itself — the 90-percent-threshold-for-advancing structure — may have been the primary driver of the gains, with tutoring serving more as the delivery mechanism than as the causal ingredient. Whether this argument is right is contested. What is not contested is that the 2.0 sigma came from both interventions operating together, not from tutoring alone.

Third, the assessments were researcher-designed tests aligned closely to what the instructional units had taught. This is, as the second essai of this volume established and as the fifth essai examined at length, a measurement choice with known effects on the magnitude of reported effect sizes. Proximal assessments — aligned to what the intervention specifically taught — produce systematically larger effect sizes than distal assessments that measure broader constructs. Bloom's 2.0 sigma was produced under the measurement conditions that maximize reported effects. This is not a criticism of the Anania and Burke studies; they measured what they were designed to measure. It is an observation about what Bloom's figure is a measurement of. It is a measurement of aligned-item performance after three weeks of instruction, on content the students had not previously encountered, under intensive instructional conditions. It is not, strictly, a measurement of "learning" as that construct is used in the field's vocabulary today.

Fourth, and perhaps most importantly, Bloom's 2-sigma finding was reported with an explicit cost acknowledgment. The paper's title is *The 2 Sigma Problem* for a reason. The problem, as Bloom framed it, was not that tutoring worked; the problem was that tutoring's effectiveness came at a cost structure that prevented its universal deployment. Bloom wrote that one-to-one tutoring was "too costly for most societies to bear on a large scale." His paper was a call — explicit, repeated, structurally foregrounded — for research into methods of *group* instruction that might approach tutoring's effectiveness without tutoring's cost. The 2-sigma figure, in the paper where it originates, is not offered as a benchmark to be pursued. It is offered as a gap to be closed through scalable methods that the paper explicitly acknowledges have not yet been developed.

Bloom, in 1984, was asking a question. The question was: can we achieve, through group-instruction methods that can actually be deployed, something close to what intensive tutoring produces? The question was genuine. He did not claim to know the answer. He did not claim the 2-sigma gap could be closed. He did not claim any specific technology or method would close it. He named the gap and asked the field to pursue research that might close it. That was the paper.

---

The replication record across the subsequent four decades is its own story, and one that the contemporary invocation of the 2-sigma figure rarely engages.

The earliest meta-analytic work on tutoring, conducted by Peter Cohen and colleagues in 1982 — actually published two years before Bloom's 2-sigma paper — found an average tutoring effect across the then-available studies of approximately 0.33 sigma. This was a respectable effect by the standards of education research, but a small fraction of what Bloom's figure would later describe.

By 2011, Kurt VanLehn conducted what remains one of the most methodologically careful meta-analyses of tutoring and intelligent tutoring systems. VanLehn aggregated effect sizes across human tutoring and across computer-based intelligent tutoring systems, and reported medians of 0.79 sigma for human tutoring and 0.76 sigma for intelligent tutoring systems. Both numbers are substantial by the standards of educational interventions. Both are less than half of Bloom's 2.0 sigma figure.

In 2020, Andre Nickow, Philip Oreopoulos, and Vincent Quan conducted a systematic review and meta-analysis of ninety-six randomized tutoring evaluations. Their pooled effect size was 0.37 sigma — meaningfully smaller than VanLehn's 2011 figure, and less than one-fifth of Bloom's 2.0. The study found that professional teachers and paraprofessionals working as tutors produced stronger effects than nonprofessional volunteers, which complicates the story but does not rescue the magnitude. No condition in the Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan data approached 2 sigma.

I want to be careful here, because the replication story is easy to overstate in either direction. The honest claim is this: the 2-sigma magnitude Bloom reported has rarely been reproduced in subsequent meta-analytic work. Tutoring effects in the subsequent literature tend to cluster in the range of 0.3 to 0.8 sigma, with the higher end appearing in carefully controlled studies with specific populations and aligned measures, and the lower end appearing in large-scale field studies with heterogeneous populations and more distal measures. The 2-sigma figure sits well above this distribution. It is not that Bloom was wrong — the Anania and Burke studies measured what they measured — but that the specific conditions that produced 2 sigma have not, in four decades, been systematically reproduced.

What conditions would it take? A short instructional window, probably; prior unfamiliarity with the material, probably; a proximal assessment closely aligned to the instruction, almost certainly; a careful implementation of mastery learning alongside tutoring, probably. The question is whether these are the conditions that characterize educational interventions as they are typically deployed, or whether they are specific experimental conditions that optimize for the largest possible reported effect. The subsequent literature has generally not taken the position that these conditions are representative. When interventions are deployed at scale, over longer timescales, across heterogeneous populations, with more distal assessments, the effect sizes typically shrink. This is not an indictment of the interventions, and it is not an indictment of Bloom. It is a description of how effect sizes behave in this literature — a description the fifth essai of this volume examined at length.

---

There is a second dimension to the replication question that I want to name directly, because it is usually left implicit. Bloom's paper was explicit about cost. The 2-sigma figure was produced under conditions that are expensive to maintain. What did they cost, and what does a current equivalent cost?

Bloom's original tutoring condition required a one-to-one or one-to-three ratio between tutor and student, sustained over three weeks of instruction. In the contemporary United States, research-validated high-dosage tutoring programs provide a reasonable current cost benchmark. Saga Education, which has run large-scale tutoring programs in American high schools for over a decade and has been the subject of multiple rigorous evaluations, operates at a two-to-one student-to-tutor ratio, with tutors working with the same students every school day for a class period. Saga's cost per student per school year runs in the range of 3,500 to 4,800 dollars. MATCH Corps, a similar program, runs at comparable costs. The core human-resource requirement of these programs — trained tutors working with small numbers of students over sustained periods — is what drives the cost structure, and that structure is not substantially different from what Anania and Burke's studies required.

Now compare this to the cost structures of the educational technologies that invoke Bloom's 2-sigma as their benchmark. Khan Academy's institutional product, as the second essai of this volume noted, runs approximately ten dollars per student per year. Rori, the AI-powered tutor deployed in Ghana that the fifth essai discussed, runs at approximately five dollars per student per year. Duolingo's free tier is free; its paid tier is on the order of a hundred dollars per year. The AI-tutor systems currently being evaluated — the ones that invoke Bloom as the benchmark they are approaching — operate at cost structures two to three orders of magnitude below the tutoring programs that have actually been evaluated against Bloom-like benchmarks.

This is not a critique of low-cost interventions. A 0.36 sigma effect at five dollars per student is an extraordinary achievement on its own terms. What it is not, however, is a comparable finding to Bloom's 2-sigma at a cost structure two hundred times smaller. The interventions are not on the same axis. They are producing different magnitudes of effect at radically different cost structures, which means they are answering different questions and serving different policy roles. A ten-dollar platform that produces 0.26 sigma is an intervention at one part of the cost-effectiveness space. A four-thousand-dollar tutoring program that produces 0.37 sigma is at another. Bloom's original 2-sigma result lived in a research condition that, if it had to be sustained at scale with professional tutors, would cost something closer to the latter than to the former. The 2-sigma figure at scale, if it could be produced at all, would not be a cheap finding.

When Sal Khan, in his 2023 TED talk, invoked Bloom's 2-sigma as the benchmark Khanmigo was designed to approach, the implicit claim was that AI could produce Bloom's result at a cost structure that Bloom's tutors never achieved. This is, in principle, a reasonable aspiration. In practice, it is also a claim about a specific quantity — the cost-adjusted magnitude of the effect — that the evidence base does not support and that the contemporary AI-tutor literature, which I will examine in a later essai of this volume, has not delivered. A claim that AI can "close the 2-sigma gap" is not a claim that AI can produce 2 sigma; it is a claim that AI can produce Bloom-magnitude gains at cost structures two orders of magnitude below Bloom's. The subsequent Khanmigo evaluations — which I examine elsewhere — have produced effect sizes far smaller than 2 sigma, at cost structures that are admittedly much smaller than Bloom's, but without the claimed magnitude ever materializing.

---

There is one more dimension of Bloom's original finding that the contemporary invocation rarely engages, and I want to surface it before closing this essai.

Bloom's paper was not about building a technology to automate tutoring. It was about identifying the variables — the "alterable variables" in Bloom's vocabulary — that might be combined in group instruction to produce effects approaching what tutoring produces. His paper included a table of such variables, drawn from the research available in 1984, which has circulated widely in the intervening decades. Mastery learning produced effects of approximately 1.0 sigma. Reinforcement produced approximately 1.2. Feedback-corrective approaches produced approximately 1.0. Cues and explanations produced approximately 1.0. Student time on task produced approximately 1.0. Graded homework produced approximately 0.8. Cooperative learning produced approximately 0.8.

These numbers, like the 2-sigma figure itself, are artifacts of specific experimental conditions and do not generally replicate at magnitude in subsequent work. But what I want to point at is the character of the variables. They are all, in Bloom's original vision, instructional design variables that teachers — human teachers — would implement in classrooms. Bloom was not imagining a technology that would automate the teacher's role. He was imagining a humanized and intensified version of the classroom teacher's role. "The teacher," Bloom wrote explicitly in the 1984 paper, "is more important than ever." Technology, in Bloom's framing, was to be used to liberate teachers from the administrative burden of assessment and correction, so that they could focus their attention on "diagnosing misunderstanding" and "motivating and rewarding achievement."

This framing — technology as freeing the teacher to do more teacher-work, not as replacing the teacher — has been largely lost in the contemporary invocation of Bloom. When Sal Khan writes, in *Brave New Words*, that AI will allow every student to have "a personal tutor," he is invoking Bloom's 2-sigma finding but inverting Bloom's proposed solution. Bloom was not proposing more tutors. He was proposing better-supported teachers, with tools that would allow group instruction to approach tutoring's effectiveness. What has actually been built, in the name of Bloom's vision, is largely tools that attempt to bypass the teacher rather than to support her. Whether this is a better strategy than Bloom's — whether AI tutors can, in fact, produce outcomes that supported-teacher group instruction cannot — is an open empirical question. What it is not, however, is a fulfillment of Bloom's 1984 paper. It is a different project, borrowing Bloom's number while departing from his theory of what would close the gap.

---

Here is what the 2-sigma figure is evidence for, read carefully.

Fourth, fifth, and eighth graders studying unfamiliar material over three-week windows, in small groups or individually, under conditions combining one-to-one tutoring with mastery-learning principles, assessed on researcher-designed aligned tests, performed approximately two standard deviations above students in conventional classroom instruction on the same assessments. This is a real finding. It was produced under specific conditions. It has not, in four decades, been reliably reproduced at that magnitude when the conditions have differed substantially.

Here is what the 2-sigma figure is not evidence for.

It is not evidence that human tutoring, deployed at scale over the course of a school year to heterogeneous student populations, produces 2 sigma effects. The subsequent meta-analytic record shows effects around 0.4 to 0.8 sigma under such conditions, with substantial variance by tutor quality and intervention design.

It is not evidence that educational technologies can approach 2 sigma effects, whether or not they are more cost-effective than human tutoring. The subsequent AI-tutor literature has produced effects in the range of 0.17 to 0.73 sigma, depending on conditions, and the higher end appears only under the specific measurement regimes that systematically produce larger reported effects.

It is not evidence that any specific intervention — human or technological — would produce 2 sigma if only it were implemented with sufficient fidelity. The conditions that produced 2 sigma in Anania and Burke's original studies are not conditions that any known scalable intervention reliably reproduces.

It is not evidence that "the 2-sigma gap" between tutoring and classroom instruction is a fixed quantity waiting to be closed by better technology. It is evidence that, in one specific experimental setting four decades ago, a 2-sigma gap was observed between two specific instructional conditions. What is happening when the field invokes "the 2-sigma gap" as though it were a standing feature of the educational landscape is something other than citation of Bloom's finding. It is the deployment of a number that has acquired rhetorical life independent of what it originally measured.

---

I want to close by naming what I hope this essai has done and what it has not.

What I hope it has done is rescue Bloom from the misuse his finding has been subject to. The 1984 paper is a careful piece of work. Bloom was not making the claims that are now made in his name. He acknowledged the cost barrier explicitly; he framed his result as identifying a problem rather than as advertising a solution; he emphasized the teacher's role rather than imagining the teacher's replacement; he called for research into scalable methods rather than claiming that any specific method would close the gap. What Bloom did is not what the field has done in the decades since. The field has taken a number from his paper and attached it to projects that do not resemble the research his paper was calling for.

Rescuing Bloom does not mean defending the number as a contemporary benchmark. The number does not generalize in the ways the contemporary invocation requires. The apparatus built by the second essai of this volume — the five questions about outcome measure, baseline, population, timescale, and cost — applied to the 2-sigma finding produces a much more modest and specific claim than the one that circulates. The finding is a finding about specific conditions. Removed from those conditions, it becomes a shibboleth — a word used to signal membership in a discourse, not a measurement of anything current.

What I hope the essai has not done is treat Bloom as a villain or his 1984 paper as a methodological error. Neither characterization would be accurate or useful. Bloom did careful work on an important question. His paper's framing has been distorted by subsequent citation in ways he could not have anticipated and would probably not have endorsed. The distortion is not Bloom's responsibility. It is the responsibility of the apparatus that has circulated his number, and that is what the rest of this volume continues to examine.

The next essai examines what happens when the number changes from a single finding to an aggregated one. If Bloom's 2-sigma suffers from the specific conditions of its production — conditions that are invisible when the number is cited — what happens when many such findings are combined into a meta-analytic summary? What does the aggregation show, and what does it conceal? That is the question Essai 6 takes up.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai has tried to walk a careful line, and I want to name explicitly where I am unsure about whether the line is in the right place.

I am not sure I have been careful enough about the distinction between "the 2-sigma finding in Bloom's paper" and "the 2-sigma number in contemporary discourse." These are not the same object. The first is a specific result from specific experiments. The second is a rhetorical benchmark with a life partly independent of the paper it originated in. The essai tries to address both, but a reader might fairly say that the first is given more charitable treatment than the second. If the essai has drifted, in its treatment of the contemporary invocations, into a kind of unearned certainty about what invoking the number is *really* doing, that would be a drift worth naming. I am counting on readers who disagree with specific characterizations of contemporary invocations — Khan's, the Gates Foundation's, the general rhetorical field — to tell me where the essai has been unfair.

I am not sure I have given enough attention to the argument that the 2-sigma finding, even if not literally reproducible, might still serve as a useful aspirational benchmark. There is a version of this argument that says: even if no actual intervention reaches 2 sigma, the benchmark motivates research, funding, and ambition in ways that produce better interventions than would exist without it. This argument is not trivial. It is essentially the argument that aspirational targets matter even when they cannot be achieved. The essai treats this argument dismissively, by implication, by framing the rhetorical life of the number as distortion. A reader who thinks the aspirational benchmark has been useful — that 0.36 sigma would not have been achieved if the field had only aspired to 0.36 sigma — has a fair case to make. I have not made that case's strongest version here.

I am not sure about the balance of the replication-record discussion. I have presented the subsequent meta-analytic record as showing that tutoring effects cluster in the 0.3-to-0.8 range, well below Bloom's 2.0 figure. This is accurate as a description of central tendency. But the range has variance, and there are studies in the literature that have found larger effects, and there are conditions under which larger effects reliably appear. A reader who works in the tutoring-research literature might reasonably say that the essai's summary undersells the evidence that well-designed, well-implemented tutoring can produce effects above 1.0 sigma in favorable conditions. I have chosen to emphasize the central tendency because the central tendency is what the contemporary citation apparatus most obscures. But the emphasis is a choice, not a neutral summary.

Finally, I am not sure the cost-structure argument in Block D lands with the right weight. The claim that Khanmigo at ten dollars and Bloom-style tutoring at four thousand dollars are not on the same axis is, I think, correct. But it can read as dismissive of low-cost interventions, which is not the intent. The intent is that cost-matched comparisons require cost-matched claims, and that the invocation of Bloom's number in the service of ten-dollar platforms is a comparison without a matched cost structure. A reader who finds the argument more critical than it should be — who finds the treatment of Khanmigo's cost structure as part of the critique when it should be part of the product's credit — should tell me.

The next essai examines meta-analyses as a form. If the 2-sigma figure suffers from conditions-obscured-by-citation, what happens when we aggregate hundreds of such conditions-obscured-by-citation findings into a meta-analytic summary? That question is what follows. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 4. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 22–24 pages. Word count: approximately 5,400.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**The discipline: rescuing Bloom rather than debunking him.** The spec was specific about this, and the draft has tried to hold it. Every analytical observation about the 1984 paper is paired with an acknowledgment that Bloom's own framing is not what the essai is critiquing. The villain of the essai is the rhetorical apparatus that has traveled with the number, not Bloom himself. The closing explicitly says so.

**Block structure.** Five-block structure as specified: Block A (the number and its reputation), Block B (reading the original paper — the Anania and Burke studies, the conditions, the four features most often unnamed), Block C (the replication question — Cohen 1982, VanLehn 2011, Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan 2020), Block D (the cost question — Saga, MATCH, Khanmigo, Rori), Block E (what the 2-sigma figure is and is not evidence for). The draft adds a sixth thematic section between D and E on Bloom's "alterable variables" and his teacher-centered vision — I included this because the contrast between Bloom's theory-of-solution and the contemporary AI-replaces-teacher move is material the essai's argument benefits from. If the revision pass wants to cut this for length, it can be compressed to a paragraph in the closing section.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 5,400 words, projecting to 22-24 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — within the 22-25 page range specified, closer to the floor than the target 23. The essai could bear a bit more length if the revision pass wanted to expand the replication-record discussion or the VanLehn "granularity" taxonomy. It could also be tightened by cutting the "alterable variables" section. The current length feels right to me, but the decision is yours.

**Direct quotes.** The draft uses direct quotes from Bloom's 1984 paper sparingly — "too costly for most societies to bear on a large scale," "the teacher is more important than ever," "diagnosing misunderstanding," "motivating and rewarding achievement." These are the quotes the Deep Research document highlighted as load-bearing for the rescuing-Bloom move. If you have access to the original paper and want to verify them or add others, the draft is structured to accommodate additional quotes without restructuring.

**What the draft does particularly well.**
- Opens on the number as specimen rather than target: places Bloom's figure in the context of the rest of the education-research effect-size distribution before beginning the close reading.
- Names the specific conditions of the Anania and Burke studies that most invocations omit — the undergraduate tutors with one week of training, the three-week window, the researcher-designed proximal assessments, the tutoring-plus-mastery-learning confound.
- Treats the replication record with appropriate care — not "Bloom was wrong," but "the magnitude has rarely reproduced under different conditions."
- Uses the cost question (Block D) as a continuation of Essai 2's apparatus rather than as a new departure, which reinforces the spiral structure of the book.
- The "alterable variables" and teacher-centered section distinguishes Bloom's actual theory from what the field has invoked in his name — Khan's AI-replaces-tutor move is shown to be a departure from Bloom, not a fulfillment of him.

**What the draft may have gotten wrong.**
- The treatment of Khan may be slightly harder-edged than the invitational register calls for. Specific passages to audit: the "Khan was invoking Bloom but inverting Bloom's proposed solution" passage, and the characterization of what invoking 2-sigma "is really doing." A revision pass should check whether these read as description or as implicit indictment.
- The VanLehn 2011 figures are invoked without much context on what makes that meta-analysis distinctive. A reader who does not know the ITS literature may want more. Compression for length cut some of this; a revision pass could restore it if the overall length budget permits.
- The essai does not engage VanLehn's "granularity" taxonomy (answer-based vs. step-based vs. substep-based tutoring) that the Deep Research document highlights. I left this out because it is methodologically fascinating but would extend the essai substantially, and the discipline is to rescue Bloom, not to provide a complete tour of the tutoring-effectiveness literature. The next essai on meta-analyses is a better home for that material if it belongs in the book.
- The closing "what the 2-sigma figure is not evidence for" passage is rhetorically parallel and may read as hectoring. A revision pass should check whether it lands as analytical summary or as polemical finish.

**Faux-humility check.** The closing "What I am not sure about" section is long — four paragraphs rather than three. The essai is load-bearing enough that I allowed this, but a hostile reader should audit whether the specific uncertainties I name are genuine uncertainties or whether any of them drift toward false-humility performance. In particular, the paragraph on "the aspirational benchmark argument" is honest but deliberate; I have not engaged that argument's strongest version in the body, and naming that I have not is itself a disciplinary move. Whether the move reads as honesty or as the author's curated display of balance is something a reader would be better positioned to judge than I am.

**The bridge to Essai 6.** The draft closes on the question *what happens when individual findings like Bloom's are aggregated into meta-analyses?* This sets up Essai 6 directly. It is the right bridge.

Ready for your revision pass.
# Before the Draft — A Source Verification Concern

The Deep Research document you shared has several elements that worked well (the Kestin/Eedi/Rori methodological differences are clear; the Cheung & Slavin 0.73/0.13 gap sourcing is specific; the Kraft 2020 framing supports the essai). It also has several elements I want to flag as unverifiable or possibly fabricated, because the essai's discipline — refusing to adjudicate between competing sigmas while examining how they get placed side by side — cannot survive uncertain sourcing.

**Unverifiable or suspicious in the Deep Research document:**

1. **The 0.73/0.13 gap attribution.** The document attributes this to Cheung and Slavin (2016) as "the researcher-designed vs. standardized measures" distinction. I cannot independently verify that Cheung and Slavin published exactly this paired figure in 2016. The pattern they documented is real and well-known; whether the specific 0.73/0.13 pair comes from them, from Kraft 2020, or from a synthesized claim across multiple sources is the question the essai depends on. I will use the pattern (researcher-designed measures producing systematically larger effects than standardized measures) without asserting specific paired numbers unless the sourcing is cleaner than this document provides.

2. **The "ExperiMentor" system name.** The Deep Research document refers to Kestin's system by this name with specific architectural details. I cannot verify this matches the actual published Kestin et al. study. I will refer to Kestin's system generically as "the AI tutor used in the Kestin study" rather than by a potentially fabricated product name.

3. **Specific Eedi sub-group numbers ($0.17$ ITT / $0.34$ CACE / $0.25$ for Pupil Premium / $0.23$ for lower-attainment students).** These specific numbers have the shape of real data but I cannot verify them against the primary Eedi evaluation. I will use the general pattern (engagement-filtered subgroups showing larger effects than intent-to-treat) without citing exact numbers that I cannot confirm.

4. **"Banerjee and Duflo's 0.28 SD."** The Deep Research document cites this as a Rori comparison point. I cannot verify this specific attribution. Banerjee and Duflo have published extensively on education interventions in development contexts; whether "0.28 SD" is a specific figure from a specific study of theirs, or a synthesized claim, is unclear.

5. **The "Zhang et al. 2025 meta-analysis reporting large average effect of 0.88."** I cannot verify this citation exists.

6. **The "pedagogical failures rising from 17.7% to 77.8%" statistic.** I cannot verify this specific figure from a specific study.

**What I will use from the document:**

- The Kestin study's general claim of large effect sizes in Harvard physics (0.73-1.3 range) — the general magnitude has been reported in public discourse
- The Rori study's deployment context (Ghana, WhatsApp, roughly $5/student, cluster RCT, around 0.36) — these details are consistent with public reporting of the Henkel et al. study
- The Eedi study's general magnitude (around 0.17 ITT) and the existence of engagement-based sub-group analysis
- The Kestin context: Harvard, physics, selective student population, active-learning baseline
- The general point that Kestin used researcher-designed topic-specific assessments, Eedi used a standardized measure, Rori used a curriculum-aligned assessment

**The essai's approach given this sourcing:**

I will write the essai against the *pattern* rather than against specific paired numbers. Where a specific figure is needed, I will use it with a hedge appropriate to its sourcing. The essai's discipline — refusing to adjudicate — is actually strengthened by this constraint: it prevents me from building a counter-sigma argument around specific numbers I cannot verify. The essai can document the practice of sigma-comparison and the incommensurabilities it smooths over without pretending to have a more trustworthy set of numbers to offer in its place.

If after the draft you have access to primary sources that verify the specific figures, we can revise. For now, the essai errs on the side of acknowledged uncertainty over false precision.

---

# On the Lives of Numbers

*Essai 5 — Draft*

---

In April of 2026, on the Learning Engineering Google group, a participant named Jonah Bard posted a short message into a thread about the public retirement of Khanmigo. The message was generous, curious, and well-intentioned — the kind of message that serious practitioners in this field write when they are trying to understand a field in transition. *"I'm curious what the consensus is,"* Jonah wrote, *"on the Greg Kestin physics tutor at Harvard, the Eedi project with Google Research, or Rori — these also have quite large reported effect sizes, some even indicating much stronger results than 1-1 human tutoring!"*

I want to sit with this sentence for a moment before this essai goes any further.

What Jonah is doing in that sentence is doing something the learning-systems field does many times a day, in Slack threads and conference talks and investment committee meetings and journalism pieces. He is placing three findings on a single axis and suggesting that the axis is meaningful. *Kestin*: an effect size in the range of 0.73 sigma or higher, produced in introductory physics instruction at Harvard. *Eedi*: an effect size around 0.17 on intention-to-treat, from a UK middle-school mathematics deployment. *Rori*: an effect size of about 0.36 in a cluster-randomized trial in Ghanaian secondary schools. All three are reported as strong or meaningful findings in their respective contexts. All three have circulated in current AI-tutor discourse as evidence that this generation of systems is producing effects worth attention. And Jonah, reading them together, wants to know what we should make of the comparison.

I am not going to tell you what we should make of the comparison. That is not the essai's project. What the essai will do, across the pages that follow, is examine the comparison itself — the practice of placing sigmas side by side, the incommensurabilities that practice smooths over, the rhetorical work the comparison does even when no one involved intends for it to do rhetorical work. By the end of this essai, I hope you will be able to read Jonah's question differently. Not because you will know which of the three studies is "right," but because you will see what placing them on a single axis asks you to accept.

I want to be explicit about one thing before we begin. Jonah's question is a good question. He is asking it in the spirit of a serious practitioner trying to make sense of competing claims. The critique this essai offers is not a critique of Jonah, or of anyone who has ever placed sigmas next to each other in a meeting. It is a critique of a practice the field has institutionalized, which all of us — myself included — have performed. The apparatus that makes such comparisons feel natural is the apparatus this volume is examining. Jonah's sentence is evidence of the apparatus working as designed, not evidence of anyone doing something wrong.

---

Begin with the three studies.

The Kestin study was conducted at Harvard. The population was undergraduate students enrolled in introductory physics — a selective university context by any measure, and a particular sub-population even within that university. The AI tutor used in the study was carefully designed: specific system prompts constrained the tutor's behavior, forbidding it from providing direct answers and requiring step-by-step student engagement before the tutor would advance. The outcome measure was a post-test constructed specifically to assess what had been taught in the intervention sessions. The baseline was active-learning classroom instruction — not a weak baseline, to Kestin's credit, and arguably the strongest baseline the study could have used. The timescale was short: outcomes were measured at the end of the intervention, within a single course. The reported effect size was large, with figures of 0.73 sigma or higher circulating in the discourse that followed.

The Eedi study operated in a very different context. The population was Year 7 students in British state schools — roughly ages 11 and 12, across a range of school settings from well-resourced to under-resourced. The intervention was a mathematics platform integrated into teachers' practice over the course of a school year. The outcome measure was a standardized assessment of broad mathematical progress, not an assessment built around what the platform specifically taught. The baseline was business-as-usual mathematics instruction, which varied across participating schools. The timescale was a full academic year. The reported effect size on intention-to-treat — that is, across all students assigned to the intervention, whether or not they used the platform intensively — was around 0.17 sigma. For the sub-group of students who engaged with the platform at a high level, the effect was larger, roughly twice the intent-to-treat figure.

The Rori study was different again. It took place in Ghana, in a cluster-randomized trial across approximately seventeen schools and roughly 2,500 students in grades 6 through 9. The intervention was delivered through WhatsApp on inexpensive mobile phones, at a marginal cost estimated at around five dollars per student per year. The outcome measure was a curriculum-aligned mathematics assessment. The baseline was regular mathematics instruction in Ghanaian schools, which tends to face resource constraints quite different from those of British or American schools. The timescale was several months. The reported effect size was approximately 0.36 sigma, translating in the authors' framing to roughly an additional year of learning gains.

Three studies. Three effect sizes. 0.73, 0.17, 0.36.

When Jonah places these three numbers on a single axis and asks what the consensus is, the axis he is proposing is something like: *which of these systems produces the strongest learning gains?* And on that axis, read naively, the answer would be: *Kestin, by a considerable margin, with Rori second and Eedi third.*

This is not what the three numbers actually support.

---

Notice first what each study was measuring. Kestin's post-test was built for what was taught in the intervention. The test items and the intervention content were aligned by design. This is not a methodological flaw in Kestin's work — it is a legitimate choice that privileges sensitivity to the specific learning the intervention was attempting to produce. Researchers have a term for this: *proximal* assessment. Proximal assessments are sensitive to the intervention because they test exactly what the intervention was built to teach.

Eedi's assessment was the opposite. The standardized measure used in the Eedi evaluation was not aligned to what the platform specifically taught; it measured broad mathematical progress across a national curriculum, much of which the platform did not directly address. This is what education researchers call a *distal* or *independent* measure. Distal measures are less sensitive to specific interventions because they test broader constructs than any one intervention is designed to move. A 0.17 sigma effect on a distal measure is, by any honest accounting, a finding that survives a harder test than a 0.73 sigma effect on a proximal measure.

Rori's assessment sat between these. It was aligned to the mathematics curriculum the students were expected to be learning, which made it relatively sensitive to what the intervention targeted, but it was not built around the specific content of the Rori platform itself. In the landscape of EdTech efficacy research, this is closer to how independent assessments typically work.

What Jonah's question places on a single axis is therefore not three comparable numbers. It is three numbers from three different measurement regimes, each of which has a known and substantial effect on what sigmas tend to look like. There is a well-established pattern in education research, documented by Alan Cheung and Robert Slavin, by Matthew Kraft, and by subsequent methodological work, that effect sizes on researcher- or developer-designed assessments are systematically larger than effect sizes on standardized measures for the same interventions. The difference is not small. It is, by various estimates, a factor of several — interventions that produce effects in the 0.5 to 0.7 range on proximal measures routinely produce effects in the 0.1 to 0.2 range when measured on distal ones.

This pattern has consequences for Jonah's comparison. The 0.73 and the 0.17 are not measurements of different amounts of learning. They are measurements of different things, using instruments of different sensitivities, in contexts where the alignment between intervention and assessment differs substantially. To compare them as though they indexed the same underlying quantity — to place them on a single axis where higher means better — is to assume away the very differences that would make the comparison informative.

---

Notice next what each study was comparing its intervention against.

Kestin's baseline was active-learning classroom instruction at Harvard. This is a strong baseline. Active-learning pedagogy in physics has been the subject of decades of research, much of it showing that active approaches outperform traditional lectures. Harvard students experiencing active-learning physics instruction are receiving something close to the state of the art in undergraduate physics pedagogy. An intervention that outperforms this baseline is competing against good teaching, not against bad teaching or no teaching.

Eedi's baseline was business-as-usual in British state schools. This is a much weaker baseline, in a specific sense: it is the average of what varied schools actually do, which includes well-resourced teachers doing careful work and under-resourced teachers doing what they can with less. The average British mathematics classroom is not the Harvard active-learning seminar. An intervention that produces a 0.17 sigma effect against business-as-usual has outperformed a realistic baseline across a realistic range of school settings.

Rori's baseline was regular mathematics instruction in Ghanaian schools. This is a third kind of baseline — one where the constraints on typical instruction are substantial, the resources available to classrooms are thinner than in British or American contexts, and the gap between what is possible with more support and what currently happens is wide. An intervention producing 0.36 sigma against this baseline has outperformed a baseline that is already substantially constrained.

Now consider what this means for the comparison. To say that Kestin's 0.73 is "higher" than Rori's 0.36 is to say something, but it is not to say that Kestin's intervention produces more learning. Each sigma measures the intervention against its own baseline. If Kestin's baseline is stronger than Rori's, then a smaller sigma-difference against a stronger baseline can represent a larger absolute amount of learning than a larger sigma-difference against a weaker baseline. The comparison across the three studies is not a comparison of absolute learning quantities; it is a comparison of three different *deltas* against three different starting points.

This is not a new insight. It is a standard methodological observation that every introductory course on causal inference addresses. What is striking is how routinely the comparison ignores it. When three effect sizes are placed next to each other in a Slack thread, the baselines they are measured against are almost always invisible. The numbers circulate unattached to what they are numbers of.

---

Notice next who was being studied.

Kestin's population consisted of Harvard undergraduates in introductory physics. This is a specific population in every relevant sense. They are students who cleared the admissions threshold at one of the most selective universities in the world. They are students who chose to enroll in introductory physics, a course that self-selects for a particular kind of quantitative and analytical inclination. They bring high levels of what researchers call *self-regulated learning* — the capacity to direct their own attention, manage their own studying, persist through difficulty. They have reliable internet access, capable hardware, stable home environments, and an academic support infrastructure that most students anywhere in the world do not have access to. They are, in short, close to ideal subjects for an AI tutor that asks leading questions and expects the student to engage with the question productively rather than defaulting to extraction of the answer.

Eedi's population was Year 7 students in British state schools, across a wide range of school and family contexts. This population includes a significant share of students on what the UK education system calls *Pupil Premium* — students from lower-income families whose schools receive supplementary funding to support them. It includes students learning English as a second language, students with special educational needs, and students across the full range of prior mathematical attainment. It is, in other words, a substantially broader population than Kestin's, with more heterogeneous starting conditions and more variation in the non-school resources students can draw on.

Rori's population consisted of Ghanaian schoolchildren in grades 6 through 9, attending schools where the infrastructure for digital learning is thinner than in British or American contexts, using the intervention on basic mobile phones via a text-messaging application. This population faces constraints the other two do not: intermittent electricity, shared household devices, limited home support for mathematical study, and schools that are under-resourced by the standards the other two studies take for granted.

Each population is the population the study actually studied. None is the population the other two studied. When the three effect sizes are placed on a single axis, the implicit suggestion is that the interventions are interchangeable — that what Kestin did at Harvard could be done in Ghana, that what Eedi did in British state schools could be done with Harvard physics undergraduates, that the three sigmas are measuring what any of these systems would do in any of these contexts. None of this is supported by any of the three studies. Each study measured what its intervention did in its context, with its population, under its conditions. What any of the interventions would do in the other contexts is not in the data. The comparison treats these as interchangeable contexts, which they are not.

This is not a minor point. Students' starting conditions, their access to non-intervention support, their capacity for self-directed engagement with the tool, and the baseline instruction they would have received without the intervention — all of these shape what any intervention produces. The Harvard physics undergraduate and the rural Ghanaian eighth-grader are not, for purposes of AI tutoring efficacy, the same kind of subject. The sigmas they each produce do not fit on the same axis.

---

Notice next what timescale each study measured.

Kestin's assessment was administered immediately following the intervention sessions. The window between instruction and measurement was short — hours, perhaps days, not weeks. This is the measurement window that research on *storage strength* and *retrieval strength*, a distinction developed over decades by the experimental psychologist Robert Bjork and others, treats with specific caution. Immediate post-intervention performance can be high when retrieval strength is high — the material is fresh, the testing context is similar to the instructional context, the cognitive pathways that produced the learning are still active. Storage strength — the property that predicts whether a student will retain and be able to use what was learned three months later, six months later, a year later, in new contexts — is only measurable at a delay.

Eedi's timescale was a school year. Assessments were administered at the end of that year, on a standardized measure administered in the conventional testing context. The effect size reflects what had been learned, survived the months between instruction and testing, and was retrievable on a test that did not particularly reward familiarity with the platform's specific content. This is a much harder measurement to move than an immediate post-test on aligned material. A 0.17 sigma effect on this kind of measurement is, by that fact alone, evidence of something more durable than a 0.73 sigma effect on an immediate proximal post-test.

Rori's timescale was several months. The assessment sat between the other two — not so long as a full school year with broad curriculum coverage, not so short as an immediate post-test. What Rori's 0.36 reflects is something the intervention produced over a multi-month window, measured on a curriculum-aligned instrument, on a population where the absence of the intervention would have meant continuing with the baseline instruction available in those schools.

When three effect sizes are placed on a single axis without regard for what timescale each represents, what is being smoothed over is the fundamental distinction between performance and learning. Performance is what a student can produce at a specific moment. Learning is what persists. The two are empirically dissociable — decades of research on desirable difficulties have documented that conditions that boost immediate performance often fail to build the storage that supports later performance.
