An efficacy claim that reports only immediate post-intervention performance is reporting on something, but it is not reporting on learning in the sense that matters for whether the intervention produced lasting effects. The sigma number at the short timescale and the sigma number at the long timescale cannot be read on a single axis without either collapsing the distinction between them or pretending the distinction does not matter.

---

Notice, finally, what each intervention cost.

Kestin's intervention required computational resources, research staff, system development time, and a deployment context that assumes universal access to capable hardware and reliable internet. The marginal cost per student is not reported in the primary study; the fixed costs of developing, maintaining, and deploying such a system at scale are substantial. The intervention works in a university with significant technology infrastructure and a research team dedicated to the system's careful configuration.

Eedi's intervention operates within British state schools, at a cost structure defined by the platform's licensing model, the schools' IT infrastructure, and the teacher training and support required to integrate the platform into classroom practice. The per-student cost at scale is meaningfully lower than Kestin's bespoke university deployment, though the exact figure varies by contract and setting.

Rori's intervention reports a marginal cost of approximately five dollars per student per year. It operates on basic mobile phones via WhatsApp, requiring no dedicated hardware, minimal bandwidth, and relatively modest development and maintenance overhead compared to systems that require web interfaces or tablet-based delivery.

These cost structures are radically different. A $5-per-student intervention producing 0.36 sigma and a multi-thousand-dollar-per-student-equivalent intervention producing 0.73 sigma are not alternative points on an effectiveness axis. They are interventions occupying different positions in a cost-effectiveness space. The more expensive intervention is producing more of the thing it measures — but it is also requiring more of the resources it deploys. A 0.73 sigma effect at high cost and a 0.36 sigma effect at low cost are findings that answer different questions. Neither answers the question "which intervention is most effective," because "effectiveness" without a cost denominator is not a comparable quantity. I will develop this point at length in a later essai. For now, the observation is that cost is the fifth variable in the apparatus and it is very nearly always absent from the scalar comparisons the field circulates. When Jonah places three effect sizes on a single axis and asks what the consensus is, the question as asked cannot be answered, because the axis he has proposed has a missing dimension.

---

Five ways in which the three sigmas are not on the same axis. Alignment between intervention and outcome measure. The quality and type of the baseline. The population studied. The timescale of measurement. The cost of producing the effect.

Place these against each other, and a pattern emerges. Kestin's 0.73 was produced under conditions that maximize the magnitude of any effect size: a proximal assessment, a selective and ideal-for-the-intervention population, a short timescale, and a high-resource setting. Eedi's 0.17 was produced under conditions that tend to produce smaller sigmas: a distal assessment, a heterogeneous real-world population, a long timescale, and a school-system deployment context. Rori's 0.36 sits between them in some ways and outside them in others: a curriculum-aligned assessment, a constrained population, a moderate timescale, and an unusually low-cost deployment.

None of these are the same intervention. None of them are measuring the same thing. And yet the three numbers — 0.73, 0.36, 0.17 — circulate as though they were points on a single axis, with Kestin's number at the top and Eedi's at the bottom.

I want to say something carefully at this moment in the essai. I am not arguing that Kestin's 0.73 is "inflated" or that Eedi's 0.17 is "more trustworthy." That is a move the apparatus invites and that this essai refuses. Each effect size is a report on what its study measured under its specific conditions. Each is defensible on its own terms. Kestin's researchers did careful work and reported what their measurement showed. So did Eedi's researchers. So did Rori's. None of them claimed, in their own papers, that their finding was a general benchmark against which other interventions could be judged. The claim that these numbers can be compared is not a claim the researchers made. It is a claim the apparatus of citation makes, when the numbers leave their original contexts and enter the rhetorical economy of the field.

The apparatus does not need any individual to claim that the three numbers are on the same axis. It needs only for the numbers to circulate, bare, without their conditions attached. A sigma number, once detached from its conditions, can be placed next to any other sigma number, because without conditions there is nothing to indicate non-comparability. The sigmas look like each other. The word "sigma" is the same in each. The numerical comparison seems to require no special knowledge to perform. And so, in Slack threads and conference talks and investment committee meetings, the comparison happens. The apparatus works.

---

The pattern I have just described — where researcher-designed measures produce larger effects than standardized measures for the same interventions, where proximal assessments yield stronger sigmas than distal ones, where studies optimizing for conditions that produce citable effects tend to find citable effects — has a name in the methodological literature. Matthew Kraft, in his 2020 paper on interpreting effect sizes in education research, documents that effect sizes in large-scale education RCTs are distributed in ways that reveal the measurement regime under which they were produced. The effect sizes reported in studies using researcher-designed outcome measures tend to cluster substantially higher than the effect sizes reported on standardized measures, even for comparable interventions. The gap is large enough that Kraft explicitly warns against comparing sigmas across these different measurement contexts without accounting for the measurement regime.

I will not cite specific paired figures for this gap. The pattern is real and well-documented; the specific numerical ratio varies by literature, by meta-analysis, by the specific studies being aggregated. What matters is the direction and the magnitude: the effect sizes reported in education research are not scalar measurements of learning. They are conditional reports, and the condition that matters most — the nature of the outcome measure — is almost always invisible when the sigma is cited.

This is the observation that makes Jonah's comparison structurally impossible. He is asking what the consensus is on three numbers that are not on a consensus axis. The question, as asked, has no answer — not because we lack information, but because the answer would require the numbers to be commensurable, and they are not.

---

I want to close this essai by naming what it has been doing — and what it has refused to do.

What it has done is show you five specific incommensurabilities that the practice of placing sigmas on a single axis systematically smooths over. Alignment between intervention and outcome. Quality of baseline. Population. Timescale. Cost. These are five specific moves the apparatus of the five questions from the second essai gives you. When you encounter a comparison of effect sizes across studies — in a Slack thread, in a conference keynote, in a policy brief, in a journalist's summary of the state of the AI-tutoring field — these are the five dimensions on which you can examine the comparison. The question you ask is not *which of these sigmas is right*. The question you ask is *what would it take for these sigmas to be on the same axis, and is that condition met*. In most of the comparisons you will encounter, the answer is no.

What the essai has refused to do is adjudicate between the three studies. It has refused to say which is the "better" study, which sigma is more trustworthy, which intervention is more effective. That refusal is the essai's discipline, and I want to be explicit about what the discipline requires.

The temptation, having identified the incommensurabilities, is to produce a ranked ordering of trustworthiness. Eedi's distal measurement with standardized outcomes over a full year against a heterogeneous population is methodologically harder to move than Kestin's proximal measurement with researcher-designed outcomes over a short window in a selective population. From this observation it would be easy — and, in a certain rhetorical register, satisfying — to conclude that the Eedi finding should be more believed than the Kestin finding.

This conclusion is not what the essai is arguing. It is not what the essai can argue. What each study measured was real. Kestin's study demonstrates what an AI tutor can do with high-preparation Harvard physics students in a carefully constrained interaction over a short window. That is a genuine finding about a specific population under specific conditions. Eedi's study demonstrates what a similar class of technology produces when deployed at scale across British state schools. That is also a genuine finding. Rori's study demonstrates what a very low-cost deployment produces in Ghanaian secondary schools. That is also a genuine finding. All three are real reports on real things. None of them, taken on its own, is subject to the critique this essai makes.

The critique is not of the individual studies. It is of the practice that takes three individual findings, from three radically different studies, and compares them as though they reported on a single underlying quantity. That practice is the subject of the essai. And that practice does not depend on any individual finding being wrong. It depends only on the numbers circulating without their conditions. The apparatus needs no fraud to produce its effects.

What I am asking of you, as you leave this essai, is to hold a discipline the essai has tried to model. Do not, when you next encounter a sigma comparison, ask yourself which sigma is better. Ask yourself what would have to be true for the comparison to be literally meaningful, and notice how often the answer is *more than is currently on the page*. When a comparison cannot be literally meaningful, it is doing rhetorical work rather than analytical work. That rhetorical work may be intentional or inadvertent, well-meaning or cynical, from a journalist trying to summarize a complex field or from a marketer trying to sell a product. The rhetorical character of the comparison does not depend on the motives of the person performing it. It depends only on the structural incommensurabilities the comparison smooths over.

The next essai examines what happens when these individual comparisons are aggregated at scale, in the form of meta-analyses. If a single comparison across three heterogeneous studies is structurally impossible, what is a meta-analysis doing when it aggregates hundreds of such studies into a single summary number? That is the question Essai 6 takes up.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai is the one I was most worried about writing. Its discipline — dismantling the practice of sigma-comparison while refusing to participate in it — is delicate, and the failure modes are specific. I want to name what I am unsure about in how I have written it.

I am not sure the essai resists the counter-sigma temptation cleanly enough. There are moments in Block C and Block D where the analysis of measurement regimes and timescales lands close to an implicit ranking — where a reader could come away with the impression that Eedi's 0.17 "really" reflects more rigorous measurement than Kestin's 0.73. I have tried to close each such moment with the refusal to adjudicate, but the balance is subtle, and a hostile reader may find places where the refusal does not quite land. If the essai has drifted into counter-sigma in specific passages, I want to know which passages.

I am not sure I have given the three studies enough credit as individual works. Each represents serious research, conducted by researchers doing honest work, reported within the conventions of their respective fields. The essai's target is the comparison practice, not the studies. But by using the studies as illustrations of the comparison practice, I may have implicitly undermined their standing as individual contributions. A reader who knows any of the three studies well — who knows Kestin's work on physics instruction, or the Eedi research program, or the development-economics context of Rori — may find the essai's treatment unfair to the specific research. If so, tell me where.

I am not sure the 0.73 / 0.13 pattern I invoke at the end of Block E (without citing paired figures) is described clearly enough for readers who do not already know the methodological literature. The pattern is real, well-documented, and material to the essai's argument. Whether I have made it clear without the crutch of specific paired numbers is something a reader unfamiliar with Kraft 2020 or Cheung & Slavin's work is in a better position to tell me than I am.

Finally, I am not sure the essai's refusal to adjudicate is actually useful, rather than merely disciplined. A reader could reasonably ask: *if I cannot compare sigmas across studies, how am I supposed to make decisions about which interventions to fund, adopt, or recommend?* The essai does not answer this question. Its discipline prevents it from answering. A later essai, *On Cost in the Denominator*, will offer a partial answer. The closing essai will offer another. But within this essai, the refusal may feel like a foreclosure rather than a move forward. If that is how it reads to you, say so. The discipline may be right, and the essai may still need more care in making clear what the discipline enables rather than what it forbids.

The next essai turns to meta-analyses — the practice of aggregating many sigmas into one. If the comparison of three sigmas across three studies is structurally impossible, what is a meta-analysis doing? I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 5. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 22–24 pages. Word count: approximately 5,600.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**The source-verification problem addressed upfront.** I flagged in the pre-draft note that the Deep Research document contained several specific figures and attributions I could not independently verify. The draft handles this by describing the 0.73/0.13 pattern without citing specific paired figures, using general magnitudes for the three studies that are consistent with public reporting, and referring to Kestin's system generically rather than by a potentially fabricated product name. Where the draft invokes specific sub-group numbers or meta-analytic figures, it does so only where the general magnitude is well-established in public discourse. This makes the essai slightly less concrete than it could have been with verified primary sources, but more honest. If you have access to primary sources that confirm specific figures, we can revise and sharpen.

**Block structure.** Five-block structure as specified: Block A (the comparison as a shape, opening on Jonah's quote), Block B (what makes a sigma — teaching block with the five methodological dimensions), Block C (the pattern — the 0.73/0.13 observation without specific paired numbers), Block D (the refusal to participate), Block E (the discipline as portable skill). The teaching block is actually split across what amount to five sub-blocks, each addressing one of the five questions from Essai 2 applied to the comparison across studies. This is a structural move that links Essai 5 directly to Essai 2's apparatus rather than introducing a new framework.

**The refusal-to-adjudicate discipline.** The specification flagged this as the essai's central craft move. The draft handles it in three ways: (1) explicit naming of the discipline at the essai's opening and closing; (2) consistent qualification after each analytical move that the observation is not a verdict on any individual sigma; (3) explicit treatment of the three studies as "real reports on real things" that are not individually vulnerable to the critique. A hostile reader's job is to find places where the discipline slips.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 5,600 words, projecting to 22-24 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — within the 20-23 page range specified, slightly over the 21-page target. If the revision pass wants to tighten, Block B's teaching sections (which walk through all five methodological dimensions) are the natural place to compress.

**The opening move.** The essai opens on Jonah's quote directly, treating his sentence as the ecologically real instance of the comparison practice. This was the specification's preferred opening. It grounds the essai in a real moment from the discourse the book is about, rather than opening abstractly on the practice of sigma-comparison.

**What I am least sure about.** The treatment of the three studies as cases is delicate. The essai needs to use them without indicting them. The passages about Kestin's Harvard context and the ideal-for-the-intervention population could be read as dismissive of Kestin's work. The intent is to note what the context means for what the finding supports, not to suggest Kestin's researchers gamed their design. A revision pass should audit specifically for this: does the treatment of Kestin (and of Eedi and Rori) read as description of what the studies measured, or does it read as critique of the researchers for studying what they studied? The former is what the essai wants. The latter is what the essai must resist.

**The bridge to Essai 6.** The draft closes on the question *what is a meta-analysis doing if comparisons across three studies are structurally impossible?* This is the right bridge. It sets up Essai 6 directly without preempting its material.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On Meta-analyses and What They Conceal

*Essai 6*

---

In 2011, Kurt VanLehn published a paper in *Educational Psychologist* titled *The Relative Effectiveness of Human Tutoring, Intelligent Tutoring Systems, and Other Tutoring Systems*. The paper ran twenty-four pages. It aggregated the findings of fifty-four specific comparisons drawn from twenty-eight evaluation studies, published between 1975 and 2010, of tutoring interventions of various kinds. VanLehn's inclusion criteria were strict: only experimental studies with individual-based assignment, requiring equivalent instructional duration across conditions, with sufficient statistical reporting to compute effect sizes, published in English in peer-reviewed venues. The analysis compared five categories of instruction: no tutoring, answer-based computer-aided instruction, step-based intelligent tutoring systems, substep-based tutoring systems, and human tutoring.

The paper's headline findings have become, in the fourteen years since its publication, among the most-cited numbers in the AI-tutoring advocacy literature. Human tutoring produced a median effect of 0.79 sigma across ten comparisons. Intelligent tutoring systems — the step-based variety that Cognitive Tutor and similar systems exemplify — produced a median effect of 0.76 sigma across twenty-eight comparisons. The two numbers are nearly indistinguishable. Together they have been cited as establishing that intelligent tutoring systems can approach the effectiveness of human one-to-one instruction.

I want to be clear about what this essai is and is not doing. It is not an attack on VanLehn. His 2011 paper is a methodologically careful piece of work. VanLehn himself was transparent about his inclusion criteria, about the limitations of his aggregation, and about the conditions under which his findings apply. He noted specifically, for example, that when one outlier study was removed from the human-tutoring sample, that median dropped from 0.79 to 0.68. He noted that the ITS effect size of 0.76 was produced within systems' "limited area of expertise" — well-defined problem-solving in STEM domains, primarily. He framed his own findings as a "progress report," not a final verdict, and explicitly recommended that ITS be used as "learning supplements" rather than as replacements for classroom instruction. His caveats are in the paper for readers who want them. What has happened to the two headline numbers in the fourteen years since is not VanLehn's responsibility.

The essai's project is to examine what meta-analysis as a genre does — what it aggregates, what it smooths over, what it produces as a summary number, and what relationship that summary number has to the underlying studies it was computed from. VanLehn 2011 is the specimen. The essai reads VanLehn carefully as an instance of a genre, and asks what kind of evidence a meta-analytic summary sigma actually is, and what kind of evidence it is often cited as being.

I want to ask you to hold two numbers in mind as the essai proceeds. VanLehn's human tutoring figure: 0.79. His step-based ITS figure: 0.76. They travel as though they were claims about the world. They are, more precisely, claims about a specific aggregation of fifty-four comparisons drawn from twenty-eight studies selected under specific inclusion criteria. The relationship between those two descriptions is the subject of this essai.

---

Begin by looking at what VanLehn actually aggregated. The twenty-eight studies he included spanned thirty-five years of research, multiple subject domains, a wide range of populations, and substantially different outcome measures. The heterogeneity is not a methodological error on VanLehn's part; it is what the literature he was summarizing actually looked like. But the implications of that heterogeneity for the meaning of the summary number are worth walking through carefully.

Consider the duration of the interventions. At one end of VanLehn's dataset are studies like Valerie Shute and Robert Glaser's 1990 evaluation of the Smithtown system, a discovery-based environment for teaching microeconomic inquiry. The intervention lasted approximately five hours. Students engaged with the system for less than a single school day. The outcome measure was a researcher-designed test of inquiry behaviors and economic concepts, administered shortly after the intervention concluded. At the other end of the dataset are studies like Joanne Anania's 1981 dissertation, conducted under Benjamin Bloom at the University of Chicago, which ran for an entire academic term and measured achievement on final achievement tests in probability and cartography.

Both studies contributed effect sizes to VanLehn's meta-analysis. Both are legitimate research. But they are measuring fundamentally different things. A five-hour intervention on inquiry behaviors cannot produce evidence about long-term retention of academic content. An academic-term intervention on probability cannot produce evidence about short-term skill acquisition during a laboratory session. The two findings are not interchangeable reports on "tutoring effectiveness" — they are reports on different interventions, over different timescales, measuring different constructs, in different populations.

When VanLehn's aggregation computes a median across all the studies in a category, the median treats these as if they were samples from a common distribution. In a narrow statistical sense, they are. In the sense that matters for what the summary number tells us about whether tutoring "works," they are not. The median smooths over the difference between a five-hour discovery learning intervention and a term-long mastery learning curriculum. Both contribute one effect size each to the calculation. The weighted average of heterogeneous effects is what produces the headline number, but the heterogeneity is what makes the headline number hard to interpret in any specific context.

Consider next the outcome measures. At one end of VanLehn's dataset are studies by John Anderson and colleagues on early Cognitive Tutor variants — the LISP Tutor and the Algebra Tutor. These studies used developer-designed assessments that were, in the authors' own methodological papers, tightly aligned with the specific problem-solving steps the tutors scaffolded. Students who worked through the tutor were tested on the kinds of problems the tutor had prepared them to solve. Effect sizes in these studies ran around 1.0 sigma. At the other end are studies like the Mathematica Policy Research evaluation conducted by Larri Campuzano and colleagues in 2009, responding to the No Child Left Behind mandate. This evaluation covered ten commercial software products across thousands of students in thirty-three school districts over one to two school years, with outcomes measured on standardized state tests. Effect sizes across this body of work were, on average, statistically indistinguishable from zero.

The 1.0 and the 0.0 are both real numbers. Both come from careful research. Both are evidence of something. What they are evidence of, however, is different. The Anderson-style effect is evidence of what a well-designed tutor can produce when assessed on items aligned with what it teaches. The Campuzano-style effect is evidence of what a variety of deployed commercial software produces when assessed on broad standardized measures across typical implementation conditions. Neither reading is wrong. They are readings of different things.

When VanLehn's aggregation computes a summary ITS effect of 0.76 sigma, that number averages across this range of outcome measurement. The number is neither the Anderson 1.0 nor the Campuzano 0.0 but something in between — a weighted average of findings produced under radically different assessment regimes. A reader who encounters "0.76 sigma for intelligent tutoring systems" and does not know that the underlying literature includes both 1.0s and 0.0s depending on the nature of the assessment has been given a number whose conditions are invisible. This is not a flaw in VanLehn's analysis; he was transparent about the range. It is a feature of the genre: summary numbers travel independently of the conditions that produced them.

Consider also the populations. VanLehn's inclusion criteria spanned K-12, higher education, and adult learners. Studies of college students solving physics problems in laboratory conditions contribute effect sizes alongside studies of fourth-graders learning probability in classrooms. The populations are different in every way that matters for how interventions produce their effects — cognitive maturity, prior knowledge, motivation, classroom context, the role the intervention plays in the student's broader educational life. Aggregating across these populations is defensible if you believe that "tutoring effectiveness" is a property of the intervention that generalizes across populations. If you do not believe that — if you think interventions produce different effects with different learners, which a substantial research literature on aptitude-treatment interaction has argued for half a century — then the aggregation is smoothing over differences that matter for whether the summary number applies to any given situation.

The point of walking through this is not to claim that VanLehn should have excluded some of these studies. Meta-analysts have to aggregate across the literature that exists. VanLehn's inclusion criteria were defensible. The point is to notice what the summary number is the summary of. The 0.76 for ITS is a weighted average of fifty-four comparisons drawn from research that varied in duration by three orders of magnitude, in outcome measurement from perfectly aligned to maximally distal, and in population from adult learners to elementary schoolchildren. The 0.76 is not a claim about what ITS will do in any specific setting. It is a claim about the central tendency of what ITS produced in the research literature as aggregated by VanLehn's inclusion criteria. Those are different claims, and the difference is the essai's first observation.

---

This brings us to a structural feature of meta-analysis as a genre that is easy to miss: summary numbers are artifacts of inclusion decisions, not neutral measurements of "what the evidence shows."

Consider what happens when other meta-analysts have addressed overlapping literatures using different inclusion criteria. In 2013, Saiying Steenbergen-Hu and Harris Cooper published a meta-analysis of intelligent tutoring systems in K-12 mathematics. Their summary effect size was 0.05 to 0.09 sigma — essentially zero. Their conclusion was that ITS had no meaningful effect on performance at the K-12 level. In 2014, the same authors published a follow-up analysis of ITS effectiveness with college students, finding effects of 0.32 to 0.37 sigma. In 2016, James Kulik and J.D. Fletcher conducted a meta-analysis of fifty controlled evaluations of ITS. They reported an overall summary of 0.66 sigma, with a notable moderator: 0.73 for studies using developer-designed assessments and only 0.13 for studies using standardized tests. In 2020, Andre Nickow, Philip Oreopoulos, and Vincent Quan published what remains the most systematic meta-analysis of randomized tutoring evaluations, reporting a pooled effect of 0.37 sigma — less than half of VanLehn's human tutoring figure.

All of these meta-analyses examined substantially overlapping literatures. All used defensible inclusion criteria. All reported different summary numbers. VanLehn's 0.79 for human tutoring, Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan's 0.37, Kulik-Fletcher's 0.66 for ITS, Steenbergen-Hu and Cooper's 0.05 to 0.09 for K-12 ITS, Kulik-Fletcher's 0.73 versus 0.13 split on outcome measure — these are all answers to roughly the same research question, and they are very different numbers.

The differences are not errors. They are consequences of different inclusion decisions. VanLehn required individual-level assignment, which excluded cluster-randomized trials. Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan included cluster-randomized RCTs and a broader range of tutoring types, including paraprofessional and volunteer tutors. Steenbergen-Hu and Cooper included answer-based CAI systems that Kulik and Fletcher treated as methodologically distinct from step-based ITS, and Kulik and Fletcher explicitly critiqued Steenbergen-Hu for precisely this decision. The intra-genre debate — *one meta-analyst's thoroughness is another's lack of rigor* — is ongoing and unresolvable. Each analyst is making principled choices about what should count. The choices produce different summary numbers.

This is the second observation the essai wants to surface. A meta-analytic summary sigma is not a measurement of the truth of its underlying literature; it is a measurement of what a specific aggregation of that literature produces under specific inclusion rules. Change the rules, change the number. The fact that careful meta-analysts disagree about the right rules is not a problem to be resolved before meta-analysis can be trusted — it is a structural feature of the genre. Summary numbers are interpretive artifacts, not neutral summaries. When a reader encounters "meta-analyses show tutoring produces effects of 0.79 sigma," they are encountering one meta-analyst's interpretive choices, not a fact about the world. A reader who encounters "meta-analyses show tutoring produces effects of 0.37 sigma" is encountering a different meta-analyst's interpretive choices, also not a fact about the world. Both are legitimate summaries. Neither is the summary.

---

Now consider what the underlying literature itself is made of.

A meta-analysis aggregates published studies. Studies with null or negative findings are systematically underrepresented in the published literature, a phenomenon that Robert Rosenthal named the *file drawer problem* in a 1979 paper that remains foundational in meta-analytic methodology. The logic is straightforward: journals preferentially publish studies with statistically significant findings; researchers preferentially submit studies with significant findings; the cumulative effect is that the published literature systematically overrepresents positive effects relative to what was actually conducted. Rosenthal did not stop at naming the problem. He proposed a specific statistic for interrogating it — the Fail-Safe N — which answers a concrete question: how many unpublished studies averaging a null result would have to exist, sitting in researchers' file drawers unseen, to neutralize the statistical significance of the published meta-analytic finding?

The formula is worth putting on the page, because it makes the critique concrete. For a meta-analysis of $k$ studies with a mean standardized score of $\bar{Z}$, the Fail-Safe N is given by:

$$k_0 = \frac{k(\bar{Z}^2 - 1)}{Z_\alpha^2 - \bar{Z}^2}$$

where $Z_\alpha$ is the critical value of the standard normal distribution at the chosen significance level. Rosenthal offered a "rough and ready" threshold for interpreting the result: a meta-analytic finding is reasonably resistant to the file-drawer threat if the Fail-Safe N exceeds $5k + 10$. If your meta-analysis aggregates twenty studies and the Fail-Safe N is three hundred, the published finding is robust — three hundred unseen null studies would have to be sitting in file drawers to overturn it, and this is implausibly many. If your meta-analysis aggregates twenty studies and the Fail-Safe N is fifty, the finding is fragile — fifty unseen null studies is entirely plausible, and the apparent significance of the finding evaporates if they exist.

This statistic is elegant, easy to compute, and rarely reported. I have not been able to locate a Fail-Safe N for VanLehn 2011, or for Kulik and Fletcher 2016, or for Steenbergen-Hu and Cooper, or for most of the other meta-analyses that have shaped contemporary AI-tutor advocacy. The statistic exists. Rosenthal has made it available since 1979. The education-research literature has, by and large, declined to use it. A reader who wants to know how robust a meta-analytic finding is to the file-drawer threat typically has to compute the statistic themselves from what the paper reports — which most readers will not do.

This would matter less if the underlying literature had correction mechanisms for publication bias. Some fields have those mechanisms: registered reports, pre-registration, null-result journals, replication initiatives. Education research has substantially weaker versions of these than, say, clinical medicine. A 2020 survey by Wolf and Frankenbach of recent meta-analyses in flagship education journals found that they "usually neglect publication bias adjustment." When such adjustments are applied, corrected effect sizes tend to be markedly smaller than the uncorrected summary numbers.

There is a second and more specific form of selection bias that applies to educational technology research particularly, and that Wolf and colleagues documented in a 2020 paper. Effect sizes in studies conducted by program developers — by the researchers and organizations who built the intervention being evaluated — are, on average, approximately eighty percent larger than effect sizes reported by independent evaluators of the same class of interventions. The number is worth sitting with. Interventions evaluated by their creators produce effects almost twice as large as the same interventions evaluated by researchers who did not create them. Wolf and colleagues estimated that approximately sixty-six percent of this gap is attributable to publication bias — that is, to selective reporting of positive outcomes by researchers with a vested interest in the intervention's apparent success. The remainder is attributable to other factors: researchers with more experience on a given intervention tend to implement it more faithfully; developer-evaluators may have privileged knowledge of what outcomes the intervention was built to move; and so on.

What this means for meta-analytic summary numbers is specific and important. Any meta-analysis that aggregates studies without distinguishing developer-conducted from independent evaluations is producing a summary that is partially an artifact of who conducted the included studies. The research literature on intelligent tutoring systems, throughout the period VanLehn aggregated, was heavily populated by developer-conducted evaluations. The LISP Tutor was evaluated primarily by Anderson and colleagues, who built it. The Cognitive Tutor line was evaluated primarily by Koedinger and colleagues, who built it. Early AutoTutor research was conducted primarily by Graesser and colleagues, who built it. This is not a scandal — it is how early-stage intervention research often works, because the developers are the people who understand the intervention well enough to evaluate it. But it means that summary effect sizes from this era disproportionately reflect the evaluations of researchers whose careers were invested in the interventions they were studying.

This is a structural feature of the literature meta-analyses aggregate from. Publication bias and evaluator-conflict bias are not errors in the meta-analysis; they are features of the underlying studies that meta-analysis, as a genre, tends to smooth over rather than correct. A summary number produced from a literature that systematically overrepresents positive findings will itself be biased upward. Subsequent independent evaluations — the kind that meta-analyses done a decade later can include — tend to find smaller effects than the original developer-conducted research. The pattern across the ITS literature fits this expectation: effect sizes tend to shrink as the research matures and independent replications accumulate.

---

There is a further problem with meta-analytic aggregation that is worth naming explicitly: the summary number can conceal heterogeneity that matters far more than the central tendency.

Methodologists have developed statistics for this. The I² statistic reports the percentage of variation across included studies that is attributable to heterogeneity rather than to sampling error. Larry Hedges and others have argued that meta-analyses should routinely report not just the summary effect but also a prediction interval — the range of true effects expected in a new study drawn from the same population as the included studies. The distinction matters enormously. A meta-analysis that reports a summary effect of 0.50 sigma with a prediction interval from 0.40 to 0.60 is reporting a consistent effect across the literature. A meta-analysis that reports the same summary effect of 0.50 sigma with a prediction interval from 0.10 to 0.90 is reporting a literature in which effects are extremely variable and the average is nearly uninformative about what any new study will find.

The vast majority of published meta-analyses in education research do not report prediction intervals. When such intervals have been computed post-hoc for prominent meta-analyses, they often turn out to be quite wide — suggesting that the apparent precision of summary effect sizes in this literature is partially illusory. A number like "0.76 for step-based ITS" is typically reported without the wide prediction interval that honest reporting would require. The number looks like a measurement. It functions more like an average across a distribution of measurements that themselves vary substantially.

John Ioannidis has made a related critique at the level of meta-analysis as a methodological practice generally. His argument is that the underlying studies in many research fields are themselves poorly designed, underpowered, and susceptible to researcher degrees of freedom in ways that make them individually unreliable. Aggregating unreliable studies does not produce reliable aggregates. In his 2005 paper *Why Most Published Research Findings Are False*, Ioannidis argued that across a wide range of research domains, the majority of findings are likely to be false positives. Subsequent work has documented that in psychology — a field closely related to learning-sciences research — a substantial fraction of published findings do not replicate when independent replication is attempted. A 2018 analysis by Nelson and colleagues found that in up to one-fifth of psychological meta-analyses, half of the included studies were not expected to replicate.

The implication for meta-analysis of education research is not comforting. If the individual studies being aggregated are unreliable, the meta-analysis produces a summary that is precisely estimated but not accurately estimated. The error bars around the summary look tight. The relationship between the summary and the truth is loose. This is what methodologists mean when they say that meta-analysis can produce precision without accuracy — the summary number has a narrow confidence interval, but the interval is narrow around the wrong value.

---

Now consider what happens when meta-analytic summary numbers leave their original technical contexts and enter contemporary discourse.

In his 2024 book *Brave New Words*, Sal Khan invokes VanLehn's findings to justify the trajectory from human-led to AI-driven education. The book's reading of VanLehn is that intelligent tutoring systems have been demonstrated to approach the effectiveness of human tutoring. The book cites the near-identity of the 0.76 and 0.79 figures as evidence for this claim. The specific invocations rarely mention VanLehn's caveats: that his ITS effect size was produced within systems' "limited area of expertise"; that his recommendation was that ITS be used as learning supplements rather than replacements; that removing a single outlier study dropped the human tutoring figure from 0.79 to 0.68; that subsequent meta-analyses on overlapping literatures have produced substantially different summary numbers.

Khan's invocation is not unusual. Across the contemporary AI-tutor advocacy literature, VanLehn's 0.76 has become a near-canonical reference point, cited as establishing that AI can match human tutoring. Gates Foundation program materials invoke meta-analytic effect sizes to orient "high-dosage tutoring" investment. Chan Zuckerberg Initiative materials invoke VanLehn-shaped findings to frame ITS features as "evidence-based." Academic papers in the AI-in-education tradition routinely cite VanLehn as shorthand for the claim that intelligent systems approach human effectiveness.

What is happening when these citations occur is not fraud or even careless scholarship. The citations are technically accurate in their attribution. VanLehn's paper does report the 0.76 figure. VanLehn's paper does claim that ITS approached human tutoring in his aggregation. What the citations strip is the context in which those numbers mean what they are cited as meaning. The heterogeneity VanLehn aggregated across disappears. The inclusion criteria disappear. The caveats disappear. The alternative meta-analyses disappear. The publication bias disappears. What remains is the number, detached from the analysis that produced it, circulating as though it were a stable fact about the world.

This is the rhetorical life of meta-analytic summary numbers. They are produced by researchers who understand their conditions and acknowledge their limitations. They are cited by advocates who understand the headline findings and not the conditions. The distance between the research context and the citation context is where meta-analytic numbers do most of their field-level work. They are invoked to license investment decisions, policy recommendations, and product claims that their original research contexts do not support. The invocations are not illegitimate — VanLehn's paper is real research that reports real findings — but the use of the findings in contexts where the conditions have been stripped is a specific rhetorical operation, not a neutral consequence of careful research.

---

The limit case of what this genre can produce is John Hattie's *Visible Learning*, first published in 2009. Hattie aggregated over 800 meta-analyses, comprising over 50,000 studies and data from millions of students, into a single ranked list of 138 influences on academic achievement. The list treats interventions ranging from "teacher-student relationships" to "direct instruction" to "class size" to "feedback" as commensurable effects on a single linear scale, each with a summary effect size, ranked from largest to smallest. Hattie proposed that 0.40 sigma should be treated as the average effect size of any educational innovation — the "hinge point" — and argued that educators should focus attention on interventions above this threshold.

*Visible Learning* has been enormously influential. It has been called "the Holy Grail of teaching" by educators who have structured professional development around its rankings. It has also been the subject of methodological critiques that, taken seriously, substantially undermine its central claims. Ivan Snook and colleagues have documented calculation errors in Hattie's work, including instances where Hattie's equations produced negative probabilities or probabilities exceeding one hundred percent. Steven Higgins has raised questions about the aggregation procedures. Pierre-Jérôme Bergeron has argued that the core methodology misapplies statistical procedures in ways that invalidate the headline rankings.

One example illustrates what Hattie's aggregation can produce. In his treatment of "feedback" — one of the highest-ranked interventions in *Visible Learning*, with a reported effect size of approximately 0.73 — Hattie drew on a diverse set of meta-analyses to compute the summary. One of the included studies evaluated the effect of background music on the agitation levels of elderly residents in nursing homes. Another evaluated feedback in classroom instruction. Another evaluated feedback in corporate training. Hattie's aggregation treated all of these as comparable evidence of what "feedback" produces as an educational intervention. A summary number computed across this range is not a claim about what feedback does in classrooms; it is a claim about the mathematical average of a set of heterogeneous studies labeled with the same word.

Hattie represents the limit case not because he is doing something fundamentally different from other meta-analysts, but because he has extended the logic of meta-analytic aggregation to its endpoint. If a summary number across fifty-four tutoring comparisons is a meaningful quantity, why not a summary across eight hundred meta-analyses of different interventions? If we can aggregate across different durations, populations, and outcome measures within a category, why not aggregate across categories? Hattie's work asks these questions structurally, and the answer his critics have given is that the aggregation produces numbers that do not correspond to anything in the world. Feedback in a classroom and feedback in a nursing home are not the same phenomenon. Their numerical average is not a fact about feedback; it is a fact about what happens when two unlike things are combined in a spreadsheet.

The critique that applies to Hattie applies, in more modest form, to careful meta-analyses like VanLehn's. Every meta-analysis involves aggregating studies that differ from one another in ways that matter. The question is always how much heterogeneity the summary number can honestly absorb before it becomes meaningless. VanLehn's aggregation is far more careful than Hattie's. It is still an aggregation. The summary numbers it produces smooth over heterogeneity, even when the heterogeneity is smaller and the aggregation is more principled. A reader who wants to know what the underlying literature actually shows has to look at the underlying studies. The summary number is an invitation, not a substitute.

---

Here is what I would like you to carry out of this essai.

When you next encounter a meta-analytic summary number cited as evidence for some claim about educational effectiveness — *meta-analyses show that tutoring produces gains of X sigma*, or *the research literature establishes that AI tutoring approaches human effectiveness*, or any variant of these — the questions to hold are not about whether the number is "correct." The number is probably computed correctly within its own framework. The questions are about what the number is the number of.

What was the inclusion criteria, and what would the number look like with different inclusion rules? What did the underlying studies measure, and over what durations, with what populations? Were the studies conducted by developers of the interventions they evaluated, or by independent researchers? How wide is the prediction interval around the summary, and does the interval include zero? What does a meta-analysis of the same literature with different inclusion rules produce? Have subsequent replications of the underlying studies succeeded?

You do not need answers to all of these questions to form a judgment. You need to notice that the questions exist. A meta-analytic summary sigma cited without its conditions is an invitation to examine the conditions. A citation that strips the conditions is doing rhetorical work rather than analytical work — inviting you to treat a specific aggregation as a general fact.

The apparatus from the second essai of this volume applies here with no modification. The five questions — outcome measure, baseline, population, timescale, cost — apply to meta-analyses just as they apply to individual studies. When applied to a meta-analysis, they become questions about the aggregation: what outcome measures does the aggregation include, what baselines, what populations, what timescales, under what cost conditions. A meta-analysis that aggregates across radically different answers to these questions is not producing a cleaner picture than the individual studies; it is producing a blurrier picture, with the blur concealed behind the apparent precision of a summary number.

This does not mean meta-analyses are worthless. A well-conducted meta-analysis that reports its heterogeneity openly, acknowledges its inclusion decisions as choices, and frames its summary as one interpretation rather than as a fact, can be genuinely useful. It synthesizes a literature. It reveals patterns. It surfaces the range of what studies have found. The work it does, however, is not to produce a single number that licenses claims about the world. It is to map the terrain of what has been studied, so that readers can navigate that terrain with specificity rather than trusting a summary to navigate it for them.

The next essai examines the intelligent-tutoring-systems literature that VanLehn aggregated — not through a summary number, but by reading what ITS actually measured and what ITS actually claimed. The essai argues that ITS measured exactly what it was built to measure, and that the "disappointment" narrative around ITS rests on comparing its measurements to a construct — *human tutoring* — that involves interactional moves the ITS apparatus was never designed to capture. Where VanLehn's meta-analysis placed ITS and human tutoring on a single sigma axis, the next essai asks whether the two are on the same axis at all.

---

### What I am not sure about, and what I am asking from you

This essai has tried to use VanLehn 2011 as a specimen of a genre without attacking VanLehn's work specifically. That balance is difficult, and I want to name where I am unsure I have held it.

I am not sure I have given VanLehn's methodological seriousness enough credit. His inclusion criteria were rigorous. His caveats were explicit. His framing of his own findings as a "progress report" was honest about what the analysis could and could not support. The critique this essai makes applies to the genre of meta-analysis rather than to VanLehn's specific execution, but the essai's structure — using his paper as the anchor for that critique — may read, in places, as though his work is the target. If it does, the drift is not what I intended. A reader who finds the treatment of VanLehn unfair is a reader I want to hear from.

I am not sure the alternative meta-analyses in Block B are represented with enough nuance. Steenbergen-Hu and Cooper, Kulik and Fletcher, Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan each did careful work with defensible inclusion criteria. The essai uses their different summary numbers to make the point that summaries depend on inclusion decisions, but this can read as though the analysts were disagreeing arbitrarily. They were not. Their choices were principled. The disagreement reflects genuine methodological differences about what should count as evidence for what. A more careful reading of any one of these meta-analyses would need more space than the essai gives them.

I am not sure the publication-bias section is proportionate. The Wolf et al. finding that developer-conducted evaluations produce effects approximately eighty percent larger than independent evaluations is striking, and the essai uses it to substantial analytical weight. Whether that single finding can bear the weight the essai places on it is uncertain. The pattern is real; the magnitude may be specific to particular research contexts and may not generalize as widely as the essai implies. A reader more expert in publication-bias methodology than I am may find my treatment simplified. I am counting on correction.

Finally, I am not sure the Hattie section lands where it should. I treat Hattie as the limit case of what meta-analytic aggregation can produce, and I use his critics' specific observations to argue that the logic of aggregation can be pushed past the point where the summary numbers correspond to anything in the world. But Hattie's influence on educational practice is substantial, and there are educators who have found *Visible Learning* useful in ways that my treatment does not engage. If the essai's reading of Hattie is too dismissive, or if it leaves important aspects of his work unaddressed, that is a failure of proportion rather than of argument. A Hattie-reader who wants to correct this reading should do so.

The next essai examines what ITS actually measured and what the construct of "human tutoring" involves that ITS never attempted to capture. I hope you will continue with me.

---

*[End of Essai 6. Projected page count at trade-scholarly typesetting: 21–23 pages. Word count: approximately 5,300.]*

---

## Notes on the draft

A few observations for the revision pass:

**The discipline: using VanLehn as specimen, not as target.** The spec was specific about this, and the draft has tried to hold it. Every analytical move surfaces what the genre of meta-analysis does, using VanLehn as a specific example. The essai's opening explicitly says VanLehn is not the target. The closing uncertainty section names this discipline as the hardest balance to hold.

**Block structure.** Five-block structure following the spec: Block A (the meta-analytic finding as specimen, opening on VanLehn's 0.76 and 0.79 and his transparent caveats), Block B (the heterogeneity within VanLehn's included studies — Shute-Glaser, Anania, Anderson, Campuzano as the specific examples), Block C (alternative meta-analyses showing different inclusion rules produce different summary numbers), Block D (publication bias, heterogeneity-suppression, and the Wolf et al. developer-evaluator gap), Block E (the rhetorical life of summary numbers in contemporary AI-tutor advocacy) plus a closing section on Hattie as limit case and the apparatus the reader now carries. The essai is slightly denser than five blocks would suggest because each block does substantial work.

**Length.** Draft runs approximately 5,300 words, projecting to 21-23 pages at trade-scholarly typesetting — at the upper end of the 20-23 range specified. The essai is dense with specific material; if the revision pass wants to tighten, Block B's walkthrough of specific studies or Block D's publication-bias treatment are the natural places.

**Direct quotes and specific numbers.** The Deep Research document gave me substantial specific material to work with: VanLehn's specific 0.79 and 0.76 figures with his caveats, the 0.68 drop when the Anania outlier is removed, the specific studies (Shute-Glaser 1990, Anania 1981, Anderson's Cognitive Tutor work, Campuzano 2009) that illustrate heterogeneity, the alternative meta-analyses (Steenbergen-Hu-Cooper 2013/2014 at 0.05-0.09 and 0.32-0.37, Kulik-Fletcher 2016 at 0.66 with the 0.73/0.13 split, Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan 2020 at 0.37), the Wolf et al. developer-evaluator 80% inflation finding, the Ioannidis 2005 framing, the Nelson et al. 2018 finding on psychological meta-analyses, Hattie's 0.40 hinge point and his 2009 Visible Learning. All of these numbers come from the Deep Research document. I treat them as reasonably well-sourced since the document attributes them to specific papers, but any revision pass should verify the 80% figure from Wolf et al. specifically, as it's the most distinctive finding in the essai's publication-bias section.

**The Hattie "music in nursing homes" example.** This is a specific anecdote the Deep Research document referenced. The specific example may be slightly apocryphal — the claim about Hattie aggregating a study on background music soothing elderly nursing-home residents into his feedback effect size is widely repeated in Hattie-critical discussions, and I have used it because it is specific and illustrative. If verification of this specific example is problematic, the Hattie section can be rewritten to use a more general characterization of the aggregation problem without the specific music example.

**The publication-bias specificity.** The Wolf et al. 2020 finding is the specific piece of documentation that most supports the essai's claim about developer-evaluator bias. If this specific paper and finding cannot be verified, the essai still has Rosenthal 1979 and the general publication-bias literature to ground the argument, but loses some of its specific analytical weight. A revision pass should verify this specific source.

**What the draft does particularly well.** Opens on VanLehn's numbers with explicit acknowledgment of his own caveats before beginning examination. Treats VanLehn, Steenbergen-Hu, Kulik-Fletcher, and Nickow-Oreopoulos-Quan as careful researchers disagreeing principally, not as analysts in conflict. Uses the specific 0.73/0.13 split from Kulik-Fletcher to ground the outcome-measure point from Essai 5 concretely. Connects the essai to the five-question apparatus from Essai 2 explicitly in the closing — meta-analyses can be read using the same apparatus as individual studies, just pointed at the aggregation rather than at a single study. Bridges to Essai 7 through the specific observation that VanLehn aggregated ITS and human tutoring onto a single sigma axis that Essai 7 will ask whether they were ever on at all.

**What the draft may have gotten wrong.**
- The alternative-meta-analyses section (Block B closing + Block C opening) is dense with numbers and may lose readers who do not hold effect-size literacy easily. A revision pass could add one or two concrete anchors ("a 0.09 effect size means roughly...") to keep the non-expert reader oriented.
- The treatment of Hattie is harder-edged than the treatment of VanLehn. This is deliberate — Hattie is the limit case — but the tonal shift is noticeable. A revision pass should check whether the shift feels earned or arbitrary.
- The closing "what you carry out of this essai" section invokes the five-question apparatus from Essai 2 applied to meta-analyses. This is structurally right but could be extended with a specific worked example of applying the five questions to a meta-analytic summary. I left the extension out for length; it may be worth adding back.

Ready for your revision pass.
# On Intelligent Tutoring Systems

*Essai 7*

---

In 2014, John Pane, Beth Ann Griffin, Daniel McCaffrey, and Rita Karam published a RAND Corporation report titled *Effectiveness of Cognitive Tutor Algebra I at Scale*. The study they conducted was, by the standards of educational-technology evaluation, ambitious in every dimension. It was a cluster-randomized controlled trial across 147 school sites in seven states, encompassing roughly 18,700 high school students and 6,800 middle school students, pairing schools into matched groups and randomly assigning each pair to use Cognitive Tutor Algebra I or to continue with their existing textbook-based instruction. The outcome measure was a standardized algebra proficiency exam administered at the end of the academic year. The comparison was not ITS against no instruction; it was ITS against the actual algebra instruction American ninth and eighth graders typically received in schools. The scale, the rigor, the geographic range, and the real-world setting made this, to this day, one of the most credible evaluations of any intelligent tutoring system that has ever been conducted.

The findings were specific, and I want to state them carefully because the rest of this essai depends on reading them correctly. In the first year of implementation, Cognitive Tutor produced no statistically significant effect on algebra proficiency, in either the high school sample or the middle school sample. In the second year, a statistically significant positive effect emerged in the high school sample — approximately 0.20 standard deviations, sufficient to move a median student from the 50th to roughly the 58th percentile on the end-of-year assessment. The middle school sample showed an effect of similar magnitude in the second year (around 0.13 to 0.15 sigma), but the effect did not reach statistical significance. Pane and colleagues described this as an "implementation learning curve" and were explicit in their framing: "Decisionmakers should be prepared for the possibility that CTAI will not produce positive effects immediately and should allow time for the full effects to emerge."

This is a nuanced result. It is not a clean success and it is not a clean failure. It is the kind of result that resists the binary narratives educational technology typically generates. Interestingly, the study also documented that the second-year improvement was not primarily attributable to individual teacher learning — students of teachers who were new to Cognitive Tutor in the second year performed similarly to students of teachers who had used it for both years. The learning, Pane and colleagues concluded, seemed to happen at the level of the school site — scheduling, technology infrastructure, teacher-administrator coordination, student expectations — rather than at the level of individual practitioner skill. The schools that figured out how to implement Cognitive Tutor took a year to figure it out, after which the system produced the kinds of effects its developers had hoped for.

I want to sit with this finding before the essai goes further. Pane's 2014 study is what careful, independent, well-resourced evaluation of an intelligent tutoring system looks like. It is what the field's best evidence is. Cognitive Tutor, in this study, produced a genuine but modest second-year effect at high schools, no significant effect at middle schools, and required substantial institutional learning to produce any effect at all. The cost of implementation — RAND's documentation estimated approximately $97 per student per year for Cognitive Tutor versus approximately $28 per student for traditional textbook instruction — fell on school budgets. These numbers are what the best evidence for ITS, read honestly, looks like.

The narrative the field has constructed around results like these — across Cognitive Tutor's thirty-year history, across the AutoTutor and ALEKS and SimStudent and related research programs, across the sustained body of ITS research from the 1980s forward — is of *disappointment*. Intelligent tutoring systems were supposed to approach the effectiveness of human tutoring. They did not. The gap between the promise of early ITS work and the subsequent evaluation record has been narrated, across both proponents and critics, as evidence that ITS has somehow failed to deliver.

This essai examines that narrative. I want to argue that the disappointment framing rests on a specific category error — a comparison between what ITS measured and a construct, *human tutoring*, that ITS was never fully on the same axis with. Read through the apparatus the second essai of this volume built, ITS measured what it was built to measure, with striking methodological discipline. What it did not measure, and what Pane's 0.20 sigma does not index, is what human tutors do that is not step-level procedural guidance. The disappointment narrative confuses these two things: the measurement of procedural mastery that ITS delivered, and the measurement of something else — something that lives in a different set of interactional moves — that the comparison benchmark required.

This is not a defense of ITS against critics. Some ITS critiques are correct. This is an argument about what kind of comparison the field has been making, and what that comparison has been invited to obscure.

---

Before going further, I want to be specific about what this essai is and is not doing.

The ITS research tradition — John Anderson, Kenneth Koedinger, Albert Corbett, Art Graesser, Kurt VanLehn, and a generation of their students and collaborators — produced some of the most careful, theoretically grounded, methodologically serious work in the history of educational technology. These are not EdTech entrepreneurs pitching inflated claims to investors. They are researchers who built cognitive theories before they built software, tested their software against their theories, published their methodologies openly, and acknowledged their systems' limitations in the primary literature when they existed. When Anderson, Corbett, Koedinger, and Pelletier published *Cognitive Tutors: Lessons Learned* in 1995, they were writing, in the plural of "lessons learned," the specific things their decade of work had taught them, including the limitations. Their 1995 paper contains, in its own words, the acknowledgment that tutors work better when they present themselves to students as nonhuman tools rather than as emulations of human tutors. The researchers knew what their systems were. The disappointment narrative came later, from the field's uses of their work, not from the researchers themselves.

This essai's critique is therefore not a critique of ITS researchers. It is a critique of the measurement apparatus their systems inherited and operated under, and of the rhetorical move that compared their systems to a construct (human tutoring) whose full character was never on the same measurement axis. The researchers deserve credit for building what they built. The apparatus around their work, which placed their systems in comparison with human tutoring via summary sigma numbers, is what this essai examines.

---

Begin by examining what Cognitive Tutor was designed to do.

Cognitive Tutor is an embodiment of the ACT-R theory of cognition developed by John Anderson at Carnegie Mellon University. ACT-R posits that cognitive skill is acquired through the conversion of declarative knowledge (facts, concepts) into procedural knowledge (production rules, condition-action pairs). A production rule is specifically a statement of the form: *if the goal is X and the current state is Y, then take action Z*. Under ACT-R, becoming skilled at algebra means acquiring an increasingly sophisticated set of production rules for algebraic manipulation — recognizing that when the goal is to isolate a variable and the current state shows an equation with a coefficient, the next action is to divide both sides by that coefficient.

The instructional design principle this entails is specific: *instruction should be designed with reference to a cognitive model of the competence the student is being asked to learn*. If you can specify the production rules that constitute the target competence, you can instrument instruction to track whether each rule has been acquired. Cognitive Tutor does exactly this. Its engineering rests on a computational model of the problem-solving process the student is expected to develop. When a student works through an algebra problem, the tutor compares each step the student takes against its model of valid solution paths and responds accordingly.

The technology that results is built around two specific operations, both of which are worth naming precisely.

The first is *model tracing*. As the student works, the tutor monitors each individual step — each equation manipulation, each choice of operation — and matches that step against its internal representation of the production rules that constitute correct problem-solving. If the student takes a step that matches a valid production, the tutor proceeds. If the student takes a step that does not — or that matches one of the tutor's stored "buggy" productions representing common misconceptions — the tutor responds with immediate feedback, typically a short error message identifying what went wrong. If the student requests help, the tutor offers a sequence of hints calibrated to the specific production the student is currently struggling to fire. This is step-level instrumentation. The tutor knows, in real time, what the student did at every point in a problem, and responds to what the student did specifically.

The second is *knowledge tracing*. Across many problems, the tutor maintains a running estimate of whether the student has acquired each of the production rules underlying the domain. These estimates use a Bayesian framework, updating as the student succeeds or fails with items that exercise each rule. The tutor will not allow a student to advance to new material until its knowledge-tracing estimates cross a threshold indicating mastery of the current material. This is the *mastery learning* principle as applied inside a computational system: students do not move forward until the system's measurement apparatus says they are ready to.

Together, model tracing and knowledge tracing constitute the measurement apparatus of Cognitive Tutor. What the apparatus measures is specific: step-level correctness, time per step, requests for hints and the specific hint levels utilized, error patterns and their correspondence to stored misconceptions, and estimated mastery of each production rule in the domain. These are not arbitrary choices. They are the measurements ACT-R theory specifies as relevant to procedural skill acquisition. The design is internally consistent with the theory the system was built on.

What the apparatus does not measure is also specific, and the 1995 paper — along with later ITS literature — is forthright about the boundaries. Cognitive Tutor does not measure the student's affective state. It does not model whether the student is frustrated, bored, motivated, or emotionally disengaged. It does not measure conceptual confusion at levels above the production-rule grain (a student may execute productions correctly while failing to understand the domain at a deeper conceptual level, and the tutor cannot detect this). It does not measure transfer to unlike problems, to non-mathematical contexts, or to new domains. It does not measure the durability of learning beyond the unit or the semester. It does not measure the student's motivation for pursuing algebra, or their relationship to mathematics as a field, or the meaning they derive from mastering it. None of these dimensions are in the apparatus. The tutor was not built to measure them. The researchers who built it did not claim it measured them.

When Pane's RAND study evaluated Cognitive Tutor, the outcome measure — the standardized algebra proficiency exam — was aligned with the construct Cognitive Tutor was designed to build. The test measured procedural algebra competence, with items that could reasonably be decomposed into production rules the tutor had been designed to strengthen. The 0.20 sigma second-year effect reflects what Cognitive Tutor actually does: when implemented at high schools, over two years, with teachers who have figured out how to integrate it into their instruction, the system produces measurable gains on procedural algebra assessment. This is a real effect. It is what the tutor was built to produce.

---

Now consider what the ITS research tradition is typically compared against.

The comparison — explicit in VanLehn 2011 and throughout the meta-analytic literature, implicit in nearly every discussion of whether AI tutors have "achieved human-tutor effectiveness" — is with human one-to-one tutoring. The most frequently cited benchmark is Bloom's 2-sigma finding from 1984, which Essai 4 of this volume examined at length. But the comparison point is not only Bloom. Subsequent meta-analyses have found that expert human tutors, measured across many studies, produce effects in the range of roughly 0.4 to 0.8 sigma relative to classroom instruction. This is the number ITS is typically compared against when the field asks whether ITS has "matched" or "approached" human tutoring.

What, precisely, are these human-tutoring effect sizes measuring?

This is where the essai's argument lives. The human-tutoring construct, as characterized in the research literature on what expert human tutors actually do, involves a set of interactional moves that the ITS measurement apparatus does not index and was not built to index. These moves have been documented across several decades of careful research — much of it, interestingly, by researchers who also built ITS and who studied human tutoring in order to inform their systems' design. The researchers are not unaware of what they were not measuring. They were explicit about it. The field that inherited their work often was not.

Art Graesser, whose research program included the AutoTutor ITS and who therefore has particular credibility on both sides of this comparison, spent years analyzing videotaped tutoring sessions between expert human tutors and students. The goal was to characterize what expert tutors did, so that AutoTutor could be designed to do some of it. What Graesser's analyses documented was a specific set of interactional moves tutors routinely performed:

*Expectation and Misconception Tailored dialogue*, in which the tutor approaches a topic with a specific set of "expectations" — the components of a complete correct understanding — and a specific set of "misconceptions" — the common wrong ways students tend to think about the topic. The tutor probes the student's articulation, notes what components of the expectation are present and absent, identifies which misconceptions the student is exhibiting, and dynamically constructs the next move to address what the student specifically needs. This is substantially more flexible than matching a step against a stored production rule. It works with free-form student utterances, evaluates them against conceptual expectations rather than syntactic correctness, and adjusts the tutor's response based on what the utterance revealed.

*Comprehension checks*, in which the tutor continuously probes whether the student has understood what was just established. "Do you see why that works?" "Can you put that in your own words?" "What would happen if we changed this part?" These are not assessment items in the ITS sense; they are real questions that real tutors ask, whose answers real tutors then use to calibrate what to do next. The tutor is monitoring the student's understanding in real time, not as an external measurement but as part of an interaction whose purpose is the joint construction of understanding.

*Politeness and face management*, in which the tutor manages the student's emotional state and relationship to the material. Graesser documented that human tutors are often strategically imprecise about negative feedback, delivering it indirectly or softening it to protect the student's willingness to continue engaging. This is not a pedagogical flaw; it is a feature of interactions designed to sustain rather than damage the student's relationship to learning.

*Handling student-initiated questions*, in which the student asks something the tutor had not planned to address. Human tutors engage with these questions, sometimes following the student's interest to places the tutor had not intended to go. AutoTutor, Graesser noted with characteristic directness, has to use "diversionary tactics" to stay on its agenda when students ask questions it was not designed to handle.

Michelene Chi, whose ICAP framework distinguishes Interactive, Constructive, Active, and Passive forms of engagement, has made a related argument from a different angle. Chi documents that what makes human tutoring effective is not primarily the information the tutor delivers; it is the *interactivity* of the exchange, in which the tutor's questions and prompts elicit the student's own elaboration and self-explanation, and the student's attempts at articulation reveal gaps and misconceptions that the tutor then addresses through further interaction. Chi's research program has repeatedly shown that student self-explanation is a primary driver of conceptual change — and that expert tutors are specifically skilled at eliciting the right kinds of self-explanation from students through well-calibrated prompts and questions. An ITS can prompt for self-explanation — some systems do — but the prompt-to-prompt flexibility of a human tutor reading a student's partial answer and responding to that specific answer's specific weaknesses is not what production-rule-based systems do.

Wood, Bruner, and Ross, in a foundational 1976 paper, identified six functions that a tutor performs when scaffolding a learner through a task: recruitment of the learner's interest, reduction of degrees of freedom (simplifying the task), direction maintenance (keeping the learner focused on the goal), marking critical features (drawing attention to what matters), frustration control (managing the emotional risk of error), and demonstration (modeling the task for the learner). Of these six functions, Cognitive Tutor is specifically designed to perform the second — reduction of degrees of freedom — through its step-level scaffolding and hint system. The tutor makes the task more tractable by breaking it into smaller operations and providing graduated hints as needed. What the tutor is structurally blind to is recruitment (it cannot engage the student's interest), frustration control (it cannot read or respond to the emotional register of the interaction), and — in any rich sense — demonstration (it cannot stylize the solution in ways that make the expert move visible to the novice).

---

Here is the structural observation the essai wants to crystallize.
